The Red Pape
PAGAN ODE.
Jn the dim catacombs of long ago
The dead gods lie, who lived and ruled an
age,
And who, lihe puppets in a shadow-show.
Loitered fantastically across the stage
Into the wings of death, and so were gone.
And death is equally the heritage
Of all the deities who rule to-day
Save one, save only one:
.4 goddess whose unsacram,ended sway
Gives all the centuries her benison.
Her name is Beauty: Sole authentic queen
Of all the universe! The one bright star
Of constancy that shines remote, serene,
Above the plains of peace and peaks of war!
A wonder-light to lead the wildered soul
Through mazy life to what great goal afar!
A. deity to whom no temples rise,
No dolorous musics roll,
Nor any sacrificial sorrow cries,
And whom no cloistered servitors cajole.
'To whom no temples rise: and yet her shrine
Is everywhere. The rainbow-raftered sky
Is her wide dome; and all the stars combine
To jewel it with changing tracery.
And she has many an altar: morning-light;
' The faery plaidies of a butterfly ;
Rubies and roses; icebergs in the sun;
And lightnings in the night;
Laughter; and laughing clean cascades that
run
From granite mountains; and a clean-
fought fight.
A gaudy sunset on g level sea;
Lagoons among the hills; and silver sand;
Gold of the wattle; and the greenery
Of gum-trees after rain; green palm-trees
fanned
By lazy trades; ivhite schooners; and the
slow
And stately dancers of a saraband;
A’ pampered racehorse; aeroplanes that rise
Like rocs of long ago;
A schoolboy, trotting homeward with a prize;
And sun-brown surfers on a beach of snow.
And many another: tyrian-tinted fans;
Old moonlit mountains; sulphur caves and
caves
Beamy with glow-worms; hurrying caravans
Of ants; a royal wine; mosaic paves;
The culm contentment in the doting eyes
Of new young mothers: galloping hordes
of waves;
Comets, that run in starry steeplechase
Across the rolling skies; —-
These are her sanctuaries, most common-
place
And yet more wonderful than all surmise.
And Earth is her bright censer, slowly swung
In splendor-teeming halls of night and day.
And deities and angels move among
The worshippers who walk her flowery
way.
And all the street and mighty musics are
An antheming of her benignant sway.
The planets bow to her —an all-supreme,
A sun-eclipsing star!
A wonder-light beyond the seas of dream
Shining on what mysterious land afar.
M.L.
BARTLETT ADAMSON.
Australian
This, also, is the real oil — Crusading at
Ansae, a.d. 1915, a 'series of pen-and-ink
sketches by Signaller Ellis Silas, A.LI.
(The British-Australasian, London;
2s. 6d.) What the pictures lack in accurate
drawipg, and that is very little, they make
up in graphic realism. Considering the cir-
cumstances in which they were probably
made, it is scarcely fair to criticise the art
at all. The author’s brief comments are as
interesting as his drawings. He was in the
first landing, and notes that soon he was
the only signaller left out of A and B Com-
panies of the 16th Battalion. Anyhow, sig-
nallers were expected only to last three
days.” He remarks that it is not with the
desire for morbid sensationalism that he in-
troduces the dead in every drawing. They
were part of our daily life; they were part of
the character of the Peninsula —at least ot
Anzac.” “One of the most trying experi-
ences was when our ships mistook the range,
and began to plump shells into us; when the
Navy gets its mark it’s a hit every time.
They put 11 shells into us before we could
signal to them to alter range.” In a gully
where “it rained lead” and all round could
be seen the sparks where the bullets were
striking, General Godley calmly did up the
signaller’s puttee for him. Silas implored
him to take cover, but he wouldn t hear of
it. “Then, with an amused chuckle, he
passed his hand across the top of his cap,
remarking: ‘That was a pretty near
A bullet has singed the top of his cap.
Once, in a gully choked with dead, he saw a
Maorilander come tearing down, smothered
with blood, and quite delirious, kissing every
man he passed, upon whom he left a splash
of blood! Refering to another of Ins draw-
ings he remarks that the man on Ins right
“caught it” badly, but whether he died Silas
does not know. “There was little time to
think of these matters. He was out or
action; another man must take his place.
When the 16tli Battalion was ordered, by
mistake, into the firing-line, instead of
merely acting as reinforcements to the Light
Horse in their charge at Quinn s Post, bilas
had to go six times across a lead-swept
plateau in order to find the battalion and
deliver the order to retire. When he found
Lieut. Harwood and gave his message, Har-
wood yelled above the din: Silas, this is
fine. I wouldn’t be elsewhere for a thou-
sand pounds!” His drawing of the Rc.
Call at Quinn’s Post after this error is
heart-rending. The artist says: Name after
name would be called; the reply—-a deep
silence, which could be felt, despite the noise
of the incessant crackling of rifles and
screaming of shrapnel.” It was just a
ilino of weary, ashen-faced men.” with
es lying about which they were unable to
f for some days. One of the last pie-
's of this wonderful record shows the
st at the Palace Hospital, Heliopolis,
i a nurse bending over him. It Ims just
title, “Heaven !” The book will bring
.mint memories to many an Anzac.

Let us meditate upon the etiquette of
meteries. Here is an indispensable hand-
ok of the convenances of tombstone society,
yd’s Acre: notes on the care and manage-
ent of churchyards, cemeteries and burial
ounds in the Diocese of Goulburn (The
iocesan Registry, Goulburn, 7d.). Lewis
oulburn lays down the rules of cemetery
shavior in an admirable foreword. “ ‘God s
ere’ should he the best-kept plot of ground
the whole neighborhood. Here is work for
illing hearts and hands, work for free after-
>6ns and summer evenings. Especially
ould I plead for the bare graves of. the
iknown or friendless dead.” But the pious
spiration of Lewis Goulburn evidently
les not work out in practice. “It frequently
ippens that one section of a cemetery is re-
irded with greater favor than another, on
mount of its position or of special associa-
ons or of the kind of monument erected
lerein.” Evidently cemeteries have the
ime characteristics as the desirable allot-
lents of the estate agents. “It is of course
ndesirable that any idea of social distine-
ions should be observed or encouraged m
tie allotting of graves; but it is quite open
o local managers to charge more for exclu-
ive rights in one section than in another.
Mr example, where it is known that the
elatives or friends of the deceased will
ventually kerb, in the grave and erect a
lonument, they may be allotted a. grave in
he section where a higher rate is charged.
)n the other hand, the grave of an inmate
f a gaol or asylum or hospital, with regard
o whom nothing is known locally, or no wish
las been expressed by friends at a distance,
ieed not be placed in a prominent position on
;he main drive, as it is unlikely that such a
jrave would be kept in decent order in future
mars. In other words, in laying out a
:emetery it is advisable to open at least
;wo sections” —one for the sheep and another
:or the goats, one for the society push and
mother for the paupers. So the inhabitants
>f the Goulburn Diocese know what to
ixpect. If they can’t afford a monument
they will be buried in the slums; but the
susceptibilities of the society corpses will
be jealously protected, and these will have
the satisfaction of knowing that their coffins
are not contaminated by contact with the
corpses that in life did not get their names
in the society columns of the press. In the
Goulburn Diocese, and apparently in other
dioceses, the snobbery of life is perpetuated
in death.

Once again this page offers a warm wel-
come to that most Australian of magazines,
The Inlander, published by the Home
Mission Board of the Presbyterian Church
of Australia, and edited by Rev. John
Flynn, a quarterly that gives amazing value
for Is. (Gordon and Gotch). The July
issue of the third volume contains a splendid
illustrated article on “The Land where the
Cattle Camps Are,” full of strange infor-
mation about that inner continent of Aus-
tralia of which the dwellers on the rim
know so little. The writer says that he
douhts if safety from, draughts can be
guaranteed, but “interior railways giving
direc-t communication between south and
north would save millions of cattle and
sheep in ordinary drought times. Roughly
speaking, rainfall south of a line from
Jervis Bay to Onslow has its climax in
Jme, but north of the line the climax is
in January. Thus it can easily be under-
stood that droughts are mostly local,_ t.e.,
it is uncommon to have disastrous visita-
tions in north and south simultaneously.
At present, however, the only _ means or
transit where railways do exist is east to
the State Capital City, then north or south
to the next Capital City, then west to the
good pastures. Which roundabout way is
I rifely out of the question. ‘ But we have
nost ceased to have any faith in the pro-
sed north-south railway.” In another
ride a woman makes the apposite sugges-
n that the first step in the settlement of
? Northern Territory is to name the
ice. Of the two words of the present
,el, she rightly says, “never were two
irds joined together to form a name more
11 more uninteresting and uninspired,
e adds tliat it ought to be a criminal
ence to call a land by that name, and,
ticipating the inevitable, suggests that
3 naming be not made an opportunity ot
mortalising any battlefield in Europe or
y General operating thereon, but the
.ruble and peculiar people who were its
iginal inhabitants.” Such a live maga-
le deserves a large sale, as it aftoids so
cellent an advertisement of the splendid
id difficult work that The Australian
iland Mission is doing, as without any
spicion of wowserism, they express it,
f o r Christ and the Continent.”
Lieut. David Doull, whom we are in-
rmed on the title page of Mhtli the Aiizacs
Eg apt, was “one of the Gallipoli heroes
ho was with the 17th Battalion, writes
lidebookily, but interestingly, of the super-
nal aspects of Egypt as they struck the
ustralian soldier. His description ot
gyptian life and history tells us nothing
SW ; but liis aim is modest, merely to till
it, as it were, the brief references in Bili-
m’s letters from Egypt with corroborative
jtail. (Packer, Sydney.)

Money in Bees in Australia, a practical
treatise on the profitable management of
the , honey-bee, by Tarlton-Rayment
(Whitcombe and Tombs, Melb. and M.L.),
is a comprehensive and valuable text-book.
Written by a successful apiarist, this well-
illustrated volume should find lodgment in
the library of all Australasian bee-lierdsmen.
A unique feature is the section devoted to
the indigenous flora that is honey and pollen
producing. Most of our honey is produced
from the eucalypts; and full information is
supplied—information that has hitherto been
impossible to procure—as to the quality and
amount of honey that an intelligent and
energetic bee may gather from each variety.

The valuable services of the Y.M.C.A. on
every British Front during the present war
is indicated by the Y.M.C.A. publication,
At Home and at the Front, issued by that
organisation in Melbourne.
J*
Jack Beresford Fowler asks one shilling
for A Heroine of Russia (Specialty Press,
Melb.). The title piece is a one-act play
for two characters, the scene being set near
Lodz during the war, and the characters
being a Russian girl and a German spy.
The play is quite ineffective. A short story,
“Prematurely a Genius,” is extremely youth-
fill work, the story of a child who writes a
masterpiece and conveniently dies, witli
the inevitable result that “Thousands flocked
to visit the author’s grave, and every year
at the anniversary flowers are placed upon
it.” They do these things in Australia,
apparently. The third story is as futile.
Jack Beresford Fowler is advised to culti-
vate a sense of humor: it is the deplorable
lack of that quality of self-criticism that is
responsible for the publication of this book.
*}* /
The latest “Bookstall” shillingsworth,
The Belle of the Bush, by George Darrell,
is a mining yarn, quite up to the level of
interest and execution of the preceding-
issues of this series, but done without any
passionate yearning for literary distinction.
An Australian Decalogue
11. I am thy ’Native Land; thou shalt have
none other gods before me.
2. Thou shalt not take unto thyself any
imported thing; 'whether it come from
the" Empire unthin or the foreigner
without. For I, thy land, am a goodly
land, bearing in plenty all thy needs,
and jealously abhorring the super-
fluity from oversea, and visiting the
fiscal sins of the fathers upon the
children even unto the third and
fourth generation of them that belittle
me, and doing good unto millions of
them that love me and keep up my
own end.
3. Thou shalt change the form of the talcing
of the name of the Lord in vain; for
——- is a played-out and humorless
expletive, and —r— is plain dirt.
Jj. Remember that thou keep holy the will
to ivorkj for by it was thy nation
made, thy liberty and all that it doth
mean; thou shalt turn thine ear
therefore from him that saith: Go
slow, and listen not to the other that
saith: It’s good enough.
5. Honor the memory of the pioneers thy
forefathers and thy foremothers j for
by the sweat of their brows gave they
thee the post upon which thou noiv
n mayest lean.
6. Thou shalt not kill the goose that lays
the golden eggs nor thine enemy with
thy mouth; but rather shalt thou
train thyself from thy youth up to
pay thy way and shoot straight to
defend thy home.
7. Thou shalt hot adulterate; neither thy
food nor thy speech, thy ideals nor
thy breed.
8. Thou shalt not he puffed up with too
inordinate a self-conceit, there are
other peoples besides thee; and thou
shalt learn that there are still two or
three things of which thou wottest
not, unlikely though it seem.
9. Thou slialt not consider it weakness to
mind thy manners, nor to revere that
which thou dost not understand; yea,
even to fear God, and to upe a napkin
at thy meals. . ‘
10. Thou shall not covet thy neighbor's job,
nor his success in life, nor his tailored
suit, nor anything that he hath won;
but rather shall thou butt in and try
to gain as good as he.
YXCTOIiI A.
“COPABELL A.”
Who did invent the word “Anzac”? First
of all the credit was claimed by a signallers
corps at Gallipoli. Then, in The Anzac
Book, General Birdwood definitely stated
that when he took over the command of the
Australian and New Zealand Army Corps
in Egypt, he was asked to select a tele-
graphic code address for his Army Coips
and adopted the word “Anzac.” Later on,
after the landing, he was asked by General
Headquarters to suggest a name for the
beach, and in reply he christened it Anzac
Cove.” Now comes as definite a counter-
claim. General lan Hamilton states, in bis
preface to Crusading at Anzac, A.D. ' •ilo,
by Signaller Ellis Silas: “As the man who
first, seeking to save himself trouble,
omitted the five full stops and brazenly
I coined the word ‘Anzac,’ I am glad to write
a line or two in preface to sketches which
may help to give currency to that token
throughout the realms of glory. ’ Well,
which are we to believe? The matter is,
after all, of considerable historical interest,
and this direct conflict of evidence will
worry the makers of dictionaries. Why
not cable Fisher and get him to bring the
question before the Dardanelles Commis-
sion? The settlement of this historical
question would possibly be of more value
than any other decision the Commission
seems likely to arrive at. ____________

IMPORTANT.-LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

the breath, harden the gums and disinfect the mouth (thus preventing decay).

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Noon: Sourabaya.
Outside the darkened house the white street
runs,
Dazzling as though a myriad shattered suns
Lay strewn in cosmic atoms in its dust.
Heat shakes the intolerable atmosphere, as
lust
Runs in long tremors through the aching,
bare,
Bright limbs of one who hears upon the stair
His lover’s footstep. Nothing stirs at all
Under the dusty trees by the canal,
With pools of purple shadow motionless ;
Where coolies sleep after their midday mess
Of rice and fish and chillies. In the East,
Like the last lamp left burning from some
feast,
When through the curtains pours the garish
dawn,
One sees the moon —how frail and thin and
wan !
N.S.W.
FRITZ S. BURNELL.
Glory.
The beat of drums was in his heart.
He heard the trumpet’s martial note
Tear silences of peace apart,
While pouring from its brazen throat
To marching hosts and cheering throngs
The splendor of adventure’s songs.
The passing clouds took shape to him
As armies marching proudly by.
The whole world breathed applause. War’s
grim,
Red fields of pain where thousands die
Faded before the marching tread
Of glory with a bandaged head.
His comrade praised him to his face,
High citizens their tribute gave;
Fair women, eager 1 , rushed to grace
With sweet applause the honored brave.
But this hope most his fancy moved :
To share his glory with his loved.
But these were dreams! He woke to find
A grey, cold world, with grieving wet,
'While o’er him blew a bitter wind.
Afar, the battle thundered yet;
And he was but a thing of pain
Still living on the cumbered plain.
Then all his dreams of glory fell
Before the pain of wounds of steel;
He saw a vast forsaken hell,
Where lie, alone, survived to feel
The anguish of his wounds, and, worst
Of all, the bitterness of thirst.
“Unknown, untended, I will die.
A wasted clod, without a name!”
He muttered, with a bitter sigh :
“I, who have sought the crown of fame,
Like some poor mangled rabbit fall;
And what is glory, after all?”
But, strange ! While swooning with his woe,
Flis aching wounds, and failing blood.
A light about him seemed to glow,
A radiance, where a spirit stood
That placed a crown upon his head;
And “Glory is my name!” it said.
“The world may grant its tinselled gauds,
And give to men their passing fame,
But he whose deeds his heart applauds,
Although he lie without a name,
Has still a prize that should suffice,
For glory is in sacrifice!”
They found him staring at the sky
W’here, through the clouds, the sun, half-
sad,
Smiled wanly, with unseeing eye;
But on his frozen lips a glad,
Triumphant smile had kept its place
And glory shone upon his face. ’
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Morning: A Fragment.
There comes a sound of milkcarts through
the gloom,
And the day’s news, arriving with a thud,
Lands on the doormat. Ugh! It’s half-past
six.
Monday, confound it! And my boots are
damp—
And where the Hades has my towel gone!
What, no clean collars! Lord, now look
at that!
Nothing to do, and yet they can’t All
right.
I’ve got it. No mistake, the prospect’s vile ;
Dribbles of grey, uncomfortable light,
Bleaker than darkness, more disconsolate,
Over a dreary, dank, wet-footed world.
For Heaven’s sake, Matilda, shake it
up
N.S.W.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper arc copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The liditor ivill carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,

ajrplying to MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 17, 1916.
AUSTRALIAN INTERESTS AND
LOCAL DOG FIGHTS.
George Beeby, ex-Minister for Lands in
N. S. Wales, who is whipping-up what he
calls the “Progressive” A'ote to help the
Liberals tear Holman out of his seedy job,
shows an inclination to talk about things
that matter. Thus at Wagga the other day:
The scheme of government is wrong. The
national Government does not possess the powers
necessary to enable it to meet a great national crisis.
Jt particularly lacks the power to definitely assist
and encourage the establishment of those industries
which the war has shown are necessary for our
national existence. Are we prepared to face an
honest reconstruction of the whole scheme of govern-
ment ; are the smaller States prepared to abandon
(heir special representation in the Senate in
exchange for the necessary extension of Federal
powers; are we prepared to forget our old concep-
tions of State rights; are we prepared to take our
eves off State boundaries, and seriously consider re-
construction of the whole system of government?
The war has more than ever shown that we have
too inanv Parliaments, too many petty, conflicting
interests'between different sections of the Common-
wealth. Are we .prepared to weld these interests in
a movement towards true nationalism ? The wai has
shown that we import many commodities essential
to national life, and that the first duty of a Govern-
ment is bv all means in its power to build up
tertain fundamental industries that can supply these
real essentials. ... e
These are questions which go to the root ot
Australian politics. They raise issues which
above all others matter, nnd ns I he
Bulietin has for a long time been using
words to the same effect, it hopes to hear ot
Beeby talking in this way as often as possi-
ble before the uproar of the general election
breaks out. When that happens it will be
good-bye to everything but the howl and the
yell and noises as of 756 hyenas struggling
for the last bone in the universe. Tearing
their passion to tatters, infuriated party bar-
rackers will leap around the constituencies
with copious promises, undertakings, per-
juries, abusive epithets, allegations, reminis-
cences and forecasts. And after everyone
has forgotten what it is all about and every
question of any real importance to the citi-
zens has been flung into the ash-barrel, the
indecent orgy will suddenly end by all hands
falling in a heap. Then from the fringe of
general untidiness a crowd of dishevelled
survivors will emerge with what they will
call a “mandate” and proceed to do their
worst. It is a ghastly thing to look forward
to, this unclean demonstration of what Par-
liament derives its authority from. The
only answer Beeby can get to these ques-
tions of his is a loud bellowing roar, of
which the correct interpretation may be
something to do with beer or the tote or the
cost of living or the Norton Griffiths
syndicate.
* * * *
Meanwhile, pending the cloud-burst,
Beeby ought to keep going. The position of
Australia in regard to government is one of
the wonders of the earth. The cost of the
system is appalling. The muddle and ineffici-
ency of it is almost too dreadful to think
about. The idea that Australia has a
national Government expired long ago. In-
stead, the nation has seven Governments,
each of which is struggling for pre-eminence
in squandering the public estate and piffling
with national questions. Seven Governments
are taxing Australians, making laws for
them to obey, and pawning them. When
Ivnibbs last reckoned up the cost of main-
taining these seven Parliaments with all
their attendant officialdom and flunkeydom,
he found the annual bill to be £725,265, and
that there were 622 individuals in the Com-
monwealth described as “members of the
Legislature.” That was two years ago.
Sihce then the cost lias gone up; and if the
cost which is indirect be added, the seven
Parliaments now mean a million a year and
probably much more. All these institutions are
handling millions of revenue, splodging
around in a demented carnival of borrowing,
and delving their hands deeper and deeper
into the public pocket. For what? Among
other things for what would bankrupt the
richest nation ever known. During the 10
years before the war the State debts had
gone up at the rate of £9,650,000 a year.
But that is only half the story. The increase
was relatively small at first; but by 1912
those in charge were putting up the mort-
gage at the rate of £20,000,000 a year. Since
then they have spent every shilling they
could lay hands on, and the Federal Govern-
ment has had to find an additional
£50,000,000 per annum for war. The total
owing was £317,000,000 in 1914. At the end
of this year perhaps only Cohen will know
accurately, but if it is less than £450,000,000
Australia is going to be lucky. That is what
seven Parliaments have managed to do. They
stand as a menace to the Australian people
almost as malignant as the Hun. The coun-
try might have stood it a few years longer
had war not intervened. It cannot expect to
stand such madness when the fray is over.
What it can confidently expect is to find
more use for a million than in subsidising half
a dozen superfluous and incompetent Govern-
ments.
* * *■ *
Beeby is only partly right in saying that
the war has shown Australia to have “too
many Parliaments,” and that “we import
many commodities essential to national life.”
These things were sticking out a mile years
before the war-gong went. All that the war
has done is to accentuate the general fat-
lieadedness of the national arrangements
and prove the littleness and mental bank-
ruptcy of 622 politicians and their hangers-
on. A reconstruction of the conditions which
have left Australia a prey to the depreda-
tions of a horde of political filibusters is
imperative. Equally imperative is it that the
people should get into their hands the
weapon of the referendum for protection
against the tyranny and laziness of their
representatives, whether there be only one
sovereign Parliament or seven. As matters
stand now the people are impotent. What
should be the national Government is suffer-
ing from a form of the same complaint. But
the mere widening of its powers would alone
be of little use. Something more than that is
wanted —the driving force of the popular
will, the spur and goad of a democracy par-
ticipating directly in the work of Paiflia-
meijit. The powers of the Federal Parlia-
ment in some directions have been of the
most ample kind. They have been used, and
are being used now, with maddening futility.
Every five minutes some inspired idiot is
leaping to his feet and blowing off long rig-
maroles about the various things Australia
has “solved,” and undertaking to solve some-
thing else. Australia has solved nothing—
at any rate, nothing more than the problems
of Micawber. Public and private finance,
party policies, legislation, settlement, tran-
sport, industrial undertakings, production of
material and the consumption of foreign-
made goods—all have rested on the insecure
foundation of the collective p.n. Even the
first essential of national life —reliance on
national effort —has been sedulously neg-
lected while years and millions were wasted.
To expect Australia to grapple successfully
with the mighty problems facing the Com-
monwealth, hampered by the calamitous sys-
tem existing to-day, would be as vain as to
expect an infant to thrive on a diet of rabbit
poison. The vital needs of the continent are
population and the establishment of indus-
tries —and the hat is going round to repatri-
ate Australians, and the Australian clothes-
peg cannot be bought! Are our Seven Par-
liaments to be depended on to meet these
needs, or shall they be swept away
and a truly national Legislature vested
with the authority the others have so
grossly abused? The Bulletin has little
doubt that a drastic change must come, and
come soon. It will have to come. The
necessity for reconstruction is desperate, and
though not long ago the case was different
there is perhaps no public man in Australia
now who would refuse to admit this to be so.
But the question is not to be decided by
being tossed into the hurly-burly of a State
election riot about booze and petrol and buy-
ing apples on Sunday and the bridge at
Sink-or-Swim. These vast issues on which
tremble the fate of statesmen who draw ex-
penses for “delivering patriotic speeches” re-
quire an atmosphere of their own, and ought
to get it.
WANTED—A DEFENCE ACT TO
MATCH A CITIZEN ARMY.
The first duty of a citizen is to bear arms
for the defence of his country. The first
thing that happens to a citizen of the Com-
monwealth who enlists in the armies of his
country is that he loses by that process
an important part of his citizen rights. It
is specially, stated in the Defence Act that,
although a soldier, the person in uniform is
a citizen also and subject to civil law; but
in the matter of the political freedom which
is the essence of citizenship, the Act makes it
perfectly clear that he is tied and restricted.
Thus, soldiers and officers in uniform are
forbidden to attend or institute “any meeting,
demonstration, or procession for any religious
or political purpose.” They may not even
go to church or attend a funeral unless
“authority has been duly obtained from Dis-
trict Headquarters.” Further, except on the
recommendation of the Military Board and
with the express permission of the Governor-
General, no soldier of the permanent forces
may engage in trade, business or the practice
of a profession or hold any civil office in or
under the Government of any State. Some-
where at the back of all these things is an
Army Act, framed in the remote past and
shaped to meet the needs of the English sol-
dier in the days of Marlborough or earlier,
and viewing an army from the point of view
held by statesmen before modern democracy
came into existence. Under this Act the
soldier is liable to sudden death or some
other . frightful penalty for all sorts of
offences and misdemeanors which in civil life
would be lightly regarded; yet such liability
does not exempt him from the workings of
the civil code so that, even where the court-
martial would lose its terrors, the plain
everyday beak steps in with power to admin-
ister more punishment. In a word, the sol-
dier comes under a strict discipline which
takes away the advantages of his citizenship,
yet he remains a citizen at all points at
which it is to his disadvantage to be one.
* * * *
The idea of an Australian citizen army is
entirely foreign to the Regulations governing
the present forces. The original authors
of the provisions for maintaining discipline
and control among the hefty seventeenth-
century rascals of the first British standing
armies had no conception at all of a mighty
force of sober and law-abiding men taking
up arms in a regular and systematic fashion
for the defence of their country. What is
wanted to meet the new order of things is a
democratic Defence Act, whole within itself
and dependent upon no Army Act of the
past, in which a fair and workable com-
promise is made between military necessity
and citizen freedom. That discipline is
necessary no sane man would attempt to
deny; but a form of discipline based on the
idea that the soldier is naturally a lawless
and dangerous person defeats its own ends
when applied to a citizen army. It is cer-
tainly true that a good deal which remains
on statute books is at most times allowed
to remain a dead letter; but while it exists
in cold print it is always a threat and a
menace, and is liable to be put to bad uses.
To make the soldier feel his citizenship, with
all its honors and privileges, and to widen
that citizenship rather than restrict it, is
one of the first steps towards the creation
of the ideal army, which not only knows its
job but knows what its job is for. That
the task of framing such an Act might be
a big one and fraught with difficulty is no
sort of reason why it should not be
attempted. The defence of Australia is a
big task, too, and fraught also with difficul-
ties ; and where blood and pain and death
are likely factors in the undertaking from
the point of view of the soldier, it ought not
to be too much to expect the politicians
to do their painless share of the job, and see
to it that words which control the soldier
are so framed that they put him on the best
possible footing for soberly carrying out his
work.
* * * *
Among the forces operating against free
enlistment must be counted this Defence
Act, with the dead hand of the antiquated
Army Act laid upon it. As an objection to
a system of compulsory service the wording
of the thing is far worse in effect than any
anti-conscription meeting that could he
held, or than all the anti-enlistment placards
and manifestoes that the Government could
seize. To make the army system of Aus-
tralia democratic is to draw the teeth of that
fierce opposition which lias arisen in some
quarters. To an alleged citizen army, which
isn’t one but is framed on the model of
the standing professional army of the past,
objections of a tolerably emphatic sort can
be raised by any person whose feet are cold
or who feels a call to make a loud noise;
but objections to a true citizen army of the
right shape and bound by regulations in
accord with democratic thought and feeling
could only be of a nature to raise a laugh of
scorn. In the interests of the war most of
our members of Parliament and politicians
feel called upon to keep quiet and do nothing.
Here is an honest job for them in the period
of enforced calm. Let the Defence Act,
which is as vital as anything can be to
Australia’s successful assistance of the
Allied cause, be re-slmped carefully, line by
line, word by word, with true regard for
the altered conditions and with the make
and temperament of the Australian soldier
always in view. No service rendered to the
Commonwealth could be greater than this;
and the time to attend to the business is
now while other subjects of discussion are
held up and there is nothing to obscure the
view of the essentials of our military
system.
* * * *
Any idea that the Australian army won’t
matter much when the present scrap is over
must he put aside. No man living can tell
where this war may lead us, or what new
combinations of forces and powers may arise.
The tumult which has raged over Europe
was certainly foreseen by some, but the
wildest dreamer would never have ventured
an opinion three years ago that Australia
could have been called upon to put so big a
force into the field or to play so strenuous a
part in the desperate game. The future is
shrouded in haze and uncertainty; and the
one great need is to be ready for anything
that may occur. The Australian notion of
a citizen army in which every man will take
his place as naturally as he breathes air is
the surest safeguard that the child will be
safe in the cot and the roof remain over
the wife’s head. We need our manhood
organised and drilled to efficiency; we need
arms and munition of our own manufacture
and the plant and equipment for making
more; we need our railway system set in
order for the swift transport of troops and
the main north and south line completed;
but as much as these, because without it
these things are deprived of a great deal of
their value, we need a Defence Act to meet
our special circumstances.
BIN ALONG AND BURROW A—Y.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was glad when they said unto me, “Let
us go into the house of the gum-tree.” It
was good to “go bush,” even a paltry 250
miles from Sydney. A train vomited me
at Binalong at 4 o’clock on a cold and foggy
morning, and all was not well. A man told
me that “over there” in the impenetrable
rain there was a valley, and in the valley
that most welcome thing a pub. For even
the wowser asketli not hymn-tunes nor
desireth he at such a time to till his belly
with the east wind. I climbed a hill of
mud and then felt my way on a descending
grade of slush. All I could see of the
valley was a wet hole filled with wet fog
so thick that the rain seemed unable to
fall into it and had to stay on the hill and
me. I walked further into the slush and
heard a river roaring curses to itself, but
the darkness was thicker and there was
neither light nor noise. As for the pub, I
might as' well have walked into the bush, if
I could have found it, and asked a stringy-
bark for a double bed and a kookaburra to
bring the. shaving water at seven. So I
felt my way back to the hill and down to
the railway, where the signal light merely
blurred the fog. The man in charge gave
me the run of the office fire, and I sat there
till daylight. The Melbourne express gal-
loped past to Sydney, and the Albury mail
tore through to Yass, and I smoked till all
my flues were full.
At daylight there was a saturated hotel
and a damp cook aching from two weeks of
rain. Binalong was an old place in the
early days, but it is being made over. A
ruined church, white on the summit of a
green rise; new brick houses and streets
switchbacking over a baker’s dozen of hills.
The barber is also a bootmaker, and uses
razor and awl according to the sacred prin-
ciples of public demand. Healthy children
with clean eyes and noses not so clean,
happy in the rain and slush when the watery
sun shone for an hour ; the new grass green
with the cold greenness of a cemetery under
rain. A white horse trotted up the street,
drawing a sulky festooned with dead rabbits.
The hotel was sorry that it hadn’t sent
a man to meet the train as usual; but who
could expect a hotel runner for the one
passenger a month whom bad luck or fool-
ishness sends to Binalong? And the six
o’clock lunacy will make matters worse.
What country hotel can afford to cater for
man or beast arriving after midnight when
the main bringer of revenue is turned oil:
at the tap before dusk? While I waited
the return of the kindly crookbacked man
who rode a bareback horse over the hills to
find a vehicle for my explorations, the
equally kindly lady publican directed me
to the lion of the town —which is the grave of
Gilbert, the Bushranger. Gilbert and Dunn
were trapped near Binalong. Local sentiment
hates Dunn’s grandpapa, who gave them
away to the police after pouring water into
their guns so that they could not make a fight
for it. It’s a healthy thing, that hatred of
treachery which removes the sympathy to
the betrayed even if he be a ruffian.
Christ’s tragedy would be not so poignant
but for the treachery of Judas and the
defection of fair-weather friend Peter—
who, unregenerate, was a very friable rock
to build a church on.
The local picture-show was to screen
Ned Kelly that night— Ned who was born
too early; whose resource, rapid action,
courage and Napoleonic ability to move
quickly and strike decisively would have
made him a great soldier in these days. I
declined to make the pilgrimage to the dead
bushranger, and then a big car came up and
took me through the slush for a half-mile
until we struck the road to Burrowa —a
quick road hard on horses. It was a drive
to thank God for when the sun came out —
the blue and purple hills; the copperas
green of new crops; the brown-green of
pasture; the eucalyptus green of trees.
Much of the land is held in share-farms,
the landlord’s share being up to 225. 6d. an
acre, which is a fine yearly rent. It is
figures like these that make the landlord
believe in leasehold of land—by Private
Enterprise. Leaseholds to farmers from the
Government are immoral because the Gov-
ernment lessee doesn’t pay more than 4d.
or 6d. an acre. Down on George’s River
the Sutherland Estate is selling 91) years’
leases of building lots at the price of free-
hold. thus eating its cake and having it too.
Water snaked in jard-wide streams
through the foothills; the car steeplechased
through foot-deep lagoons in the road. And
so we ran into Burrowa, now swankily
called by the Commonwealth “Booroowa,”
hut by the old hands “Burroway.” They
were wild men, those old hands. Their
traditional holiday speech was: “God bless
the good blue rock of Burroway that
wouldn’t break on a man’s skull, and devil
damn him that would cast the first stone
on the sports ground.” But they’re good
people. They made a working bee for the
Belgians, and in a day ploughed and sowed
100 acres of wheat for the people without
a country. It should be good wheat, too,
for it was sown for the poor, on a Sunday.
The windiest mailcart in Australia runs
from Burrowa to Frogmore, Avith a big.
white Italian umbrella over the mailman’s
seat to make the winds feel colder when


they strike the corners. But the mailman,
like most of these tall, thin old men here,
is tough and apparently full of bear’s
warmth. One long old prospector here has
often walked the 21 miles to Yass in 41
hours.
It is a cold day for the prospector from
Binalong to Burrowa. Out at Ivangiara
there are mixed metals that mean penal-
isation over and above the deductions
and returning charges of that public
Bahabbas and pirate, the smelting company.
One very old show, profitable when last
worked, has been closed to prospectors by
a shepherder who bluffed peggers with tales
of an antique land grant. He is an old man
with whiskers that look like clean lace
curtains, and he had a habit of silently
approaching the pegger and shouting loudly
in his ear: “Booh!” Whereat the pegger
mostly dropped the peg and fled.
I went out there to see a copper show ; but
the Burrowa River roared a-fiood, and the
mine from a distance didn’t seem important
enough to warrant me in performing the
Lord Ullin’s Daughter trick. So we stood
on the hither side and looked over the
muddy river at the dump. Like Moses on
Pisgali may have viewed the Compromised
Land.
But if I didn’t find copper there I found
gold—true metal, a woman of nigh 70, who
looked and moved as if she were 40. She
runs a boarding-house for 18 miners; has
been mother and father both to 10 children,
and, having seeii them to maturity, must
needs take over four grandchildren, the
youngest a bottle baby when he came to her.
She was born in the late ’4o's, the daughter
of a shepherd at Rye Park, and is a great-
grandmother.
“I was my father’s boy,” says this great-
grandmother, “and I split rails and fenced
and ploughed and reaped, and now I can’t
get tired. Use —that’s what it is.”
For 17 years she drove the mail from
Burrowa to Rye Park three days a week,
and the three other days she did the laundry
work of 40 Burrowa people. She was at the
washtub from 4 a.m. till 9; then away
with the mail to drive the 30-mile round;
back at dusk to use the flatiron till 11.
Her son wants her to quit work, and she will
not, because she can’t be happy idle. She
has for payment the love of the four grand-
children she mothers; I saw that for myself.
The 60 years of bushwork have not limited
her sympathy. Every traveller with a swag
is invited to a feed —to a camp if it be late,
and a breakfast to give him courage in the
morning.
Here in this old-young woman, with the
unselfish affection of youth in her—this fine
Australian who can’t get tired—is the real
spirit and genius of Australia. You shall
not find it among the flag-flappers and self-
advertisers in the cities, but out among the
gum-trees—the spirit of kindliness which
sends no man hungry on his way because he
has not any money.
“Can’t get tired !” One week of her life
js worth to Australia probably more than all
the years of all the leisured women of Castle-
reagh-street, with the half-mast skirts and
the high boots and the cultivated taste in
cocktails. Two generations more and the
great-great-great-grandchildren of the woman
who can’t get tired may wear the then
equivalent of the fashion that decreases the
skirt to increase its legs; but the fine spirit
of that bushwoman will survive in them.
Born in the cruel days of New South Wales
before the discovery of gold gave all men a
chance, she has fared through her many
maternities while doing the labor of man—
paid at the last by the children who know
only one mother, and that one their mother’s
own. There are many like her in the bush—
the big, sturdy mothers of the world-beaters
who gave decency to the blunder of Gal-
lipoli. They get no spoon-feeding at Govern-
ment hands. In most cases they would resent
it. But that doesn’t make right the spoon-
feeding of misfits. There is a case of assisted
immigration in England—the case of an
Englishman and his wife who paid but £2 a
head of their own money for the trip to Aus-
tralia. Great “settlers” they were, and the
result of their importation is that the man
peddles typewriters. And down at Terang
(Vic.) there_is a family of 10 Australians
who pay £ll7 a year to Private Enterprise
as the rent of 160 acres of dairy pastures!
They make good only because, like the young
great-grandmother by the Burrowa River,
they can’t get tired.
Back in Burrowa that night I ate cold
meat in a gloomy cathedral garnished with
tomato-sauce bottles. There were others
tending for the train journey. But the cold
mutton was also cold shoulder. Eaten in
silence with never a champing of the bit.
It was worth it all—the marooning at
Binalong. the slush, the river spate that
would not let me see the copper show, and
the cold shoulder in the licensed cathedral;
worth it all for the knowledge of the Aus-
tralian great-grandmother who can't get
tired.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Is it a Fair Thing?
Stripped of oratorical decorations the
problem connected with compulsory military
service boils down to this —Is it a fair
thingV If it is not, Australia wants none
of it. If it is, no justification exists for
evading the consequences. In any case the
question has to be answered, not by hot air
and rhetorical bombast, but by plain yes or
no. It is the facts, responsibilities and perils
of the hour that Australia has to consider
and to meet. They cannot be met by throw-
ing words about, or taking as the basis of
action some theoretical scheme of social
perfection. The most gigantic struggle in
the history of mankind is going on. Men
are being killed by the hundred thousand.
To some of the warring nations it is a fight
for life. Already they have all bled severely,
and the brutal truth is that victory cannot
yet be seen. So far as the Allies are con-
cerned the best that can be said is that they
have turned aside defeat and are now able
to hit back. But to imagine that the war will
end in a month or two would be to again
become victims of that demented optimism
which long after the conflict opened para-
lysed the thought of the British people. For
the Allies to now become divided in their
purpose, or for one or more of them to fight
with any less than full strength or ease up
a bit to see how things were going, would
be something the like of which Berlin has
long been anxiously hoping to hear. Every-
thing depends upon the Allies sticking
together and all doing their best. Desperate
attempts to separate them have been made
by Germany. But they still stand pledged
to solidarity. In the common cause, which
Australia shares —for we are in it up to
the neck —the soldiers of France and
Russia and Italy are fighting with unex-
ampled bravery and endurance. They are
fighting for the Commonwealth as definitely
as the Anzacs are. Would it be a fair thing
for Australia to be the first to pull out?
Would* it be a fair thing for the Australian
people to do what they would howl execra-
tion at any other country for doing? If it
would not, is there any fairness in Australia
depending on the Frenchmen and the Rus-
sian to put the whole of their weight into
the scrum while Australians plod on with
something less? In this business they are
our mates. Are we going back on them?
The recruiting figures have long made it
plain, in spite of the juggling of politicians,
that these* cpiestions will have to be
answered, and soon. They are what Aus-
tralia is now waiting for Hughes to handle
in the language of candor.
In the Market for Votes.
Of course it is no use protesting: the
Holman Government has found out that
the easiest way to get political power is to
buy it, and for the present that is the end
of the matter. Hence the Bill authorising
the North Shore Bridge has again been put
through the Assembly, and unless the Coun-
cil throws out the measure the work will
he proceeded with at the most inopportune
time in all our history. That the Govern-
ment should even think of borrowing heavily
for a work of this nature, when every avail-
able pound is wanted for our national preser-
vation, is a scandal; and the two Ministers
principally concerned have no decent right
to ever go upon a platform again and talk
about “patriotism.” In this business, at
any rate, they are the allies of Germany.
But. apart from that consideration, the
work should not be thought of at present
because of the abnormal rate of interest.
The Government has just borrowed a couple
of millions or so in England to keep its
friends or masters, or whatever they are.
the Nokton Griffiths syndicate, supplied
with commissions as agreed; and the rate
of interest is 5i per cent. Add various
charges, and the total cost is not a penny
less than 5i per cent., as against the average
pre-war rate of about 3| per cent. On
present appearances the cost of the bridge
will considerably exceed £3*000.000; and
the extra If per cent, will mean loading the
country with a permanent extra charge of
£52,500 a year. Certainly, as Cann, the .Min-
ister for Works, says, the job will not be all
done at once; but there is going to be dear
money for a long while yet—probably dearer
money before the Avar is over ; and, in view
of this grave uncertainty, the fact that the
money will not be all wanted at once is not
in our favor at all. In any case, every £lOO
spent even now means an entirely unneces-
sary burden of £1 15s. per annum —put
upon the people of this country, let. it be dis-
tinctly understood, for no other purpose than
to_ buy votes. Those Avdiose votes are bought
with a job-will no doubt pay the expected
price at the next general election. It will
remain for decent people, with healthier
ideas about political honesty, to save the
country from the adventurers.
The Wage-earner’s Dole of Suet.
The signal for a weary old controversy was
hoisted at Adelaide the other day when “the
basic Jiving- wage” came into an Industrial
Court argument. The employees were ask-
ing for more cash to keep up with the in-
creased cost of living, and someone produced
a scale of expenditure, prepared by Dr.
Arthur, M.L.A., of Sydney, setting out
“the reasonable minimum diet for a man,
woman and three children.” Arthur
figured it out this way:—
Bread, 201 b £0 2 11
Meat, 161 b 0 10 .8
Blitter, 111 b. .. .. 0 2 2
Oatmeal. . . . .. 0 0 8
Milk, seven quarts . . 0 3 0
Sugar, 41b. . . . . 0 12
Jam, lib. . . .. 0 0 9
Treacle, 21b. .. .. 0 0 7
Rice, 21b. .. .. 0 0 0
Pegs, 21b. . . .. 0 0 0
Potatoes, 141 b. . . .. 0 16
Raisins, lib. . . . . 0 0 8
Tea, lib. -. . .. 0 0 9
Suet, lib. . . . . 0 0 8
Flour, 41b. .. .. 0 0 8
Condiments .. .. 0 0 5
Vegetables .... .. 0 16
Total .. ..£197
As soon as the dismal-looking document
had been read the employers’ representative
threatened to produce in rebuttal medical
evidence to prove that a family could be
kept in good health on a less costly dietary.
Since then the details of Arthur’s list
have been turned inside out all over Aus-
tralia, and the air has been filled with pro-
testation _ that (1) it is about right, and
(2) .it is all wrong. Iteally it conveys
nothing, _ though probably the average
citizen will have a wild yearning to know
why Arthur objects to allow an additional
three ha’pence for condiments. At Bath-
urst gaol last year the daily cost of main-
taining 317 prisoners ran out at fid. per
head, and if five prisoners can be fed for
2s. 6d. per day it is possible that 17s. 6d.
per week would keep a family of five from
perishing. If tb<*y were fed on the same
scale as the British and other deep-sea sea-
men who live on windjammers, the cost
could be reduced even below that figure.
That many workers’ families are kept in
food on an even smaller expenditure than
£1 9s. 7d. per week The Bulletin has no
doubt after consulting Knibbs. The aver-
age weekly wage of 14 classes of workers
examined by the Commonwealth Statis-
tician was £2 15s. A man earning that
sum and julopting the Arthur scale would
have £1 ss. sd. left to provide five people
with clothes, fuel, medical attendance, a
house to live in, lighting, boots, books, hair
cuts, amusements, tramfares, renewals of
furniture and so on. The thing to wonder
at is not that it can be done but that it is
done by so many, and that a few do it on
much less. But what in Heaven’s name is
the use of producing in Industrial Courts
these sorrowful estimates of how much
oatmeal and treacle and suet and sugar are
required by a man, woman and three kids ?
Suppose the old man objects to suet, that
treacle gives the youngsters toothache, and
mother gets bilious if she tackles meat?
There is no conceivable rule by which a
Court can fix wages on the basis of five
persons devouring 141 b. of butter per week
and getting through lib. of raisins. Experi-
ments in that direction would either be
dodges or preliminaries to the preparation of
official lists for various types of wage-
earner, setting forth how much of each
week’s income must be disbursed in rent,
attire, food and sundries, with elaborate
details of the drapery and grocery bills.
Every five minutes afterwards the atmo-
sphere would be shattered by medical men
explaining that Bill Smith, slaughterman,
was consuming too much proteid, and
angry carpenters would demand to have
an allowance for currants. If wages were
paid on the basis of “the maintenance of
health,” according to medical formula, Aus-
tralian labor and industry would be brought
to the level of the coolie. It is all wrong.
But if it is right for the employee it is
right also _for the employer. Why should
he make £5OOO a year if he can buy enough
rice and potatoes to keep him and his family
alive for £6O?

Australia’s War.
“Australia has done well.” In a limited
sense that is true. But “well” is only a
relative term. The circumstances are such
that we don’t do really well if we don’t do
all we can. It is not theories that are at
stake. _ The whole future of the Common-
wealth is involved. Its national existence
is in the scales. Australian soldiers are
not fighting “to show their loyalty to
Britain.” They are fighting and dying to
protect the freedom of their country against
an enemy who aimed at domination of the
world, and had established himself at the
back door of Australia. If the German
were to win this war he would return there,
and with something more formidable than
the two cruisers which dodged the Australia.
He would hold this continent in the hollow
of his hand. The job Australia started on
was to prevent him doing one or the other,
either at once or later on. That job has to
be completed. Australians are as much
called upon to stick to it as any of the
others. And it is a reasonably fair thing
to ask that the Federal Government, even at
the eleventh hour, shall act on the belief
that Australians want to stick and play
the game with their mates. The argument
that this war is not Australia’s war has no
foundation. Even if it had, it would be
an objection to having had anything to do
with the disturbance, not an argument
against compulsory service. But how often,
by the way. was the objection put forward
while the Gniesenau and the Scharnhorst
were sneaking about our waters?
The Australian Wash and Shave.
The Commonwealth Government having
prohibited the importation of soap, the next
thing to expect is an outburst of wrath from
someone because he is compelled to go with
dirty hands or because the health of the
rising generation is imperilled through
having its face washed with the Australian
product. 'We are one of the few peoples
on eai th who are unable to rely on them-
■selves for anything except 'water when
they take a bath. We usually import the
soap and the towel and if we run to a
sponge we import that too. And yet we
are one of the few peoples who grow their
own fat. Last year we imported 1300 tons
soap, of which about half consisted of
little ornamental packages with a nice
smell. The rest was the other sort of soap.
A e paid over £125,000 to the foreigner who
changed the fat and other things into soap
and gave it an aroma and packed it up in
several layers of decorative paper. For
® oa o that was just plain soap we paid
£-0,000. And on top of that there were
heavy charges for freight and insurance and
dragging about and all the rest. Most of
the “toilet, fancy and medicated” soaps
have been coming from Britain and Yankee-
land, with limited consignments also from
France and Germany. Is there any conceiv-
able reason why Australia should not make
its soap on the premises V This paper knows
of none. The fat is here, the smells are
available, there are people looking for jobs
and there are five million people who wash
at Yeast once a day. This is a case in
which the Government has an undoubted
right to insist that the country shall manu-
facture for itself. The present arrange-
ment is in consequence of there being an
immense demand for glycerine, which is a
by-product of the manipulation of tallow in
soap manufacture and is used in the making
of munitions. But this is no reason why
Australia should not after the war go on
making all the soap Australians require and
continue supplying additional quantities of
glycerine to the over-sea buyer of that com-
modity. From the national point of view
the less soap Australia imports and the
more glycerine she exports the greater is
the public saving of cash and the larger the
number of people to draw wages every
week. There are dozens of other commo-
dities to which identical considerations, apply
and should be treated in the same way. They
will have to be, anyhow, before the war is
over. Soap is not the only thing Aus-
tralians will have to make for themselves or
go without.

The Commonwealth Government’s
“Economy.”
Ax the very moment when that dull bluffer.
Defence Minister Pearce, was inviting
the public to “point out any instance of
extravagance in Government departments,”
a soulless but notoriously capable account-
ant of Melbourne, W. G. lliscock, was
giving dozens of instances. He was telling
the Canberra Commission about the things
he had seen in that dry place, and about
the things he had tried to see and couldn’t
because they weren’t there. He said the
administrator’s residence cost the depart-
ment £7055; he figured that it should have
cost about £2OOO, and he didn’t see any-
thing that represented the rest of the cash.
The power-house cost the Department
£43,951; Htscock figured thpt it should
have, cost £20,125. These irregularities vexed
his soul, but when he came to the Canberra
Hospital he needed it. That beautiful insti-
tution cost a suffering country £8785, and he
reckoned it was worth £2750. It takes
£2Oll a year to run the establishment —
doctor £s>oo, coal £BO and drugs £94 per
patient! Luckily there aren’t many patients
—one and a quarter per month is the
average—no doubt because the Queanbeyan
hospital is handy and the military hospital
at Duntroon but three miles away. When
the unimaginative investigator revived he
was able to gasp that the Commonwealth
got £454 a year rent for its various pro-
perties, whereas it ought to be getting
£SO3I. It is a lurid narrative and throws
a bright light on the vast gulf that lies
between this Government’s prattle about
economy and its performance.
George Turner came to the end of his
uncommonly useful career at the end of last
week: heart disease, from which he
had suffered for many years, took
him off in Melbourne at the age
of 65. The Bulletin’s “plain, brown
man ’ came first into prominence in
1894. when Victoria was faced with the job
of cleaning away the wreckage of the awful
borrowing boom. For years .Melbourne had
been living in a state of exaltation. George
Turner was the invaluable man who knew
that two and two are four, and had the cour-
age and honesty to face the fact no matter
how much better off he would have been had
it been five—or three. He brought to public
business the same hard commonsense that he
knew would be expected of him by any client
who called in with business to be done at his
little law office; and that was all the magic
there was about him. v How well the quality
served the State in a great crisis may be
judged by the fact that he was kept in office
for five of the most woeful and strenuous
years in the history of Victoria; and when
Edmund Barton was forming his first
Commonwealth Government there seemed no
other possible man for Treasurer but the
plain, methodical, direct, kindly, unassuming
Victorian. Afterwards he was with his
friend Deakin in the latter’s first Ministry ;
but thereafter his health was bad, and for
.10 years past he had lived as quietly as
possible, content to be lost among the crowd
of other unpretentious little men who
carried their brown bags into business every
morning.

The Harvester.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A message to the New York Times says that the
German Emperor is spending- a holiday in helping
personally to reap the harvest, and thereby gaining-
physical exercise. He has divested himself of his grey
field uniform, and toils for whole afternoons in the
heat. He soon acquired a healthy tan. — Cable.
A continent is strewed with death and loss;
The gasp of death has stifled childhood’s
mirth;
Humanity is stretched upon a cross
That throws a shadow all about the earth;
And on its brow is set an impious crown
A tanglement of steel, of weapons gross,
From which the drops of agony fall down.
Yet still survives the sunlit, fruitful field,
Smiling and peaceful, specked with peer-
ing flow’rs,
That in the glory of its golden yield
Show gems of color, though that shadow
towers;
Here some may reap, forgetting all the hates
That rage around them, in some happy
hours.
But not for him this peaceful harvest waits!
Where outraged bodies are in masses piled
Amongst the ruins where contentment
reigned—
Babe and its mother, grey-haired sire and
child —-
Where manhood lies all blood and mire
bestained;
Where madness raves within the shattered
mind,
A harvest fair have Lust and Murder
gained.
Yet still another harvest he shall find!
With bloody hand War rakes its harvest in
From rifled life ; on leprous limbs, Disease
Creeps through the land; and Hunger seeks
to win
A triumph over all the miseries
Of violated homes, and hearts that break.
The harvest that they take belong to these,
And he, who loosed them, at their terrors
quake.
O mighty fool! The harvest you shall reap
A thing of agony and sweat shall be!
The haunted mind that never-more will sleep
But fly in terror through infinity
Pursued by shrieks that of base murders tell,
Th - anguished sound of tears that women
weep,
And curses of a million souls in Hell! /

What a Little Man Saw when He Peeped
into Hell.
At Melbourne on Monday Hughes unbur-
dened himself about the war ; and, of course,
the purple patches were many and the ora-
torical trimmings plentiful. But the speech
was a notable one, all the same, not so
much for what it contained as for the fact
that its general tone was the first real indi-
cation the Australian people have had of a
determination on the part, of the Government
to take the blinkers off the public and invite
it to look squarely at the truth about the
war and the position of the Commonwealth.
Stripped of decoration, what Hughes has
to say to the Australian citizen is:—
You have had your head in a bucket. Thus blinded,
you have imagined the impossible. You have dreamed
vain dreams. This war is a desperate and bloody
business. It is draining Europe white. Victory will
only be achieved by the Allies after tremendous effort
and at terrible cost. And decisive victory—the only
victory by which the world may be saved from fur-
ther horrors —is a long way off.
There is nothing new in this. It has been
said by other people before. But recogni-
tion of it has not, so far, been the basis of
Australian policy in any single direction.
The official dope, and the unofficial blather-
skite and the political shirker have all con-
spired to prevent the public mind from
grasping the immensity of the struggle in
which the British people are engaged, or
recognising the task before them. Cock-and-
bull yarns about the enemy’s exhaustion and
all the rest of it have taken the place of
truths about his strength. But even the
blind are beginning to see that a war of
exhaustion cuts two ways. Only by a force
greater than her own will Germany be beaten
to her knees; and if she is not beaten, not
only will the arms of Germany have won, but
the spirit of Prussia will gain world-wide
ascendancy. Hot air and resolutions are not
going to win this war; nor is a continua-
tion of the demented wasting of Australia’s
economic .strength going to assist fis to meet
the ugly problems of the days to come..
Meanwhile the war is raging with furious
intensity on battlefields hundreds of miles
apart. The Austrian is getting it in the
neck, the German is fighting desperately to
maintain his grip on Northern France. The
position on the whole is full of encourage-
ment- —not to sit down in the comfortable
belief that the end is within sight, but to
make redoubled efforts to secure relief by
victory. Once more, what is Hughes going
to no about it.?

According to N. S. Wales Works Minister
Cann, the Government’s newest uncle is
not to get the job of building the North
Shore bridge:—
Tenders would have to be called throughout the
world, as there was not a sufficiently large plant
in Australia, and under the circumstances the time
allowed for the completion of the work —seven
years—would probably have to be extended.
One of the alleged reasons why the Norton-
GrifflTHS shindykit was fastened on to
N. S. Wales was the enormous plant it was
supposed to possess, or be able to produce
when required. Now, it appears, the
mighty plant has got frost-bitten, or eaten
by wild aphis, or something. Gann’s addi-
tional remark, “nor will the work be carried
out by day labor,” sounds as if one more
plank of the Labor platform is mighty close
to combustion point, too.
One of these days Victoria will simply have
to do something to put its charitable institu-
tions on a self-respecting basis. Melbourne
Hospital has drifted £26,000 in debt and is
paying interest at the rate of £3O a week.
This deplorable state of affairs is not sur-
prising when it is known that, although in
four years the annual cost of maintaining
the hospital has risen from £28,277 to
£52,066, the Government and municipal
grants have been increased by only £290.
Years ago an inspector of charities reported
that the State was wasting something like
£150,000 per annum in charitable adminis-
tration. yet no remedy has been applied.
The Government lacks the nerve to impose
a charity tax and has conscientious scruples
or something about raising a tax indirectly
by means of the totalisator. So money for
the maintenance of hospitals and other
important public institutions is raised by
the voluntary system, which costs anything
from 25 to 40 per cent, in collecting fees
and is an untidy system, anyhow.
If the newspaper reports are correct Judge
Fitzhardinge has just provided Newcastle
with some queer law. During the six o’clock
pub-closing campaign on the coalfield a Cold
Tea orator, in reply to an interjection,
“Even the police are against you,” said:
“I can quite understand that, seeing the
number of pots they receive on the nod from
the publicans.” The police were about tired
o: charges of this sort, so the orator was
charged with insulting language, and a
plain, sensible S.M. fined him £l.
Against this he appealed to his Honor
Fitzhardinge, who decided that, “had it
not been for the high character of the New-
castle police force the words complained of
might be insulting. For instance, it would
not be insulting to say that the Anzacs are
all cowards, because it would be so utterly
untrue.” The Judge’s definition of an
insult is apparently “an unpleasant truth.”
The greater the truth the worse the insult.
And the less the truth the less the insult,
until finally if there is no truth at all in a
statement there is no insult in it. It is
therefore a mere pleasantry to say that the
Judges of N. S. Wales are in the pay of the
publicans. No Judge could possibly regard
it as an insult. This doctrine taught by
the Bench at Newcastle is too dangerous
to be left untouched, and a higher Court
ought to be given an opportunity to say
that, even if Fitzhardinge was right in
allowing the appeal, in the opinion of
Supreme Court Judges he was utterly wrong
in his remarkable reasons.
Sydney Telegraph made a cynical confes-
sion the other morning. Ten years or so
back the paper was George Beid’s chief
fugleman when that unprincipled old fox,
run out of his other tricks, started his anti-
Sosh campaign and went to the country on
that cry. There are some people who still
talk of the tiger which Reid then chased up
and down the country, and who still fear the
brute. Yet listen now to the paper that was
Reid’s chief mouthpiece:—•
Once again, it is said, George Turner was reluc-
tant to take office in a Federal Ministry [the Reid-
McLean] which did not include Mr. Dear in. Mr.
Turner’s misgivings were justified by the events of
1905, when, owing to the effort of the coalition to
sink the fiscal iss-ue and its raising of a false anti-
Socialist cry, it was dismissed by the House of
Representatives after one of the most painful debates
ever listened to.
George Reid wanted Australia to go on
getting its mouse-traps, etc., from Germany;
and rather than honestly face his opponents
on the question and stand by the conse-
quences, he invented a bogus Sosh tiger. Of
course, The Bulletin proved all this
against the colossal humbug years ago. It
is some satisfaction, though, to get a cynical
confession of it now from the newspaper
that was deepest in the conspiracy.
“Hamer”: The Holman crowd appears to
have fallen into another odorous pit. It
seems that a prospective Labor candidate
for election to the Assembly did not possess
the necessary P.L.L. qualifications, but that
trifling difficulty was got over by his friends
with the aid of a union secretary who was
willing to assist in making things right. So
the candidate was accredited as a three
years’ unionist and his nomination paper
was duly signed and attested by the P.L.L.
as all in order. A Cabinet Minister and a
civil servant are alleged to have been the
instigators of the plot, and the P.L.L.
executive when it got to hear of it promptly
expelled the two minor conspirators from
its books. The Minister meanwhile has
been firmly requested to roll up and explain
his devious tracks.
Two plucky but unlucky Australians were
in the last lot of invalids to reach Sydney.
One was that good recruiter, Corporal Fern,
M.L.A., who fell sick before he reached
Egypt; the other Lieut. Hart, Australia’s
own airman, who, after being lent to the
Royal Flying Corps as instructor, suffered
from the effects of old injuries almost as
soon as he had struck the cold of the North,
and had to be invalided home.
Peacock’s newest idea is to order his
Tramway Control Board to make repara-
tion to the Railways for competition with
suburban traffic —estimated by the Commis-
sioners to cost the Department about
£40,000 a year. But the rule ought to
work both ways. Several outer and newer
tramlines bring traffic to the railways—not
only passenger, but goods, because the people
wouldn’t be where they are but for the
trams. But, anyhow, where’s the sense of
taking money out of one pocket to put it into
another? It only wears out the pocket, and
Peacock can hardly call that economy.
There’s nothing doing in regard to the six
o’clock closing crusade as far as one Scotchby-
terian parson in Vic. is concerned. Rev.
J. G. Davies, of Bendigo, told his congrega-
tion that they could do what they liked about
it, but he didn’t think it was up to him to
meddle in the matter. If the publicans tried
to close the churches at 6 p.m. on Sundays
the churches would tell them to mind their
own business, and his idea was that what
was sauce for the clerical goose was sauce for
the Bung gander.

Melbourne Princess’s brought Puccini’s well-
beloved operas, “Butterfly” and “Boheme,”
into comparative criticism. Both perform-
ances were successful, but “Boheme” was
decidedly the better of the two in nearly
all respects. “Butterfly” is the one Italian

opera in the world which can be more con-

looked fully a dozen years older that, her
real age and much less attractive than a
prima donna ought to look, her wig and
kimono being unbecoming. But she sang
the music prettily and acted pleasingly up
to the point where she grabbed an ensan-
guined piece of muslin to stanch her death
wound. Also the tenor, Lois, was pleasing
enough, although he has only a limited
range of stereotyped gestures and poses,
and he holds his notes back in his throat to
sweeten them. Belloni, the very tall and
dignified baritone, was something like an
American Consul in the first act. but grew
less satisfactory afterwards. The beautiful
“letter” music suffered from his tremolo,
and his dismal black suit suggested that he
foresaw the sad end of Butterfly and was
dressed in readiness for the funeral. Miss
Russ, of the nondescript voice, played the
part of Suzuki charmingly, and Miss Piccolo
was an elderly and colorless Mrs. Pinkerton.
The orchestra did some good work, especially,
in the silent vigil music which ends the
second act. but the fruit of Pinkerton’s
unholy wedlock Avas an infant who behaved
very badly at the first attempt.
* * -* ' *
“Boheme,” with the same three principals
in foremost parts, Avas one of the best
achievements of the season. The four men
—Lois, Belloni, Seamuzzi and Cacialli —
made a capital party of bohemians. Petrucci
Avas just excellent as the old landlord in
the first act and the gay dodderer avlio has
to pay the bills in the second. Miss Yisoni’s
bright young personality and incisive Avarble
gave neAV distinction to Musetta, the giddy
minx. And Miss Gonsalez Avas an interest-
ing Mimi. None of the charm of Puccini’s
music was lost in the singing of the solos,
duets and quartets, although the flow was
sometimes interrupted by demands for an
encore. Italians, seemingly, look for encores
in the modern operas the same as in the old-
fashioned works where the composer sup-
plied pauses for applause. The death scene
Avas played less melodramatically and more
impressively than usual. Blemishes and
shortcomings in the performance seemed due
to Avant of rehearsal. The chorus croAA’d in
the second act was very so-so, and the
orchestra and the principals had not come
to a complete understanding on all points.
But when it gets to Sydney this opera should
go as smoothly as any of them.
The novelty for this week is “Gioconda,”
which comes to hearing on Friday. Next
week will be the last of the opera season
and of Marlow’s management of the Prin-
cess’s. With Marlow it is a case of all’s
well that ends well. The Bulletin said
hard things of him when he was
running bedroom bellodrama, but on step-
ping-stones of his dead managerial self he
has risen to higher things tit popular prices.
Press and public are grateful for his recent
plucky ventures.
For the failure of “Too Many Cooks” at
the Criterion (Sydney) on Saturday night
only two of the fraternity can be blamed —
the cook who chose the play for an Austra-
lian audience and the cook who cast it. The
cook who - composed the comedy, Frank
Craven, dished it up to tickle the palate
of his own American diners ; and the nght
frothy confection doubtless satisfied pe< pie
accustomed to that sort of fare. And
the other cook, the stage carpenter, did
all that could be expected of him.
The comedy has apparently been con-
structed to show how a desirable resi-
dence can be constructed for £5OO in three
acts. There is some slight but genuine
comedy in the conflicting ideals of the young-
couple who are building their cosy home, and
in the plethora of advice that every other
character proffers to the distracted pair; but
the story drags. And the introduction of a
mob of farcical waybacks into the comedy
adds unnecessary noise without contributing
materially to its humor.
* * * *
Edwin Lester, the contractor for the build-
ing, played an important serious character
with a genial conscientiousness: and Tom
MacLarnie, despite an irritating forced laugh
and an unnecessary strut, managed to make
of his old-man part a convincing character.
Hale Hamilton had few opportunities for his
special gifts of comedy ; but all he had to do
was done with delightful naturalness. Ilonald
Bowles was inimitably grotesque; but in his
praiseworthy efforts to force the laughs he
made the character farcical. Beatrice Nichols
was. for some inscrutable reason, cast for the
character of the heroine; Myrtle Tannehill
contented herself with a minor part. Though
the former was always bright, her repartee
was unnecessarily shrewish, and she failed
to suggest the sweetness or the charm that
the part called for. With Myrtle Tannehill
as the heroine, the comedy might have a
better chance of success; for though her
part on Saturday was done in a way that
adds to this paper’s appreciation of her stage
ability, its contribution to the comedy was

The Goldin Age of illusion is ending at
Sydney . Tivoli. After Friday night the
smart citizen who has solved the high-cost-
of-living problem, explained the Holman
Government and seen right to the back of
Hughes’s mind will have to go elsewhere
for puzzles to blunt his intellectual teeth on.
The Tivoli survivors who will then keep the
orchestra from going home at five minutes
past eight may include Jack and Foris,
Floyd Mack and Mabelle, Baslo and Vivi,
Grace Hanson, Jimmy Fletcher and Os-ko-
mon. A glance at the managerial advt. will
show that these people are the most talented
performers that ever breathed on a press
agent’s ear.
This week at Sydney movie halls :—•
Lyceum: “The Primal Lure,” a story of. Canadian
pioneering days, including a spirited scrap with
the Indians; “The No Good Guy,” a comedy-drama
