“The Story of the Rosary” at Melbourne
Royal is still giving the hero, Frank
Harvey, a long run for his missus. When
the warrior escapes from custody as a
prisoner of war, he has to face a thousand
miles of hunger and wild animals to reach
Madge Fabian, his beauteous bride. Of
course, the. human phenomenon covers the
distance without trouble or mishap, and
arrives.home just in time to prevent the lady
dedicating her future to the service of the
Church. It is a big, passionate moment for
the dusty soldier when he interposes between
his wife and her final vows; but, on the
whole, his devotion appears to be a bit
overdone, for the object of it almost
seems to wish he hadn’t. However,
Miss Fabian has a painfully color-
less part, and she can therefore be pardoned
when she plays it in a mechanical, faraway
manner, and speaks the drivelsome dialogue
as if it were a dead language.
Peg, at Melbourne King’s, continues to
bring her bi’own unspeakable dawg into the
wrong room of the right house eight times a
week. Also she is making as many allusions
as before to the father who lives in New
York, and whose Heart she is the Peg of.
The best compliment one can pay to Sara
Allgood, in the name-part, is to remark that
her naturalness makes the audience think
quite seriously of the invisible wild Irish-
man she left in America. The actress does
more, than give realism to her own character.
She imparts realism to Peg’s supposititious
parent.

Ross and Wyse and the Rig Surprise is
the programmed description of a couple of
patterers and an acrobatic infant who com-
menced a season at Melbourne Bijou on
Saturday. Ross and Wyse may be dis-
missed as specialists in gags that are no
longer youthful, but the baby Surprise per-
forms balancing feats like a veteran. Estelle
Wordette and Co. are bickering wearily in
another bleak offering, “When a Cat’s
Away” ; and the Farrows are showing off in
a sketch entitled “The Bargain Hunter.”
Other new lights are Alf Holt, a comedian
and mimic, and Fenner and Sully, acrobatic
lunatics who are at their best in their lucid
intervals. The rest of the Bijou squad com-
prise Mendel and Nagel, the Three Saxons,
Ray Monde, Brown and Sorlie, and the
Robert Demont Trio.
The present programme at Melbourne
Tivoli is practically last week’s bill length-
ened by the addition of a frail skit on the
motion-picture industry. The alleged travesty
is entitled “How a Movie is Made,” but its
only noticeable feature is the inability of
three lady Tivolians to look like the film-
stars they are supposed to be. The droll
tongue of Miss Marie King-Scott is still con-
spicuous in the earlier part of the proceed-
ings, and the fiddling of Keith Kennedy con-
tinues to be agreeable to the musically-
inclined.

There seems to be no end to our loafing
on the baby. The Melbourne Hospital has
got £26,000 into debt; which means that
the old man isn’t even paying his doctors’
bills, but is passing them on now to the
unfortunate kid, along with most of the cost
of the war and other things.
Died in England, Frank P. Mahony, who
could paint a horse with as much sympathy
as anybody who ever handled a brush in
Australia, and with more power. He put
remarkable feeling into his animal studies:
witness “The Cry of the Mothers,” in the
Sydney Gallery. Mahony, born in Mel-
bourne 54 years ago, contributed very
largely to The Bulletin until he went to
London in the ’9o’s. He should never have
made that move. Neither the climate nor
the people suited his temperament; and his
output has been much less than it should
have been. He had arranged at last to
return; but chronic ill-health had gripped
him, and to the regret of this paper he died
before he could carry out his plan.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Thorold Waters,” from London:—
On the very day I was reading- some pleasantries
by “0.K.” in The Bulletin re the banishment of
“sauerkraut music” from London since the con-
flagration started, Fog critics were spreading- them-
selves with all the old-time gush because Beecham
had revived “Tristan” to a big public response. “It
recalled other and happier days,” quoth the Daily
Teleuhaph, tlie recognised musicians’ medium.
“The name of Wagner has not lost its magic in our
midst. The gratifying point is that even in such
times as the present our music-loving public, given
a good thing, are not disposed to turn their backs
upon it. Congratulations to all concerned”—includ-
ing Wagner and son Siegfried, of course. Also the
Times : “That the London public has gone for two
years without its Wagner is apparently only due to
lack of opportunity, not to any self-denying ordin-
ance or to revulsion from the Wagner fever.” Before
London got its share of the revival, Manchester had
been equally enthusiastic. Personally I would be
rather glad if Germany was wiped out to its last
man; but it is sheer rubbish to talk contemptuously
in these days about Wagner, Beethoven and co.,
simply because their latter-day compatriots have
lapsed into dinning another sort of music in our ears.
If it were not for Wagner, we would have quite a
different kind of Puccini, another Elgar altogether
and no Mascagni or Leoncavallo to speak of. He is
their fundamental, and all the screeching of “0.K.”
or any other person cannot alter that. Nor is it
right to try to make Australian music-lovers think
that England has abolished the German kind of
music. It tried to for a month or two, and now
there isn’t a decent concert in these somewhat desert
days in the Smoke that fails to give us the masters
of music, whatever their nationality.
The Orpheus Society in Adelaide has
found itself unable to decide whether to bar
all German music or not. Some members
are unwilling to sing it; others say that in
part songs for male voices—the special
object of the Orpheus’s existence—there is
very little available that had not a German
origin. The annual meeting decided to leave
the question over for the incoming com-
mittee. In England a compromise is now
being adopted: “Nothing later than
Brahms.”
“0.K.”: Cecil Brooking, of the “Peg o’
My Heart” Go., endorses all that Hugh
Buckler said about German treatment of
English mummers. Both of them were
members of Beerbohm Tree’s co. which went
to Berlin at the Kaiser’s irivitation. Brook-
ing speaks only of the arrogant attitude of
the Berlin press and public towards the
strangers who were presuming to play
Shakspeare before kultured critics. Buckler
spoke rather of the hoggish animosity
displayed by the stage-hands at the
theatre. The signs of animosity ranged
from petty annoyances to dangerous
“accidents.” Buckler himself had a
narrow escape from injury by a ham-
mer which fell from the “flies.” Unac-
countable negligence was shown in dropping
things and leaving things about where they
were likely to be tripped over. This was
when the co. was rehearsing prior to the
first appearance. Afterwards the stage-
hands must have received orders to make
themselves pleasant, or else they were
bumbled bv the splendor and completeness of
Tree’s production.

“Harrison O.”: “La Boheine,” by the Gon-
salez lightweights, is, dramatically, the most
convincing performance of the opera I have
seen. None of the principals is built on
Dreadnought lines, and it is quite possible
to believe that the Quartier quartette rarely
devours . anything more nourishing than
spaghetti and absinthe. Tenor Lois, as the
i’oet, is as real as Henry Lawson —a very
different Rudolf to the plump young soft-
goods assistant presented by McCormack, for
example. The other bohemians starve and
frivol more convincingly than usual, and are
able, to push across the footlights a real sug-
gestion of the world, the flesh and the garlic.
Miss Gonsalez is the first Mimi I have seen
who might have died of consumption; most
of the others have given the impression that
their complaint was dropsy.

(Continued on page 3.j.)
If the war lasts another seven years there
is a reasonable probability that some of the
present ingenious arrangements for wasting
money will be noticed by somebody. Then
an eye may be given to the tremendous ex-
penditure in fitting up troopships. Several
of them are used for carrying Australians
to Europe and also for bringing British
Tommies back to Egypt, India, etc. Under
the British regulations certain things are re-
quired and are provided at great expense in
England. Then the ship arrives in Aus-
tralia, and, the Australian regulations re-
quiring other things, the ship is- pulled to
pieces and the fittings which were put in at
the start of the voyage are pulled out. On
the other side the Australian fittings are
pulled out and the British regulations com-
plied with over again. So the dreadful ex-
penditure goes on. The bill will come in
presently.
“Personnel’s” story of a grievance:—
In “The Man Who Stayed at Home” the hero
coolly lights his pipe with the whits feathers the
girls send him and apparently is not hurt, because
he knows what they don’t, that he is doing his duty
to his country. I don’t know that the citizen soldier
or officer who stays at home can take things as
nonchalantly. He may have good reasons why he
has not gone to the Front, and if he is an officer
he cannot hold his commission unless those reasons
appear to the authorities to be very good. It may
be that he has volunteered and is using all the
influence he can to get to the Front, but is detained
because his services are required to mould the raw
material and impart discipline and drill.
I don’t think many people understand the tribula-
tions of “The Man Who Stayed at Home.” If he be
an officer in the citizen forces he is called up for
seivice; he must leave his business or profession
all . c \ that is less than he received as a
Militia Officer, and less than the pay of the Per-
manent Officer, though he is doing the same work
and very often doing it better. No matter what
his rank may be, no matter what high and onerous
position he may hold, he cannot be paid for his
services at a higher rate than that of a lieut.-colonel.
But worse case of all is that of the man who
has volunteered, is physically fit, and though strain-
ing every nerve to get away, is compelled to remain
to train reinforcements. It hurts him that he is
in command and is training the A.I.F. but is not
of them. His marking is “Home Service.” If he
gets an illness he is not treated in the same way
as the officer lie is training, who receives his pay
just the same. The other may not get pay, and if
lie do not get well he is turned adrift quickly.
liieie aie Militia Officers who have been training
units since the war began. They would have gone
long ago, but their services are too valuable, or
there are no vacancies for them. And they receive
white feathers, smaller pay, “no increments,” and,
what hurts most of all, are classed “Home Service.”
Does the Minister for Defence know these things?
He can very easily and quickly be possessed of the
facts, as given above.
But what can the Minister do to stop the
white feather foolishness—or worse? There
is only one cure for that. Universal service
conscription—call it what you please! ’
NOBLE YOUNG MAN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I wooed some years ago
Various ladies, Mr. Cupid,
Some were fast and some were slow,
But they all seemed “good,” I know,
To a noble youth and stupid,
Full of knightly rubbish that
Voung knights carry ’neath the hat.
When I think of fun I missed
With these willing maids and merry,
Careless girls I might have kissed
If I’d had no wowser twist,
I feel idiotic—very.
When I longed to be a rake,
Then the wowser would awake.
When I lingered in the dark,
Arms around my latest fancy,
Something grim—the Inward Nark !
Would, much like a house-dog, bark,
Warning me beware of Nancy.
Then flirtation would grow slow
Till she sighed, “It’s time to go!”
Heaven knows how many times
Girls despised me, Mr. Cupid,
For my sentimental crimes—■
Kissing feebly, writing rhymes.
Rhymes! Good lord, but that was stupid!
Those robust Australian misses
Wanted only hugs and kisses.
Fun and frolic! Rhymes are stupid.
M here have all those girls gone, Cupid?
Girls with whom I might have tarried?....
Cupid answers, “Fat and married!”
N.S.W.

“Mac” : The truest bit of real Australian I
struck after I got to Gallipoli was the day
.lira was knocked out. Rill and .Tim had been
mates all through—and then, in a hot, ex-
cited half-hour, Jim delayed a bullet. Bill
sprang to his side, held him in Ins arms, and
then with a “By God!” he was over the
parapet, his hat was flung down, and he was
screaming: “Come on, drop your guns
anv one of you s. Let’s have it out li
men!” And the horror of it was that poor
Bill fell riddled with bullets.
From Billjim, M.D., in 1 ranee . -
And I got the gum-leaves and the wattle-blossoir.
and took them into the wards; and you should have
seen how the tiny scraps were scrambled for! Soon
all the boys had the dried leaves to their noses try-
ing to get a sniff out of them! “That s what
Heaven smells like, sister,” one said. We want a
little of that, and we want it for this coming winter.
Seems a small thing, but it will be a big thing toi
old Billjim Wallaby when he comes out of some
filthy Flanders trench, and it will be a bigger thing
still in the hospitals. I’m not worrying a bit about
how B.W. will stand the Bodies; but I’m worrjing
a lot about the climate, and anything you can do to
help them through is worth while.
“Nick Bottom”: Whose are the bright
brains that compose the text-books for N. S.
Wales schools? One publication avers that
“Ocean steamers lie just inside the heads
| Tweed] while small river craft ply to Mur-
willumbah, a distance of 20 miles up the
river.” As a matter of fact, steamers that
can jump over the Tweed bar can (and the
majority of them do) discharge their cargo
at Murwillumbah. In my establishment for
the manufacture of good Australian citizens
there is a map of N. S. Wales that teems
with errors. Towns that should stand in one
place have lost their way and wandered
to another, and centipedes that are marked
this or that range crawl across a landscape
that is, in reality, as bare of mountains as a
frog is of wool. Several of the atlases sup-
plied by the Department show the Hunter
River prancing into Port Stephens, the New
England range crawling drunkenly into the
bed which had evidently been vacated by its
North Coast neighbor, and Ben Lomond and
jMt. Lindsay visiting each other. But when,
on turning up Sydney in Arthur Mee’s
Children’s Encyclopedia one finds it
standing on Botany Bay, the light of faith in
“text-books” is blown out by a great gust.
“Y.Z.”: In Melbourne the idea of sex
education in State schools, as a means of
preparing youth, and advising it that syphilis
is more to be avoided than the red devil in
any other shape, has been knocked emphati-
cally on the head by those shrinking souls
who argue that any such teaching must serve
to brush the bloom from the cheek of inno-
cence and start youngsters discussing sex
matters in play-hours. But already school-
children do discuss sex matters in the school-
ground and elsewhere. Practically every
teacher meets with constantly-recurring
demonstrations of juvenile precocity in this
direction. As things are, the children’s little
knowledge is the worst knowledge. Igno-
rance and mystery add to its baseness. The
efforts to hide away every vestige of reality
from them gives the thing a fearful
piquancy. Suggestiveness is everywhere,
truth nowhere, and wonderment, one of the
most powerful factors in the mind of a child,
works all for the worst. Teach the children
enough to rob the subject of its appealing
mystery, and children will think cleanly
about it when they think at all. Keep a
dark, locked room in your house, and you
will expect your children to be there con-
tinually, with their noses at the cracks. If
you expect otherwise, you are a fool.
“Myola”; Come to the holy man’s house
—not the one he lives in, but the one he
rents to some hard-pressed worker. The
free kindergarten teacher discovered it only
when she took home one of the poor little
mites who had coughed herself to exhaus-
tion. The child led her down various
depressing streets to a musty, kennel-like
hovel, situated below the level of a blind
alley, which was practically a drain leading
into a lane. The refuge consisted of two
rooms and a household of God knows how
many. Fumbling endeavors had been made
by the tenants to keep out the rain with
tops of kerosene tins. Pluddled in the
miasma inside were two elder children cov-
ered with “damp” sores. One of them was
almost blind. The wretched tenants didn’t
know who their landlord was. “No, we
never see him ; the agent collects the rent
every weelc and won’t do any repairs.”
Later inquiries proved that the rathole was
owned by a parson—the very one who visits
the kindergarten and buzzes to the coughing
youngsters about God!
Though the proposed Victorian legitimi-
sation of the “war baby” will be a beneficent
reform as far as it goes, most of the talk on
the subject is blather and foolishness. There
has long been a big military camp at Gee-
long ; but in the recent report of the presi-
dent of the Female Refuge in the neighbor-
hood it was stated that “for the first time in
its history the home was without inmates
early in the year, and laundry work had to
be discontinued.” So much for the cackle
concerning Billjim’s immorals.
NOT CURED YET.
HE: “I hare been on the stage for seven years."
SHE : “And you still long to be an actor?”
The notion that if both parties to a
marriage desire a divorce good and suffi-
c-icnt reason has been shown for keeping
them chained received a jolt in Victoria the
other afternoon. Franz Otto Schieblich
(three cheers for the red, white and blut-
wurst) applied to be unhitched from his
wife, Irene, on the ground of her giddiness
with the co-re.; while Irene brought a
counter petition alleging undue friendship
on the part of her hubby for a serving-
wench, and constructive desertion. After the
case had provided a couple of days’ diver-
sion from war news, counsel announced that
both sides were willing to withdraw the
aspersions they had cast on one another’s
fidelity if ’s Honor would agree to a
divorce on the constructive desertion side-
issue. The Court put up a pretence of
looking shocked, and then adjusted its horse-
hair and remarked that it seemed a dashed
sensible suggestion.
It isn’t often that the Speck’s Governor,
Ellison Macartney, abandons the unruffled
demeanor that usually goes with a cock-hat;
but the other day he landed on the Red
Cross in Hobart at a startling rate of speed.
The society has collected some £lO,OOO, but
the controllers are apparently keeping it as
a monument to their ineptitude. Anyhow,
nothing has been done, and sick and wounded
men back after doing their bit have to hang
round the town while waiting for their
recovery and discharge, or go into the mili-
tary details camps. In either case they
suffer. His Ex. pointed out that if the
Red Cross dallied much longer it would find
the scrap over and a big lump of somebody
else’s money on its hands. Hustled from
this unexpected quarter the committee pro-
posed to put up a “rest-house” somewhere in
the bee-utiful country, where T. Anzac could
watch the leaves shoot and hear the birds
twitter. But the Gov. headed them off,
declaring that the men would sooner be close
to where they could hear the musical chirp
of their old cobbers. The there-
upon got hold of a building down Wapping
way, where the loafers and prostitutes in
the town gather. A meeting of khaki made
a hearty protest, but the kick came too late.

-—-and of course assistance was immediately

The Paramount Picture of the Week.
The High Court has given “the Crown'
leave to appeal to that Court against the
decision of the Supreme Court of South
Aus., which quashed the conviction ot
Francis Snow, the metal-buyer, for doing
something which somebody thought was
technically “trading with the enemy” within
the meaning of the Act which nobody quite
understands. So now the whole weary busi-
ness will begin over again on a fresh stage;
and who will be the better for it in _ the
long run Heaven only knows. There might
be some sense in the proceedings if Snow
was still in business and still under sus-
picion. But he isn’t in business, and he
isn’t under suspicion; and the proceedings
are. not much short of a criminal waste of
money.

“UTU!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the Maoriland House, on the second anniversary
of the war, Dr. Pomare, representing: the native race,
in supporting a motion expressing Parliament’s in-
flexible determination to continue the war to a
successful issue, said: “To-day /Egean breezes stir
the grasses over the graves of mine and yours, and
wherever a Maori hears the moan of the wind,
whenever he hears the boom of the guns, it reminds
him that away beyond the seas Utu (revenge) has
got to be brought about for the dead.... The spirit
of Rewi.... lives in the Maori to-day, and I cry
in the same spirit of defiance, ‘Alee! Ake! Ake!’
(For ever and ever and ever!).”
When across the tussock-grass,
Whispering, the great winds pass,
What the message that they bring,
What the mournful dirge they sing?
To the Maori soul they speak
Of jEgean hillsides bleak.
O’er our warriors lying lone
Faint /Ugean breezes moan;
Bitterly do they upbraid:
“For our chiefs who proudly died,
Of their spirit-home denied,
For their graves unsanctified
Utu, utu is not made!”
When the steady ocean breeze
Stirs the spirits of our trees,
Angrily our gods awake:
“Why do you not vengeance take?
Never shall we know relief
Till that evil, skulking Chief
From Te Reinga’s Rock is hurled
Howling to the Under-world !
Swear that blood-debt shall be paid!
Life for life our chieftains’ code;
So to pay that debt long owed,
Send him on Te Reinga’s Road !
Utu, utu must be made!”
Though beside those alien waves
Tapu. are our warriors’ graves,
Still uneasy lie our dead,
Waiting till that soul is sped.
Still the Maori, chiefs among
Lives old Rewi, he who flung
His immortal battle-cry.
“Ake! Ake!” we reply,
“Vengeance comes, not long delayed.
In one tribe, the brown and white,
We to victory will fight.
Gods and warriors, all is right!
Maori utu shall be made!”
X.SAV.

"The Snake”: The S. Australian Indus-
trial Court has been bothered again about the
cost of living; and my friend little Dr.
Arthur, who adorns the N. S. Wales Assem-
bly, has prepared a “statement” for the
Court’s edification. As the newspaper im-
ports explained, it was “what he considered to
be the reasonable minimuni diet for a family
of a man, wife and three children,” and it
runs out at 295. 7d. per week. Mr.
Parsons, for the employers, said that he
would adduce medical evidence to prove that
a family could live and keep in good health
on less. Quite right. But a grand old
rich Tory legislator in Maoriland knew
more about it. He explained some years ago
that when he was a young man bran and
molasses were considered to be good food for
a wor-r-king man. And, of course, there
was the French gentleman who suggested
grass. Unfortunately lie came to an abrupt
end on a Paris lamp-post. But, anyhow, who
are these cattle—male, dam and three calves
-—that £1 9s. 7d. should be wasted weekly on
their fodder?

“Billy Blue”: Those eight old church
bells at Bt. Philip’s, Sydney, referred to by
“Alpha” (B. 27/7/TO), were not presented
to the church by George 111. in 1807.
Hunter brought them out with him on the
Reliance, which arrived on September 7,
1795. There is no record to show that they
were presented by anyone. There being no
church in Sydney when they _ arrived, they
were stowed away till one was built. Hunter
got to work on the church, but it was un-
finished when lie loft in 1800, so King took
a hand at the job. It was still only going
up when lie left in 1800. Then Bligh put a
finish to the work. He wrote in a solemn
despatch on October 31, 1807 :
The town of Sydney is much improved, but the
, taking more time to complete than was
expected, will not be ready for Divine Service until
von! ( vr UlS \ ma f' PiSht bells were first rung on the
29th May last, which although too small, give a
cheeifulness to the inhabitants and command attem
tion on the Sabbath Day.
One Wade was the bellringer. I don’t think
his other names were Charles Gregory.

lint Pot”: Bedford twice blundered in
his fine yarn, “Fifty Pongs of Honan” (B_
•VS/T6), with regard to the orders his hero
gives the helmsman. “Guthrie saw the-
whale-backed rock near, and the ship falling
away towards it and to starboard. ‘Port a
little,’ he said.” But as the rock was evi-
dently on the starboard bow, porting the
lie]m would bring the ship towards it, and
at this time he wanted to avoid it. Then
Guthrie changes his mind and decides to
sink the ship. “Starboard your helm,” he
cries. Now, starboarding the helm would
cant the ship’s head to port, and avoid the
rock, but Bedford makes her run full on
the reef as the deliberate result of that
order, lie errs in good company: I have
seen big Admiralty barristers get horribly
tangled over this perverse idiom of English-
speaking seafarers, whereby when you want
a vessel to go to port you order “star-
board, and dee versa. Before steering-
wheels were invented the idiom had some
sense in it, for as tiller and rudder worked
opposite ways, the tiller had to be pulled to
starboard in order to make the rudder
swing to port (or “larboard,” as it used to
be called). Sailormen of all other nations, so
jar as I know, have brought their speech
into line with modern inventions, and order
“starboard” only when they want their
vessel’s head to swing that way ; but British
and Yankee shellbacks cling tenaciously to
the old terms, and with them “starboard
your helm” means “alter course to port.”
A few years ago a British pilot who boarded
a £ ranch ship to bring her into an English
port lost her through the same old confu-
sion. He thought he knew French, and to
show, his knowledge of the lingo, instead of
showing the helmsman by gesture which
way he wanted the wheel turned, he sang
out iribord” (starboard). Of course, he
reckoned the Frenchman at the wheel would

turn the ship to port, as any English-speak-
ing sailor would on receiving the order
starboard”; but the foreigner, knowing-
nothing of contrary English customs, and
being used to a language which says what
it; means, followed the order literally and
steered the ship to starboard, running her
ou a.reef which ripped the bottom out of her.
SCHEDULED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Russian time-table was observed to the
minute.’’
We moved at three-fifteen a.m.
At four we made our first attack.
At half-past five they tried to stem
Our even march and drive us back.
At six we yielded half a yard,
At twenty past we slew a score,
Then breathed for fourteen seconds hard,
• And bayoneted eighty more.
Tlie town was fixed to fall at eight.
We took it on the earliest stroke;
Then, marking- time, we had to wait
Till at the fords the foe we broke.
A Cossack regiment, halted near,
M as fixed to charge at ten to nine;
At ten-fifteen we had to cheer
And smash the German’s second line.
And so the rigid game goes on,
Clayed to the clock with might and skill.
At twelve their chief redoubt is gone :
At one we take the nineteenth hill.
At three we take three dozen guns,
While eighteen hundred prisoners yield;
At .half-past four ten thousand Huns
Are left for dead upon the field.
On Friday morn at ten-thirteen
We seize Plavitski from the south ;
On Thursday week we shall be seen
Facing their cannon mouth to mouth
One mile and fifty-seven feet
From Skitzki junction on the Skatz,
Where we shall make the foe retreat
At twelve-fifteen across the fiats.
September Ist sees Lemberg’s fall;
October 3rd seals Warsaw’s fate;
And three days later we shall call’
With shrapnel cards at Cracow’s gate.
Steady as death and sure as doom
We move on schedule time to win—
On April Ist our guns shall boom
At B.IC around Berlin!
N.S.W.

For the title of oldest Australian-born
inhabitant the Early Pioneers’ Association
of Melbourne has unearthed three claimants.
William Ross, of Singleton, was born in the
convict ship Red Rover seven miles outside
Sydney Heads in June, ISIU. An old lady
of Dubbo, Mrs. Cross, says she is entitled
to the honor, having been born on land in
1819. The third competitor is George Tuck-
well, of Parramatta, who also come to light
in 1819.
Oldest Supreme Court Judges in Aus-
tralia by length of service: Pope Cooper
(Chief Justice of Queensland), appointed
to the Bench in 1883; Justice a’Beckett
(Vic.), appointed in lSSti (he will be 80
on August 31 next) ; Justice Chubb (Q.)
and justice Hodges (Vic.), appointed in
1889; Justice Hood ( Vic.) and Justice Real
(Q.), appointed in 1890. Chief Justice
Madden and the great Griffith were elevated
to the Bench in 1893.
HEAVEN UP-TO-DATE, —No. 1.
THE RECORDING ANGEL: “I'm at the end of
my feathers. I must buy a fountain
pen.”
Lord Clifford, the Hobart man who
dropped in for the Chudleigh -estates and a
seat in the House of Lords the other day,
lias had plenty of “colonial experience.” He
has been hemp worker (in Maoriland),
farmer, milk-contractor, politician (also
ran), canvasser for photos, insurance agent,
land agent, essayist and carpenter. Lately
he has been hustling houses together and
was engaged bn a job at Moonah when the
news of his windfall came through. He
was once turned down by the electors as
not good enough for the House of Reps.,
but by virtue of Red Tape and Tradition
he has now an unquestioned right to govern
England as a member of the House of
Lords.
H. A. Parsons, the Adelaide lawyer who
was prepared to show that a family of five
people needn’t spend 295. 7d. a week on food,
is the son of an ex-parson who took to
politics and became Government Resident
of the Northern Territory. Dad married
into the very wealthy Angus family, and the
son, who is “Paddy”. Glynn’s brilliant law-
partner, got his purse lined that way. Then
he married a daughter of Langdon Bony-
thon, who regularly makes more than 295.
7d. a week out of the Advertiser. So one
way and another the young man probably
feels that he won’t want in his old age
even if he buys an extra pound of treacle
and thereby carries the housekeeping bills
np to 295. 10 Id. a week.
Dean Talbot of Sydney was either made
or ruined at Gallipoli. Speaking at Parra-
matta the other night, he told some staring
churchgoers how he had been lately seized
by two joyful old Anzac mates, who gripped
him by the arms and, yarning boisterously,
steered him straight and with the best in-
tentions towards the nearest pub. They
stormed the place and one of them was about
to give the girl the order when the Dean
remembered he was the officer, and that the
officer did the shouting.

brigadier-general.

Among the Australians wounded in the
recent argument with Abdul at Ivatia was
Major Windeyer, youngest son of The
Bulletin’s old friend the Judge. He was
a private in the Boer row and is a major
now.
The Scotchest document ever framed in
Maoriland is the list of donations recently
forwarded to the Naval Relief Fund by the
residents of Waipu. Of 101 contributors
78 were Macs. Mac Kay was present 25
times; McLean lined up 14 deep; there
were six MacKenzies. and half a dozen
McLennans; also five of the McLeod. The
cheque which accompanied the list was
signed by Geordie Mac Kay. countersigned

Logged by death at Stewart Island (M.L.)
Captain Robert Scollay, a Shetland Islander
who arrived in Melbourne in 185!) as an
officer of an immigrant ship. Two years
later he took the first sawmill plant to
Stewart Island, and started to carve up the
big timber areas of, Rakiura. He was an
expert shipbuilder, and amongst the craft he
slid off his stocks were the schooner Mar-
garet Scollay, the Endeavor (foundered at
South Pegasus), the Eclipse (wrecked at
Stewart Island), the Lerwick, the Jane Scol-
lay. the Dauntless and the Enterprise, with
which Captain Catling twice attempted to
recover the gold from the General Grant at
the Auckland Islands. Scollay was 88 when
he was entered up.
The Atkinson will case, in which the
children of the old Bendigo doctor tried to
show that the very last All's. _ Atkinson Was
too well treated, was the juiciest peach the
Melbourne Bar has sucked since it got its
hand into the Weingarten corset case. The
monotony lasted 38 days and the costs in
sight amount to £14,000. The widow has
to pay her own.
This is the way of it. Sydney Maxted,
member of a family which lias had a lot to
do with the N.S.W. Charities Department,
couldn’t get a commission as chaplain with
the A.1.F., so he enlisted, and as long as he
was out to kill no harm befel him. Then,
at Gallipoli, he was put on to the piloting
(he had been the Anglican “priest” at
Haberfield, near Sydney), and following the
six stars of Australia to France had hardly
got to his work of carrying comfort when
he was reported killed.
Gordon Webber, Labor ALL.A. for Abbots-
ford (Vic.), was a tender blossom when first
he entered the House; but he has hardened
very rapidly. In making a few remarks
recently on the Address-in-Reply, he trickled
on for more than four hours. This sort of
thing may sometimes be done from a sense
of duty, but in the case of Webber, as dry
as dust and with a very limited vocabulary
and no special knowledge, his painful effort
was too obvious a struggle after publicity
at all costs —even though the country should
have to pay every penny of 'em.
When a memorial to Lieutenant
Cradick, of Lonesome Pine, was
being unveiled at St. James's
Church, Morpeth (N.S.W.), the
other day, Archdeacon Regg, who
looks after the local collection
plate, had something to say a*bout
the origin of the Morpeth taber-
nacle. While Wellington was
pushing Bonaparte out of Spain,
the color-sergeant of one of the
British regiments was shot in
action. Four men rushed to save
the colors, but a shell landed
among them. All dropped flat to
avoid the explosion, but three of
the four were killed by it. The
fourth, Lieutenant Close, who was
unhurt, vowed that he would build
a church in memory of his escape.
After the war he came to live in
the Hunter River district, and St.
James’s, Morpeth, is the church
that he built in fulfilment of his
vow.
W. J. Johnston, killed in France,
was many years a battler in Labor
politics before he put on putties,
lie had a seat in the House of
Heps, in the first reign of Fisher;
but at the next elections he was
thrown out by W. M. Fleming.
Then he tilted at Calar<-. and was
beaten again. Next he made a bid
for Castlereagh in the X.S.W. Par-
liament. but Arkins got the call.
A straight-hitting Laborite, he
deserved better luck than he mostly
had.

TO AN ANZAC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tlie Berlin Tageblatt’s war-correspondent says of
the Anzacs: “We don't like their faces and their
eyes. They are not the kind of people that appeal
to us. They are good marksmen, cruel fighters, and
hard as steel.” •
Though you’re a cruel fighter,
Especially attacking,
And quite a willing smit’er,
To me there’s something lacking
In your grim Australian phiz:
I don’t know what it is.
Some blemish in your grace
Analysis defies:
I do not like your face,
1 do not like your eyes.
Your moniker offends me —-
It's shockingly ungerman ;
What into frenzy sends me
I really can’t determine.
When anger fills your soul
Your eyes you do hot roll,
You don’t, like us, grimace,
Your passions you disguise;
I do not like your face,
I do not like your eyes.
You have no use for Ivultur,
Your language is laconic,
No wonder I insult your
Lean visage un-teutonic !
For you I do not {are:
You always fight so fair.
Your look shows pride of race,
Your calmness horrifies:
I do not like your face,
I dp not like your eyes.
Somehow I can't adore you:
Some barrier interposes;
And yet I would implore you
To change your Anzac noses.
When hurling bombs at us
You're quite an ugly cuss;
And when towards us you race
Your features I despise ;
I do not like your face,
I do not like your cues.
And in the charge, and after,
And even when we’re winning,
Your eyes are lit with laughter,
Your lean lips always grinning!
Your eyes are keen, and yet
Behind a bayonet
No beauties can 1 trace
That cultured Germans prize:
I do not like your face,
I do not like those eyes !
N.S.W.

AUSTPAL Pyjamas for Men.
AH siges are properly proportioned
and Have Comfort in their Fittings.
However good the fabrics and workmanship in
their make-up, a Pyjama Suit will fail to satisfy
the average man unless it fits comfortably.
By this is not meant merely that the Pyjamas
need to be large enough to ensure freedom of
movement lor the wearer. On the contrary, it is
quite certain that a good-fitting Pyjama Suit
depends as much on the actual cut which fashions
the garment as on the proportions to which the
many stock sizes are made.
.And this specialised of what men need,
applied in the out-turn of “Austral” Pyjamas,
ensures not only better value at any and every
quoted price, but the certainty of lasting comfort
and longer and better wear.
Customers who order by post will need only to state
Chest and Waist measures and Height to ensure
satisfactory fitting garments being sent to their order.

attractive lines in Men’s Pyjamas and Wearing Apparel,
will be sent post free to any address on request.

Robert Harper and Co., Ltd. (Melb.),
the rice, starch, oatmeal, etc., people ; deficit

, Dividends.- — N In

8 p.c. 6 p.c.
1916.. 42,349 8400 19,200 4961
Voted to Employees’ Profit r 4253 63,532
Sharing Fund, £lO,OOO. J
It is just 50 years since this business was
born; hence tiie gift of £lO,OOO to an em-
ployees’ profit sharing fund. The directors
—who are all Harpers—“are of opinion
that the best way to avoid industrial unrest
and secure contentment and efficiency will
be to adopt, if practicable, a wisely con-
structed and equitable system of commercial
and industrial partnership between the co.
and its employees.” The articles provide
for a profit-sharing scheme, which _ has been
in existence for some years, and it is pro-
posed to extend this. “A plan is now under
consideration. .. .Failing success in this
direction, some other means of securing
benefits for the employees will be devised.”
In ordinary circumstances it might be ques-
tioned whether vendors should not make pro-
vision for old employees. The services
of these people have aided in build-
ing up the goodwill of the business;
and to charge a co. first for good-
will and then to provide for them out of the
new co.’s profits, looks like making share-
holders pay twice over for the same thing.
But in this case it doesn’t matter much,
because the public took the 7 p.c. cumula-
tive pref. shares, while the vendors took the
ordinaries in payment for the undertaking;
so the £lO,OOO is money which the original
proprietors might divide among themselves
—assuming that they still hold the shares.
The average annual profits of the busi-
ness, before providing for interest on bor-
rowed money, were £32,874 for the 9i years
prior to the flotation of the co.; and as
mortgages, deposits and overdrafts totalled
over £130,000 at the end of the first year,
after £120,000 of pref. capital had been sub-
scribed, it looks as though the borrowed
money in the business may have previously
exceeded £200,000. The interest on that
lot would reduce the profits well below the
average shown by the present co.; so the
subscribers for the pref. shares have nothing
to complain about. In any case their divi-
dend calls for only £B4OO a year; and there
is a very big margin between that and the
profits. As for their principal, there come
in ahead of them creditors for about
£192.000; but the assets include freeholds
£116,318, stock £146,902, and book debts
£146,281. in addition to plant, etc. (£92,000)
and sundries. The 7 p.c. prefs. in this co.
are therefore as gilt-edged as an interest in
a trading concern well can be.
The table shows' £3955 lately written off.
The P. and L. balance from 1915, after pro-
viding for dividend appropriations, was
£4563, which was transferred to reserves;
and £3955 of this has disappeared—-probably
written off freeholds, etc. On the other hand
this year’s P. and L. account shows a sur-
plus of £41.429; but as £920 has been
written off goodwill and trade marks, that
amount has been added to arrive at the
profits in the table. . Maybe part of this
£3955 was used to write down the goodwill
item; in which case the profit should be
shown in the table as £41,429 instead of
£42.349. But it isn’t of much consequence,
anyhow.

J. C. Ludowigi AND Son, Ltd., leather
manufacturers, etc., Sydney :
To Re- Re-
Profits. Dividends. serves, serves.

.Tune. 1913.. 3565 10 „ „ 2832 733 7794
Dec., 1913.. 2127 8 „ ~ 2266 7655
June, 1914.. 1719 7 „ „ 1982 7392
Dec., 1914.. 1851 7 „ „ 1982 7261
June, 1915.. 2023 7 „ „ 1982 41 7302
Dec., 1915.. 2399 7 „„ 1982 417 7719
June, 1916.. 2243 10 „ „ 2832 7130
This old-established co. didn’t interest the
investor much until two or three years ago;
and then, when there were some dealings in
the shares, those who made inquiries found
that the management wasn’t a happy family.
Changes in the Board occurred every few
months; and evidently there were two tac-
tions. Recently there appears, to have been
a cleaning up ; and the Ludowici interest is
again paramount. A paragraph in the direc-
tors’ last report suggests that the contest
was very willing :—-
It should be gratifying to shareholders to learn that
the disorganising element, which had until February
last exercised such a disturbing influence, and sought
to obtain control, has been entirely eliminated Horn
the directorate and management. The change has
enabled the members of the staff to concentrate
undivided attention on their respective duties, in tne
performance of which they have been greatly ham-
pered. The result has been increased efficiency ancl
more satisfactory returns..
From which it will be gathered that party
government isn’t reckoned a good thing m
the leather trade, even though the country
does cling to it in the conduct of its own
affairs.
The capital is £56.644; and as it isn’t a
business in which extensive plant is re-
quired, this permits the co. to carry a stock
of up to £40,000 without going into debt at
all. At present the stock is shorter than
usual—£33,92o—but the co. has £5070 in
cash. Other assets are freehold and build-
ings, £16.825; book debts, £8033; and plant
and sundries, £3893. The profit and every-
thing else thus depends very largely upon the
stocktaking; and that can’t be checked.
However, the directors say that they have
“taken every reasonable precaution to pro-
vide against contingencies”; indeed., unless
they feel that the history of the year is better
than one would judge from the declared profit,
the increased dividend was not justified. The
co. has been full of complaints for . a good
many years past regarding high prices for
hides and keen competition for leather; and,
though hides are not going to be given away
for a long while to come, it seems likely that
there will be an increase in the Australian
tanning business. It has been a disgrace
to the Commonwealth so far. Go back to
1913, the last year in which imports and
exports were normal. In that year only
2892 people were employed in our tanneries,
as against 3044 five years previously.. Mean-
time the exports of raw hides to Belgium (and
we know what that means) and Germany
had increased from £132,257 to £775,232.
And to add to the shrieking insanity of it
we had in those years exported nearly half
a million pounds’ worth of tanning barks,
half of the total going to the German !

The Austral Hat Mills, Ltd. (Mel-
bourne), which began with a deficit of £273,
representing preliminary exes.: —

That is more like things should be. But it
is no use accepting the present figures of
any of these manufacturing cos. as normal.
War and high freights are giving the indus-
tries a Protection which they have never
had before; and on top of that a good many
of them have Government contracts at non-
competitive prices. Hence this paper
regards the year’s figures as probably having
no significance. What can happen to a co.
like this the 1914-15 experience showed. And
what will happen in the future depends not
only upon - the tariff but upon the spirit of
the operatives. In this particular line the
manufacturer has hitherto done pretty well
from the tariff point of view. Frank Tudor
has been Minister of Customs in four out
of the five Labor Governments; and it has
not been any disadvantage to the trade to
have had at the head of the tariff depart-
ment a man who went from felt-hat making
to politics. Anyhow, generally speaking the
duties have been sufficient to keep a very
large part of the trade; and no doubt a
still larger part could be collared if the
felt hatters allowed more female and
unskilled or partially-skilled male labor in
Australian factories. Some day perhaps
they will recognise that the employment of
this more or less unskilled labor does not
reduce the quantity of skilled labor called
for, but properly regulated is likely to
greatly increase it.
The co.’s capital is £30,000, and none of
this is represented by goodwill or other hypo-
thetical assets. Plant isn’t a very big item
in a hat-manufacturing concern; and that’s
a good thing if only because the
co thereby escapes a heavy annual
charge for wear and tear. Freehold
premises and plant run to only £11,760.
Further, because trade is of
principally with wholesalers, there is very
little money on the books —under £3500. So
it comes about that, on a capital of £30,000.
the co. can carry £20,962 of stock and still
have liabilities of only £630. There are not
many manufacturing industries that are so
well placed.

In order, no doubt, that the co.’s year might
synchronise with the income tax people’s
year, it was decided six months ago to shift
the balance along to June; hence the present
accounts are for six months only. Also,
probably with an eye on the taxation people,
the accounts are presented in a different
form. The directors say that revenue was
only £l6Bl less than in the first half of 1915.
But things didn’t liven up at Broken Hill
till well on in 1915; so that the revenue
for the nast year was only £57,219 as against
£132,145 for the previous full year. On
the other hand the half-year’s exes, were
£34,316 as against £63,949 in the preceding
12 months. That is to say, exes, amounted
to 60 p.c. of the earnings in the past half-
year, as against under 49 p.c. in 1915.
Apparently the co. has not been able to
adjust freights to meet various rises which
it has itself had to pay.
Still, sympathy would be wasted on the
Silverton * Tramway Co. No concern in
Australia has had more wonderful luck. It
got its concession 30 years ago to build a
light line carrying the S. Aus. system at
Cockburn on to the new mining rush at
Silverton and incidentally on to Broken
Hill, which then had possibilities and no
more. Silverton long ago was given over to
goats; Broken Hill continues to flourish.
The only capital ever actually put up was
the £46,800 mentioned above; but promoters’
and bonus shares have carried the total
to £250.000, and even on that the dividend
has averaged 40 p.c. for several years past.
And, when the export of zinc concentrates
is resumed, 40 p.c. will probably be earned
again—until the co. is hit by some form of
competition. At present the N.S.W. Gov-
ernment is building a line from Condobolin
to Broken Hill. This will connect the big
mining field with Sydney and the Western
(Dithgow) coalfields; and it is probable that
there will be some ore traffic over that line.
But the more serious competition to be
feared is from a Government line to con-
nect Broken Hill and Cockburn (S.A.). It
has often been talked about; but hitherto
influences have always been strong enough
to choke it off on the ground that, if the
State comes in. it ought to take over the
line on the terms of the concession; and
that would mean paying 21 times the aver-
age profit of the preceding seven years—
which would be absurd. One way and
another the Silverton Tramway Co. has had
a lot more luck than the Broken Hill Water
Supply. But investors would be foolish to
rely upon it lasting for ever.

The Australian Drug Co. (Sydney) :
June. Profits. Dividends. To Reserves. Reserves.

1912.. 3419 5 „„ 3750 9,921

1914.. 8875 TO „„ 7500 1375 12,368
1915.. 8921 10 „„ 7500 1421 13,789
1916.. 9885 10 ~,, 7500 2385 16,174
Just before Germany thought the Day had
arrived, this co. had stocks valued at £75,111.
Immediately war began the market for
chemicals was in a state of ferment;
for the painstaking Hun had got hold
of a vast propbrtion of the chemical
trade of the world. The average
increase in prices was therefore greater in
this business than in perhaps any other; and
a co. with £75,000 in stock and some more,
no doubt, on the water and contracted for
from France and England was in a particu-
larly happy position. Naturally larger
profits are shown for the past couple of
years. But “stocks are valued at cost or
under”; and, though that is a stereotyped
phrase, it indicates where the management
may have stuffed away some further profits
that it wasn’t advisable to show. And it is
a very prudent thing to thus provide for
contingencies. The stock now is valued at
£74,580, and a large part of that must have
been bought since the war, and at prevailing
high prices. Whether the co. will be able
to clear it at high prices, too, depends upon
various things, including the length of the
war. But, in any case, and whenever the
big trouble ends, the co. will have a stock
in hand that has been purchased at war
prices; and it is quite possible that many
chemicals will then see a quick fall. The
co is more than justified in providing for
this contingency out of the sweet profits it
must have made since the war started. In
addition, the management seems to have
written down freehold, plant and fixtures
liberally : they stood at £20,061 in 1914 and
are down to £17,920 now.
Share capital is £75,000. Two years ago
the co. owed £49,570; the total is now
£58,855, including £25,000 of debentures.
Capital, liabilities and reserves (before pay-
ing the dividend) amounted to £157,722,
represented by stock £74,580, freehold and
plant £17,920, book debts £56,576, and cash
and sundries £8646. Book debts are
approximately £lO,OOO more than they were
two years ago; and that, in the circum-
stances, seems to suggest an increased busi-
ness. Hitherto the co. has had a very small
Board of three, including managing-director
A. L. Silly, who put the co. on its present
footing. But all the drugs in the warehouse
can’t make the managing director as physi-
cally fit as he is mentally capable, and Albert
A. Kemp, who has been in the business for
many years, has therefore been added to the
Board.

Taxes, especially on income, have bitten
deeper into the profits during the past couple
of years. Before making the appropriations
for this purpose the net income lias been:
1914, £5195 ; 1915, £4746; 1916, £4646. Still,
even disregarding the charges for taxation,
the movement hasn’t lately been in the right
direction. Trustee cos. have been liable to
be hit three ways at once: they have had to
pay the increased taxes on their own profits;
the reduced value of their clients’ estates is
likely to bring them in less corpus commis-
sion; and the new taxes give more work in
the management of the trusts. But, after
allowing for all that, this like most trustee
cos. can’t complain : a co. which can. under
adverse conditions, pay a 10 p.c. dividend
and still put by nearly a third of the profit,
while submitting its principal to very little
risk, has obviously struck a financial patch.
And under normal conditions the trend of
profits is upwards, because the advantages
offered by an agency of tms kind are becom-
ing more widely known. In 1906 the trust
estates in hand were valued at only
£1,408,236; to-day they stand at £3,617,393.
In these 10 years the increasing profits have
allowed the dividend to creep up from 8 p.c.
to 10 p.c.; at the same time nearly £15,000
has been put by —an amount almost equal to
three-fifths of the capital. And, as before
remarked, very little risk is taken. The
funds don’t have to be chanced in plant or
stock : £20.550 is in Government securities,
and £22,500 net is in city freehold (office
premises, safe deposit, etc.) and the small
balance is in cash and mortgages. Cer-
tainly the co. may make losses through
negligently misreading a will or doing some-
thing of that sort; but that isn’t a serious
risk. A more real one is that the State may
push into the business as in N. S. Wales,
and cut rates for trustee and agency work.

From that £943 allowances have to be de-
ducted (1) for depreciation of furniture and
(2) for the fact that the co.’s lease is six
months nearer its end : it expires in 14 or
15 years from now. The co. may have to
quit then, and if so it will presumably go
into liquidation ; hence part of its job is to
accumulate enough profit to repay capital,
which now stands at £12,500. In the_ past
four years it has put by less than £5OO a
year in liquid assets; and that isn’t quite
enough. Still every further £5OO put by
adds £2O or so to the revenue; so on the
past few years’ performances the co. looks
as though it might just about recover its
capital while also paying a reasonable divi-
dend. But there are so many uncertainties
about the pub business that it isn’t a stock
to put by for one’s old age.

Income tax is the nigger that has broken
into the past half-year’s profit. Apart from
that, things are quite as good as they were
previously. The co. was formed to take over
a lease, which expires about 1950; hence it
has to accumulate profit during the oeriod
in order to replace the capital which is repre-
sented by the wasting security. Five years
ago, outside the fixed property and furni-
ture, the co. had £lOB7 and' owed £212.
To-day it has £6423 and owes £45. It
should, of course, be in a much better posi-
tion ; but the little it saved in its first
20 years it had to spend on structural altera-
tions, etc. Capital is £48,000; but all this
isn’t in the leasehold. Approximately in
freeholds and liquid assets it has ±18,000;
and that isn’t a bad start. As profit is
accumulated the interest earnings will grow ;
and it looks as if there will be something
better by-and-bye than 3| p.c.

little, however. First of all, the Brit-

to buy ships to get our wheat shifted ; and
Britain isn’t at all likely to waste shipping
space on zinc concentrates which at present
she can t turn into metal. Further, Britain
has entered into a definite contract for a
period of 10 years after the war to take a
minimum of 100,000 tons of Australian con-
centrates at a price which was entirely sat-
istactory. ’ This is. what is whooped about
as a big contract.” In his usual large,
unpredse way Hughes doesn’t mention

And apparently the one thing that would

“Tributer”: The Bulletin says that the
silver-lead custom works are dealing more
fairly with the producer than the others
are; yet, even as to silver-lead, here are
some results of the monopoly which
Prime Minister Hughes has deliberately
created. We sent 15 tons to be
treated, assaying 52.1 p.c. lead, 18.20 z. of
silver. Metals were paid for at the rate of
£2B 15s. for lead and 2s. 7 7-16 d. per oz.
silver. Metal content# were worth altogether
£209 Os. 2d. Out of this the smelting com-
pany takes £123 55., at the rate of £8 ss. a
ton for treating the ore. This is equal to
2oz. ldwt. of gold. Our other expenses for
wood, explosives, percentage to mine owners
etc., were £5B 10s. This left £76 10s. to be
cut up among 14 men for three weeks’ work,
including three Sundays the engine-drivers
worked bailing water. We didn’t get 6s. a
day. And this was out of ore worth £l7
■ss. 4d. per ton. No wonder the metal agents
and smelters are giving Hughes three cheers.
But by conferring on these people the right
to exploit us he has made mining by the
small man impossible.

Water-power is to give the Speck some
very badly-needed help to meet its lOU’s,
according to Premier Lee’s figures. He
says the capital cost of the Great Lake
scheme has been £465,000 to date, and that
some needed alterations and the cost of
developing and delivering the first 4000 h.p.
for Amalgamated Zinc will bring it up to
£550,000. At this stage he reckons on a
revenue of £14,000 per year from the Amal-
gamated and £28,000 from other sources
making £42,000 altogether. Working ex-
penses are put down at £17,000, so the
£25,000 left over will pay 41 per cent, on
the capital, which is a lot better than most
of the Speck’s public undertakings can
do. But it will be still better later
reckons. It will take another
£600,000 to divert the Upper Ouse
and put in everything necessary to
develop 30,000 h.p. for Amalgamated
Zinc. That will make £1,200,000 altogether.
But the revenue from the 30,000 h.p. the
10,000 which the Hydro-Electric Co. has a
contract for and the rest will be £120,000 a
year—-if all goes right. It is estimated that
working expenses will mop up £20,000 of
that; and the £lOO,OOO left will pay 8J per
cent, on the capital. It is reckoned * that
depreciation of plant will amount to 1.6
per cent on capital, and that and interest at
44 per cent, will run to £74,000 per year.
And there will still be £26,000 left as net
profit. It all sounds very cheerful. But
now what about a sinking fund? Doesn’t
.Lasmama want to ever really own the
undertaking?
Wallaroo-Mount Lyell Fertilisers Co.
(S.A.) has sold more superphosphate than
ever, despite the reduced acreage sown last
season for wheat. The gross profit for 12
months was £4674, plus £3OB interest. The
net profit was £27,004, and out of that the
directors pay 8 p.c. per annum, which calls
for £26,9;>2. This is 1 p.c. (£3369) more
than for the previous year. The subscribed
capital is £336.900. of w r hich freehold, build-
ings, plant and goodwill represent £206,484.

Grenfell (N.S.W.) is about to celebrate
its jubilee. Fifty years ago gold was dis-
covered there by Cornelius O’Brien; the
place was then known as Emu Creek, but
di fil in , g , s generally went by the name of
ihe Weddin,’ after a short range of hills
close by. The town was named after Land-
commissioner. Grenfell, who was afterwards

Towards the 195,500 tons of ore milled by
Creat Boulder last year, development pro-
Vided only 129,500. Thus reserves are

cided to continue operations, it is doubtful

“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : I applaud Higgs’s
rerusal to sanction the flotation of certain
VVestralian mining delusions. Here is one
of them. Promoters formed a co. with a
capital of £32,500 in 65,000 10s. shares.
Vendors, it was stated, were to receive
£4OOO cash, 10,000 fully paids and 6250
shares paid to 55., or a total in cash and
shares of £10,562. Promoters professed
then- willingness to accept £5OO cash and
b. 50 paid shares, equal to £4375. So if
the float came off, £15.437 (practically half
the nominal capital of the co.) would,
according to the prospectus, have been
divided amongst the owners and promoters.
The remaining 40,000 shares were to have
been offered to the public at 2s. (id. on
application and 2s. 6d. on allotment, and
the balance in calls. That would, on the
promoters’ figures* have brought in £lO,OOO,
out of which the owners and promoters
would have got £4500, leaving only £5500 to
equip and work a show in which no payable
ore body has yet been found. The owners
expressed astonishment when they heard
the terms of the prospectus. All the pro-
moters had contracted to give them, they
allege, was £l5OO cash and 9600 paid shares
equal to £6300.
Stuckey has got the Edna May lode in
the Deeps, and without water. For that
performance he deserves both credit and
renown. Of course, the talk of his willing-
ness to buy the first 1000 tons of ore at
£4 a ton was a bit of flam which no doubt
he regrets, his business beinsr to get gold at
the cheapest possible cost for shareholders
and not to traffic in ore. The Edna May
shoot, even in shallow levels, never ran
far oyer loz., and it can hardly be expected
that it will have fattened with depth. In-
deed, Stuckey is strikingly modest about it
now. Further, he resisted the temptation
to turn on the tap directly he started
breaking into the reef. No one knew he
had touched stone until he was 6ft. into
it. His next splash will be when the
shaft runs into the lode 30ft. or 40ft. below
the present crosscut at 480 ft. To May 31
shaft sinking had cost £8073, nearly £l9
every foot sunk. However, with T.
Duff s purse to draw on, the co. has been
to avoid making any issue of the
10,000 shares in reserve, though speculative
brokers have been nibbling after them. As
fetuckey doesn’t work out what tonnage to
look for above 480 ft. a problem can be set.
b } l PPOse he gets 10ft. of stone for a length
of 100 ft Giving him 100 ft. of backs, which
is a decidedly liberal allowance, then he will
have 7000 tons of stone for the batterv.
P 0“ oua ? e estimate this might net him
■ market valuation of the mine
is ill 0,000, so he has to develop a lot of
stone below 480 ft. to make up the present
price of shares.

_ . Sood deal of gold was got in this
legion years ago, but nothing was heard of
ie place lor years until this amazing story
was told. It will be quite time enough to
believe it when, the Government Geologist
confirms it.

BOTTLED in FRANCE.
TO CURE COUGHS USE PINE TAR
AND SUGAR.
A SPECIALIST’S SUGGESTION.
Obstinate tickling coughs, the kind that
racks you to pieces and keeps you awake at
night, can be quickly relieved by ten or
fifteen drops of bitrate of tar on a lump of
sugar, and letting it slowly dissolve in the
mouth. The specialist who recommends this
plan says it is superior to anything he has
ever tried, and may be used with perfect
safety and success on children. To make
an excellent inexpensive cough syrup which
children like, he says there is nothing better
at any price than half a pound of granu-
lated sugar dissolved in half a pint of hot
water, and then stir in 2oz. of bitrate of tar.
When cool pour into a pint bottle, and it is
ready for use. From half a teaspoonful to
a teaspoonful every hour or two will quickly
relieve coughs and colds, and if regularly
used for a few days will give remarkable
benefit in cases of catarrh, asthma and
bronchial affections. — Advt.
