A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 14, 1916.
My Deak Akini. —
Already from Paris the warning comes not
to get your spring frock too wide, otherwise
you’ll be hacking out some of its drapery
before the end of our long summer. A friend
writes from the other side that the best-
dressed women in Paris are not looking like
balloons. It doesn’t suit them. They merely
hang a moderately-wide skirt over a limp
silk petticoat. N’York, too, cannot face
the crinoline and now maintains an
ominous silence about that seven-yards-
round-the-hem we used to hear about.
Still, there is one minor atrocity to
be faced. The darts in the bodice, that
once gave a prim, pinched-in look to the old-
fashioned photo, are In. So Heaven help
the Juno form !
Strickland, with a couple of
daughters, had-a hearty tossing up on a State'
trawler the other day. Oh, yes, it was out-
side the Heads, and you know what that
feels like before you get used to it. His Ex.
and the girls stood it well. But Dave Hall
began to wish himself on a pavement that
didn’t heave, and a pressman who was with
the party couldn’t look at the titbits. Even
the pilot had to fast in the midst of plenty.
But that was bilious headache.
Our G.-G.’s Sydney crony, shipping-man
Colonel Burns, is in trouble. On his way
back from London a few months ago he had
the joy of a surprise visit from his two
lieutenants who had been out lighting the
Senussi and had got a few days off to run
into Alexandria and see dad. Shortly after
that the two boys were separated. Jack
was invalided home; R.D., the baby, was
sent on to France, where he joined a
machine-gun section. What happened to
him there nobody knows yet. He disap-
peared during an attack —whether killed or
captured even his chum can’t say. Lieuten-
ant Jim, the eldest son, who awaited his
father’s return from Europe to get his khaki
suit, has been wearing' it for two or three
months, and expects to be off almost any
day now.
Have you seen the War Chest button
designed by the Murray Campbells? It is
truly artistic, which is more than can be said
for the one sold on Button Day. My Murray
Campbell button is flat and thin, with an
edge of royal blue on a white ground, a
wreath of dark-green laurel in the centre and
on the blue rim: “In Memory of Gallipoli.”
You may get the button in different colors.
Mrs. J. H. I J . Murray and daughter Mary,
reluctantly accompanied by schoolboy Pat,
who prefers the beetles he finds in the River-
view bush, leave for England in the Mooltan.
The eldest son, Terence, fighting now at
Salonika, gets leave in September. That
settled, he hurries to London, where his
sweetheart, Philippa Kitchener, will be wait-
ing on the shore. She’s a niece of the late
Iv. of Iv., as I told you, and daughter of the
late General Sir Walter Kitchener. The
wedding will be early in October. Mary
Murray is tearing herself away from an Aus-
tralian horse and a French poodle. Pat,
who is the makings of a naturalist, goes
later on to Oxford. Just now he prefers to
catch the wild bush insect in its Lane Cove
lair. So the family climbs the Mooltan gang-
way with mixed feelings, and the French*
poodle weeps for its pal.
Belgian Consul Watteeuw and his wife
have sent out cards, stamped with the
arms of their country, to announce the arri-
val of a small son. It’s Ronald Sydney
Albert Marie, with Maurice (after papa) to
finish the list.
The Journalists’ Institute, which missed
the much-engaged Rider Haggard, has taken
all sorts of care to capture Premier Billy
Hughes. It lunches him on Monday,
August 21. His other meals are also pro-
vided, thank you. The Commonwealth Bank
festoons him with oratory and solids on the
following day. And on the Wednesday the
Metal Exchange pays for the dyspeptic
little man’s midday biscuit. He rarely ven-
tures on much more.
At the Golf Club House, Rose Bay, on
Monday night, a feast of welcome was given
by 20 meds. to Dr. Jamieson, who is back
from the strife. Dr. Sinclair Gillies presided
over the soft drinks and led the few remarks.
One still hears loud murmurs of lament over
the banishment of the whisky devil and the
hock fiend every night at sundown, and a
consequent decrease in dinner parties is
recorded at the Club’s hospice.
Marjorie Street, who travels to London in
a week or two, was to have been under the
wing of Mrs. Arthur Dowling; but the news
of Max Dowling’s death may alter the pro-
spect. Miss Street has made her home at
Broughton Lodge since her mother passed
on; for the future it will he under Lady
Poore’s friendly roof, the Admiral’s cheery
wife having “adopted” the winsome girl.
What is the matter with the peeresses of
the Domain Anzac Buffet Committee? Their
latest “explanation” is in contradiction of a
rumor which said that 4th Battalion returned
soldiers had to pay through the nose for a
party they gave in the austere compound. It
appears the peeresses charged the men only
3s. 3d. per head for afternoon-tea. Heavens!
I had almost forgotten that this included
fruit-salad! The committee records with
pride that on this particular day there were
17 soldiers present —exclusive of the 4th’s
little lot. These crowds make the imagina-
tion reel.
Here is a recent
portrait of Mary
Gilmore, by May
Moore. She writes
about many things
besides domestic
pets, as you’ll
know by her book
—“Marri’d a n d
Other Verse s.”
Since that was
published she has
written many more
versos, besides
compiling a cook-
ery book and edit-
ing a page of The
Worker. Mrs. Gil-
more came fr o m
Goulb ur n way
originally, and as
Miss Cameron was school-teaching in Syd-
ney and the Backblocks before she joined
the New Australians in Paraguay. It was a
strenuous time she had there, with a wed-
ding and a baby thrown in. Afterwards she
went to Patagonia, where she lived amidst
plenty of excitement for over a year; but
the family of three reached old Australia
safely in 1902. Here her good nature has
overflowed in many good works. Helping
young authors is one of her specialities; but
she says she is proudest of having intro-
duced pocket-hankies to the gaols of Vic-
toria. It wouldn't have occurred to anyone
but a woman like Mrs. Gilmore that even a
prisoner may get a cold in his head. Or her
head. As a matter of fact, it was a woman’s
sufferings in this respect that moved the
philanthropist to action. “Whatever did
they do before?” somebody once asked Mrs.
Gilmore. “Oh, they were allowed a petti-
coat, you know,” she answered.
Hasn’t Captain Ossy Watt made a name
for himself? The French authorities record
their regret in writing at the Sydney man’s
transfer to the Australian air squadron.
Yet Oswald looked a slim, indecisive youth,
intent merely on wearing a silk hat at the
right angle, when he took up the running as
lion. Help in Emergencies to Governor
liawson.
Every Manly boy at the Front is to eat a
slice of Australian plum-pudding if the Vil-
lage dames can manage it. They’ve organ-
ised a local Women’s Industrial Exhibition
for August 25 as a help to other fund-
sweeping operations.
Mrs. Lebby Hordern, now in England, is
in mourning for her brother, Bruce Monie.
He fell in France about three weeks ago.
The Royal Art Society’s cards are out for
the 37th annual exhibition. Private view
day is September 2. On that date Art
patrons are expected to back their fancy
with any little thing left over from the War
Loan.
Mumps have got into the best circles.
Friends this week are fussing in futile pro-
test because popular Clare Gilchrist is in
hospital with a bad attack. As it comes
under “infectious deseascs” as well as “un-
dignified .ailments,” the patients have to
suffer in seclusion.
The newest Edgecliff engagement, that of
Edith Iloldship to Lieut. Tony Brown, tea-
planter of Ceylon, is being told about. The
pair became engaged some five years since,
but let it lapse, and it stayed that way
until a cable revived it recently. The
lucks;' man is in khaki, and his fiancee
sets sail for Egypt and marriage in
three short busy weeks. Miss Iloldship, who
returned from a trip to Colombo only a few
weeks ago, has been secretary to the 7th
Light Horse sewing guild at Edgecliff ever
since the war began.
Faulkner Ormiston, who lately married
Valerie, a daughter of Bruce Suttor, of
Chatswood, got a wire of congratulation
from sister Isabel. She is the brilliant grad-
uate who used to look like a delicate piece of
porcelain china. Yet as Dr. Ormiston she
lias been mentioned or decorated for her
heroic services to the wounded on nearly
every Front. And she’s still at it.
Shirlow’s etchings in the Mitchell Library
are poems that cast a glamor over a slum
and turn a church steeple into a classic.
“The Gothic Spire,” Collins-street, is a
monument erected by Shirlow to his own
genius. One iron bridge is first-cousin to
another. But the slender span around the
Upper Yarra’s waist is idyllic since it was
put on paper by John. “The Slum in the
back kerbs of Little Bourke-street ceases to
be sordid when it becomes an etched pre-
sentiment of a picturesque slit in high*
walled bricks and mortar. Besides, you can t
see the smells of the Chinese quarters.
The Helpers’ Club, Kirribilli, has had a
musical party in St. James’s Hall proceeds
to the French-Australian League and Ist
A.D. Hospital. Consul-General Chayet was
there; Mrs. Playoust made a few re-
marks to thank everybody; and Mrs. Little-
john, in the latest Idea in fashionable attire,
kindly lectured us over a bouquet of daffo-
dils on our Duty to our soldiers. There s
quite a lot of Duty in old frocks and aprons
at every battalion depot, by the way Duty
that doesn’t say a word about it. The pro-
gramme began by a thin extract of "Mar-
seillaise” at the piano. The _ music
fell down in the middle of it; but
the French bore up cheerfully. Walter
Bentley, with a Scotch frog in his
throat, the day being damp, obliged with a
recitation or two. Mrs. Linden rounded up
the late arrivals and found them plenty of
room. Sid Macdonald.. Mrs. Bruce Robb,
Vera Cree and Raymond Pechotseh were in
the entertainers’ list. The Cullen lady sat
in the executive half-moon with her feet on
the Axminster.
They’re not forgotten in the tropic swamps
of Mesopotamia! School kiddies are going to
veil for Liberty in the Town Hall on August
2!). If nothing prevents Phil Newbury turn-
ing up he’ll warble of Maud and other ladies
for the fund. It's to help our Field Engi-
neers and wireless heroes grin and bear their
lot away up in the steaming swamps.
In a week that palpitated with Battalion
raids for Christmas cheer (I hope you don’t
find them monotonous) there was a small
diversion at Neutral Bay. Private Hazle-
wood, a young electrical engineer, has re-
turned invalided. lie still looks a sick man,
but he isn’t worrying about that. By way
of investing his savings so as to earn a
living he put them into electric bath-fittings
in his mother’s private hospital. It’s in
Ranger’s-road—the handsome villa sur-
rounded by flower-trimmed lawns that used
to be editor Heney’s home. So you can
guess that it’s name is Vailima, for ILL.S. is
one of Heney’s gods. The Cullen lady opened
the new electric department, while matron
Ilazlewood, proud mother of an only son,
beamed in the background. Dame Hay was
in the crowded audience, with Mrs. Ethel
Turner Curlewis’s blue eyes looking over her
shoulder. Mrs. Caro came with daughter
Hilda, and a strong cohort of local nobility
attended the debut of the bath.
Mrs. H. M. Merewether and Mrs. Tom
Watson are joint hon. secs, of a big com-
mittee which represents our Best Suburbs.
The cause is a suggested battle-plane from
the women of N. S. Wales to the War
Office, and Eadith Walker is one of its
keenest backers. It’s a shilling fund, but
there’s nothing to prevent anyone sending
£lOO.
Staying in Sydney this winter are the
Alma Baker couple. lie is the Malay States
man who spends his days hunting up money
to buy air-planes for presentation to the
British Government. Mrs. Alma is a daugh-
ter of the late Frederick Whittaker, a Maori-
lander who caught a knighthood years ago.
This year (d.v.) Lady Edeline .Strickland
opens the fete for the Waitara Foundling
Home —the place with the lovely gardens
and rosy-cheeked babes in airy dormitories.
The Polish Clothing Relief Committee has
just been born into this world of trouble.
The president is Mrs. Campbell Carmichael
and the hon. sec. Miss MacKee. That fiery
Polish patriot, Miss Gzaykowska, merely sits
on the executive and works in the back-
ground for Baby Kosciusko. She’s had a
letter from Henryk Sienkiewicz, chairman
of the General Polish Committee, from
Vevey, in Switzerland, imploring that big
consignments of clothing for the nearly-
naked women and children be sent before
Poland is buried in its winter snow. Oh. a
fine old German gentleman is Wilhelm ! The
clothing depot hums with sewing machines
at 5 Hamilton-street, City.
Another Australian Red Grosser who has
been working at the other end, pretty
Fairlie Cunningham, the Sydney sculptress,
is returning here, her zeal having over-run
her health.
The 17th Battalion is crying out “Help!
Help !” It wants a thousand scarves for its
men, soon to be bitten shrewdly by the
winter winds of France. It also wants to
send its thousand Christmas comforts. In-
cidentally it also wants workers at its quar-
ters, Terranora Buildings. It wants them
badly.
There is money in flower selling—of course
for some hungry battalion’s Christmas boxes.
Mrs. Alex. Gordon is a persistent bloom-
vendor in the smart striped apron that our
Best Suburbs wear when they dabble in
business. The 19th Battalion will get plum
puddings out of Mrs. Alex.’s last Saturday
morning spent behind buckets of flowers in
Paling’s.
There’s to be a French League concert
towards the end of September, with Paul
Du fault as the sta r—though with his silver
mane he could quite as easily understudy
a comet. Mrs. Harry Austin gave the
preliminary push.
The 2nd D.A.C. and Howitzer Brigades
had a Comforts Fund At Home in Falmouth
Chambers last week. They broke out of
their own den, which is full of sewing-
machines, and had an overflow party across
the passage. Mrs. sec. Ballantine, Miss
treasurer Andrew and the committee ladies
were grateful for some fat contributions to
the Christmas boxes. The Straths (Ivillara)
sent along £2O as the results of a gift after-
noon for the not-over-affluent 2nd D.A.C.
and its mates.
A crash came when Commissioner Bud-
den’s cable scratched Christmas Billy for the
race to Potsdam. The local War Chest flung
itself into the breach, and supplies the alter-
native cardboard receptacles, gratis, to
authorised inquirers.
Madge Hopwood Foldi, a Rose Bay girl,
recently stood under a horseshoe of
white roses to say “I will” to Aubrey
Lance. The knot was tied in the Foldi
home, which was a bower of blooms for the
event. Sister Phyllis and Mabel Lance
stood behind the bridal finery—Brussels
.lace and shimmers.
The sky is lifting in France. A few
artists, amongst them some Australians, are
back in Paris studios. Kathleen O’Connor,
daughter of the late engineer, C. Y.. of
Westralia, is in the batch of first fligliters.
During this week Mrs. Verbrugghen—who
is English, by the way—gives two recitals of
British Folk Songs in the Conserva tori uni.
The Welsh items include gems of inarticu-
late consonants in dizzy convolutions. The
Hebridean group, said to be their first
appearance in Australia—which I doubt —-
are interesting as a reflex of primitive life
in the Chilly Isles. When such ditties as
“The Sealwoman’s Joy” (not salewoman’s)
photographed the feminine horizon, Woman
was as meek as a tame Alderney.
“Dot and the Kangaroo” was written by
the late Ethel Pedley, to the surprise of her
friends, who knew her only as the first con-
ductor of St. Cecilia’s choir: It’s a taking
little Australian story, and Grace Stafford
has dramatised it for her pupils. Both per-
formances of “Dot’s” adventures turned
away an overflow. But don’t grieve—there
will be a repetition. The Soldiers’ Wives
and Mothers Fund, whose object is to grant
small loans for emergencies, will get the
results.
The meeting to
elect a Vera Cree
concert committee
ran on velvet tyres.
She’s a pretty Syd
ney girl here’s
her photo, to prove
it —and the brainy
pupil of Ada Gee.
Vera, as I told
you, won the only
Royal Academy and
College of Music
scholarship awarded
this year in Aus-
tralia. After the
’cello-voiced Hull,
resident secretary
of the London R.A.
and C.M., had
thrown a posy at
the girl, Oswald Anderson, as lion,
concert manager, took charge of the
meeting. Mrs. Kelso King, all smiles under
a white-winged hat. was elected president.
The farewell concert comes off in the Town
Hall on October 12. Towards the expenses,
Mrs. Alf Harris has handed in £50 —a hand-
some beginning. Ada Gee probably chap-
erons her pupil to London. They’ll leave
about February.
Kirsty, a daughter of the Andrew
Fletchers, of Lyndhurst station, up Armi-
dale way, has accepted a ring from Bob
Turnbull, of Buccarumbi. He’s a younger
son of ,T. Turnbull, of Katupua, Guy
Fawkes.
John Miller, the dark, fat, forty and jolly
ex-member for Bathurst, was quietly married
a while ago to Miss Sybil Stephen, of Mel-
bourne. The bride is one of the numerous
Stephen, C.J., clan. She is a sister of Mrs.
Arthur Payne, of Melbourne, who slipped
over to St. James’s (King-street) with the
star performer and witnessed the jettisoning
of the maiden name.
Papa William Holman and Rose Seaton
e-locuted in the Conservatorium on Saturday
afternoon. They were backed up by their
pupils in scenes from Richard lll.—who
wasn’t worth the flowers of speech in which
he has been wreathed by Shakspeare—and
“Coriolanus.”
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, August 14, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Some of the Hughes enthusiasts are won-
dering how cvev the G.-G. and his wife could
tear themselves away from Victoria by
Saturday evening’s express when William
the Tonqueror was arriving on Tuesday
morning. Private-secretary Victor Hood re-
presented Governor Stanley in the welcom-
ing party at the station, but there was no
lapelled gentlielp present from the Ferguson
household. Social weather-readers trouble
themselves greatly over these small matters
of detail.
Daily papers paragraphed a Hughes re-
ception at Federal Government House on the
night of his arrival, and various dames who
read it over their morning tea flung on dress-
ing-gowns at once and foamed for the tele-
phone. The poor young man on duty at the
House was kept busy all day explaining that
no invitations had been issued because, there
was no function —no, madam, no function at
all! There might be one in three weeks’
time, but he could not be suah.
The Hughes reception at the Town Hall
was a surge of excitement. A battalion of
police drove the curious crowd back from the
Town Bfall portico, so that the Importants
might pass through unhustled; but a Yarra
Ranker managed to trundle his cart past the
steps, right under the Hughes nose, to adver-
tise the rival showmen down the river. Ut
course, there was a rush to turn the weary
horse out of the sacred enclosurebut that
merely blocked the passage of the high-hatted
politicians who followed two-and-two, Aoali s
Ark fashion, after the little man.
Inside the hall the womenfolk were
directed to the balconies. The' body of the
room was filled with men, bottles, trays and
waitresses. When champagne was handed
round for toasting purposes one man out ot
four dozen refused the wine and asked tor
water. After the brown-clad waitress had
recovered she realised that he wasn t order-
ing a bath, but a drink. Returned soldiers
occupied the organ seats and applauded like
a thunderstorm in the right places.
The three lonely women on the platform
Mrs. Hughes, Mrs. Pearce and the Hennessy
dame—showed some brave finery. Mrs.
Hughes, in brown velvet, had large ostrich
feathers in her hat. (“The Cape” always
puts an ostrich plume in the hat of the
woman traveller who passes that way.) Mrs.
Pearce’s black velvet was almost crinolined
with braid. The Hennessy lady wore kliaki-
colored tailorings and hat —a smart turn-out.
I noticed that the excited crowd down at the
foot of the platform called for cheers for
“Lady Hughes.”
When Hughes began to speak, in his
heavy, nasal voice, he was first very sedate
and calm. Then he suddenly fired up and
threw himself into all the oratorical attitudes
which David Low has pictured him in.
The last day of the Australian Steeple-
chase meeting at Caulfield was bright,
breezy and jolly, despite the long-faced
punters who mourned for the favorites and
said hard things about Ungala for winning
at 25 to 1. There were 23 jumps to face,
hut the Robert Powers’ 6 to 1 Rendrock
toppled at the second and waved his hoofs
despairingly at the sky. The Fairbairns
eagerly watched Uncle Charlie’s Booligal
as it sailed along in the lead; but the
deluded animal finished last with a bumped
knee. The Clive Leonards’ Westaway gal-
loped home third and earned a small hatful
of gold.
Mrs. Manifold wore braided velvet and
smiles and petted the winning Becassine in
the birdcage after the first race. Then the
Manifold purple-and-gold scored another
brilliant win with Coradjill’s help. Queen
Mills of Gippsland wept gracefully in navy
blue when Oaracre hobbled in on three legs
after the flat race. Fan Dango Mackay
sat in the grandstand all day wearing a
smart little black turban and a huge
shoulder cape of black fox. Mrs. Leslie
Macdonald was in cream cloth and sup-
ported a white toque with a purple cherry
over one eyebrow and a green cherry over
the other. Mrs. Geoffrey Syme was charm-
ingly dressed in soft brown radium silk and
edgings of amber-colored fur. Her
eldest daughter made a first race-
course appearance in a checked suit and
two big brown pigtails. Mrs. Herbert Syme’s
steel-grey silk skirts flared imposingly and
were circumnavigated by three circular bands
of velvet. Mrs. Tye wore natty blue toggery
and showed slim ankles with fawn boots at
the terminus.
Masked girls in Russian dress took around
collecting boxes for Russian week. One
wore flopping knee-high boots. Another’s
Roman nose wouldn’t allow her mask to sit
on her face, but pushed it out at an acute
angle.
The Italians’ first swoop at “Butterfly”
was spoiled in some degree by the infant they
retained to play Little Trouble, the Butter-
fly-child, whose “sunny golden ringlets” are
referred to in melody as evidence of its U.S.
parentage. The poor kid bawled as soon as
it saw the audience. The orchestra thun-
dered and the principals yelled their best;
but the horrified baby got its high note in
true Italian style. When Russ propelled it
off the stage the high C still shrilled from
the wings. The audience got a lot of
amusement out of this consistent actor, for
he was wide awake at every appearance aiid
bawled like a Caruso. Some further comic
relief was supplied by the servant of the very
lively priest. The servant had to
hold" a Jap brolly over the clerical head,
and he had to work overtime to do it. Once
the brolly knocked a high arch sideways,
and it rocked perilously over the girls who
were cowering in four-and-elevenpenny
cotton kimonos in the background. Miss
Gonsalez-Butterfly, who looks all right in a
golden wig, turned herself into an Israel-
itish damsel with a heavy, black head-dress.
There was a packed house for the first
Butterflying. Amy Castles listened critically
from the circle. Mrs. Fred Eggleston, in an
embroidered bead cloak, hostessed a theatre
clutch. Mrs. Woolcott ruffled in fashionable
black taffetas. A lot of the audience these
times consists of women-parties.
Miss Russ is the most alluring little vixen
of a Carmen that Melbourne has had a
chance of seeing. If she could only sing like
the big fat Carmens who could only be
carried off in vans she would be a tremen-
dous hit. As it is, the audience feels she is
worth the sacrifice when Jose cuts loose
from his job. This barmen’s dancing is
clever and wicked, Sue is wrapped in a
creamy white shawl; her gold skirt is looped
with jewels and filmy black Spanish lace,
and the ankles and feet inside the gold stock-
ings cannot be described in the few words I
have bv me. Yellow roses and nothing else
seemed" to fasten her gown to her fascinating
shoulders. Truly she has "the beautiful
bones” that Du Maurier made his artistic
heroes rave about.
Just after the release from gaol of China-
man Chia I received a letter from a London
chatterer who tells me that Mrs. Unie
Russell Chia is leaving London for a year’s
tour of Canada and the United States with
a comedy co. That done, she will flutter in
this direction.
Tie too-excitable Chia is now waiting for
the next China boat to take him to a Uni-
versity billet in his own country. He looks
„very fit after his three years’ experience of
the simple life. But he was for-
tunate in having a plentiful supply of books.
Chinese and English classics passed away the
time in his non-working hours. He has
made an ethical study of prison life, and
views the penitential period through rose-
colored specs.
The Ben. J. Fink widow —one of the
handsomest matrons of her day—is in
London spending money patriotically. She
has donated £5OOO (4,800,000 finks, no
less!) to the Douglas Shields hospital in
London, and has endowed a Harold Fink bed
for disabled officers in memory of her dead
son.
Russian Day on Friday was mostly
boosted along by the bookies. One of them
sold ties at the top of his enormous voice all
the afternoon from a motor-car in Collins-
street. “With every tie you buy, you get a
portrait of ME!” he shouted. Who could
resist that?
Mrs. Crivelli gave a tea-party at the
Vienna to rally some of her Fren&h-week
helpers for the approaching appeal on behalf
of the orphans of Pierre and Francois. Miss
Guthrie and Mrs. Theo Weigall (she is a
daughter of one-time Governor Hamilton, of
Tasmania) were there as right-hand social
aides. The pretty little Crivelli daughter kept
one eye politely on mamma’s guests while the
other roved longingly to a neighboring party
made up of Italian opera stars. (Try this
exercise during the long winter evenings.)
Mrs. Crivelli had souvenirs tied to the
bouquets for her guests. They were small
lockets with wildflowers gathered at Verdun
by one of her fighting sons.
Gaud Mayor Hennessy and his wife gave a
cosy dinner to all the councillors and their
wives one night last week. The com-
pany fed in state in the old Council
Room under rose-shaded lights and among
pink -carnations, and there was a re-
union and coffee party later in the
Mayoress’s blue-and-gold rooms, with soft
music laid on. The Hennessy dame is most
considerate with the musicians, and always
engages different sets in turn. This gives
many of the hard-pressed entertainers a
chance. x\t this last event one of the singers
was Reba Rangan, a local soprano who is
shrilling her way to fame.
Gaud Mayor Hennessy and his wife left
for Sydney and Brisbane by the Omrah on
Saturday. They have gone in the wake of
the G.-G. couple, and are out for 15 days’
rest from the merry-go-round.
Word has been received of the death of
Mrs. Bisshopp. wife of Captain Cecil
Bisshopp, R.N. The lady was Maud, a
daughter of old-time barrister Butler Cole
Aspinall. Her death occurred under tragic
circumstances at Palmerston Quay, near
Harwich. She hafl journeyed from Scotland,
where her home was, to see her husband,
who had a couple of days’ shore leave.
Apparently in the best of health, she entered
the Great Eastern Hotel; and there on meet-
ing her husband she collapsed, and died in a
couple of hours. The excitement caused a
rush of blood to the head, and a clot ot
blood on the brain killed her.
Mrs. Algy Landale’s only brother, Charlie
Langtree, has been killed in France. He
‘went to England when war first blazed
and got a commission in the Artillery.
Colonel Appleton, home again on furlough,
is about looking like an abridged edition ot
himself. He was very ill in Egypt whilst
the merciless sun climbed up to lLLdeg. in
the shade every day for weeks.
Small war-time economies don’t worry the
Alexandra Club, which is about to shunt
from the Gas Office (appropriate location!)
to the Collins-street house which was built
by the late Dr. Beaney. Alterations are
going to cost £2OOO. The best tabbies will
soon have lovely rooms to purr in.
Claude Grice, who rode his own horse in
the V.It.C. steeplechases before he sported
the khaki of a Light Horseman, has been
missing for some months. He is thought
to be a prisoner in Turkey.
From “Rosna,” in England : —-
The Ellison Macartney lady, from Tasmania, has
arrived in London and is staying with the Justice
Darlings when she isn't delivering personal mes-
sages to Tassy’s Anzacs in hospital.
Adelaide’s load of gossip:—
With nearly 650,000 votes to her credit, Mrs. It. M.
Hawker, “Queen of Sports,” was crowned Queen of
Australia Day at the Tivoli on Tuesday. Miss
d’Erlanger, as Queen of Children, came second on the
list, and the whole competition brought in close on
£13,000 for the fund. Mayor Isaacs performed the
coronation gymnastics. Amongst the audience weie
the Galways, the Crawford Vaughans and Mayoress
Isaacs. The total of the Soldiers’ Fund, by the way,
now stands at well over £300,000.
Mareeba, the Red Cross Hospital at Woodville,
which has been more or less in use for some weeks,
was formally opened by the Governor on Saturday.
It is to be staffed by voluntary aids and financed
by Red Cross funds. The recreation hall at the back
of the building was built with State-school children’s
pennies.
Dr. Lilian V. Cooper, of Brisbane, put in a few
days here as the guest of Dr. Helen Mayo before
departing by the Kaiser-I-Hind to join the Scottish
Women’s Unit at Salonika. Dr. Cooper is to join the
Unit as surgeon, paying her own expenses and taking
her own orderly, Miss Bedford, with her. One of
Professor Stirling’s daughters, who has been working
at the St. Dunstan’s Hospital for Blinded Soldiers,
is also to join.
Dr. Swift has had news that his lieut. son, of the
East Lancashires, has been wounded. Other S. Aus-
tralians reported punctured are Captain Frank Lloyd,
who was first commandant at the Exhibition Camp ;
Lieut. Godfrey Wakelin; the Fowler Stewarts son;
Sydney, son of the Walter Smiths, of Yalumba ; and
Lieut. L. Gordon Sandford, second son of a one-time
M.L.C. for the Southern District.
Engineering seems to run in the Goodman family,
for the son of our tramways man has been awarded
the Angas Engineering Scholarship, which is worth
£2OO a year for two years. He took his B.E.
degree last year, and has enlisted.
Artist Will Ashton and a London committee have
purchased a couple of paintings for the local Gallery.
—one by Arnesby Brown, R.A., the other by Hughes-
Stanton, A.R.A. According to Ashton, “both are
splendid works and represent the artist at his best.’
Lady Victoria Buxton’s death was reported from
London on Thursday. When Sir T. F. was Governor,
from ’95 till ’99, the family was full of good works
and its dignity never wore a frill.
After all, Melbourne is to have its Jane
Hop. Donald McLeod, Chief Secretary, has
at length decided to give the idea a flutter,
in spite of Sainsbury’s objections, and has
called on the National Council of Women
to make recommendation of two suitable
ladies. The duties of the constablesses will
be to keep Little Annie and Willie from
playing in the gutter after nightfall, to dis-
courage truancy, chevy the white-slaver and
the furnished-villain, minimise “mashing,”
permeate the parks and supervise the slums.
It is a large order for a pair, but the pro-
posed extension of the system in Sydney
shows that Jane is able to give good value
for her wages.
Stella Swifte (Mrs. H. C. Piper’s attrac-
tive chick) has left for England, where a
D.S.O. is waiting to make her Mrs. Mar-
tyn. The man is a lieut.-colonel, a West-
ralian, who left with the first contingent.
A Collins-street jeweller is showing a new
trinket called “The Patriotic Puss.” The
ornament is not a miniature of a very Red
Cross commit tee-woman, but _ just _ a black
cat with a tricolor bow under its chin.
The A.S.C. Divisional Train out at Broad-
meadows brewed a great party for Amy
Castles on Wednesday, and Anay went out in
great voice and sang like a bird. Bollard
declaimed the Marseillaise, and flautist
Amadio blew very tenderly. Carmen Delprat
a gorgeous pink outfit, including even
pink slippers, and played her violin. One
of the listeners was sist-er Mawson, ex-
plorer Douglas’s wife. Amy Castles wore
all white, and the boys gave her a bouquet
of scarlet japonica to hold. There were
2000 soldiers to supply the applause, the
gorgeous Headquarters Staff were in the
front row, and nurses and doctors were
plentiful. The boys provided a great supper
for the singers and guests and motored them
home at some cold, silent hour.
Remember Cecil Parkes, the wise-looking
Williamstown youngster who fiddled so bril-
liantly at Melba’s Belgian concert? Well,
a couple of months ago he left with his
father for the republic where the unmusical
notes come from, and now a telegram from
San Francisco announces that the boy has
given a concert and scored an emphatic
success. “He is a great violinist in the
truest sense.. His understanding power is
that of a full musically-grown man,” is what
one critic wrote.
Amy Castles is off in October to fill an
American engagement. She was understood
to have been off to somewhere many months
ago.
Actress Gwen Burroughs, who is the Nun
in “The Story of the Rosary,” lost her father
the other day. Heart trouble was to blame.
He was Thomas Burroughs, landowner, of
Buckley, near Geelong.

ALITIES
OLD ASSOCIATIONS.
“You know, boys, toe often associate one
good thing with another. For instance, Health and happiness, Gold arid Sil-
ver. Note can any of you give me another example?”
“Yes, Miss; Tripe and Onions!”
“Deepsea Fisherman” : The Speck’s JTisher-
ies Commissioner Flynn revives an old controversy by
recommending- that the free use of crayfish pots be
allowed. Most of the fishermen down there who cray-
fish for a living are dead against the pots. They say
that the Victorian fishermen have pretty well exter-
minated the crayfish on the Victorian coast with the
pots, and that now they want to do the same on the
Tasmanian coast. Their theory is that the main
damage is done when, as often happens, a pot breaks
loose. The ways get in and can’t get out, and then
they eat each other, and others keep getting in, and
so on ad infinitum.. The Tasmanian fishermen use
the hoop-net, and there’s no great danger of any-
thing of the kind happening with that. Prof. Flynn
reckons the net is too slow and tedious, and that
there isn't the slightest danger of exhausting the
crayfish supply, to which the impolite fishermen reply
“Rats!” Pots are used on the Tasmanian coast, of
course, but only on the strict q.t. and at the risk of
having the boat confiscated. Anyway, the Lee
Ministry doesn’t propose to do anything with Flynn’s
report, which only cost the State £4OO.
“Analyst” : I don’t believe in quacks, but
I must and do believe my eyes. I bought a Newfound-
land pup in Sydney for a pound, and when I got him
home found he was stone blind. It was pathetic to
see him blundering into chair- and table-legs so
I took him to half a dozen vets, who talked
learnedly of congenital blindness, and did nothing.
Shortly * afterwards I took my pup with me to
Bananalar.d, and owned a very blind dog till I met a
Chow banana-grower at Gcraldton. I told the Chow
the pup’s history, and when he said he could cure him
I agreed to let him try provided there was no
cruelty in the treatment. The Chow made a mysteri-
ous preparation of paw-paw, which he blew into the
dog’s eyes, after which he bandaged the eyes with
paw-paw leaves. In a week that dog could catch a
Johnstone River mosquito on the fly. The paw-paW
should be scientifically investigated from roof to
basement.
“Hay” : The mia-mia, the wurley and the
gunyah of the coastal-district Binghi are often men-
tioned by Abo.-writers, but I haven’t seen any
description of the architectural beauties of the yap-
para. Our black bro. of the Far Back never builds a
house except when he expects heavy rain, and
directly the weather clears he moves out and never
enters the place again on any account. Mulga is his
favorite building material because it grows in con-
venient shapes. He begins by choosing a straight
piece about five feet long with a fork at the end.
This he plants upright about Ift. in the ground, and
it is the only part of the structure that is made fast
to the earth. The backbone or ridge-pole is then
laid in the fork with* a bend upwards. Next come
the ribs, bow-shaped pieces which are laid against
the backbone with their ends resting on the ground.
The complete skeleton is dome-shaped, about 4ft.
high and sft. in diameter. A coating of saltbush is
laid on crossways and another lot with the ends
down like thatch to throw the rain off. Lastly, sand
is hurled at the domicile, and the yappara, which
looks like an old-time beehive, is ready for the
housing of Mr. and Mrs. Binghi and family, half a
dozen dogs and all the smoke from the fire in the
entrance. These buildings can be thrown together
with marvellous rapidity, and frail as they seem will
stand for years. I have come across them sometimes
after the sand and brush covering had long dis-
appeared, and still the yappara stood firm and could
only be demolished by being taken to pieces bit by
bit.

*‘D.F.”: The distribution of the Oyster
Bay pine is a puzzle. It grows on a strip of country
about 100 miles long on the east coast of Tas.,
extending from Bream Creek on the south to about
the Douglas Rivet on the north, and is nowhere
found more than a few miles from the coast; Then
it is found on Maria Island, off this strip of coast,
and on the eastern Straits islands, including the
isolated Kent Group. As far as the mainland is
concerned there doesn’t seem any reason why it
should be confined to this harrow strip of lit-
toral. Within its limits it grows in all sorts of
places. It has a vertical range from the sea-level
to bleak hilltops between 2000 and 3000 feet above
It; it grows on rocky cliffs and on open, deep-soil
flats and is equally accommodating in other respects.
As it seems to be pretty ancient on the earth as a
Variety, it can’t be that it hasn’t yet had time to
Spread further. The very fact that it grows on the
Kent Group seems to show that it dates a long wav-
back. At the southern limit of its range, anyway! it
flourishes vigorously right up to the line at which
it ceases to grow.
“H.V.E.” : For arrant cowardice give me
the gohanna, or monitor lizard. A niuscovy duck
which had escaped from a bush poultry yard laid a
clutch of eggs in the hollow of a fallen tree pro-
jecting out over the salt water of an estuary. Hatch-
ing time was drawing near, but prior to the happy
event a large gohanna discovered the clutch on a day
when Mrs. Muscovy was out for an Airing. The
big go. was comfortably gorging eggs when the
outraged proprietress scuttled up the log and seized
him by the back of the neck with her powerful beak.
Without showing fight the go. plunged into the
water, with Muscovy still hanging on; but in its
natural element the duck could give the lizard a
worst time than ever, and did so. It was
a chastened and wing-beaten go. which crawled
out on the opposite shore, and a puffing but trium-
phant Muscovy which seated herself on the remainder
of the clutch of eggs.
“Te Babarantiko”: Whalemeat is. as
“Murrabit” says, good and wholesome food, but
until accustojped to J;he flavor most people would
have a strong aversion to it. Years ago while at
Trangisvaag, Faroe Islands, near the fringe of the
Arctic Circle, I helped the natives drive a “school”
of whales aground on the beach at Sandevaag Bight.
The carcases were cut up (it was not a very large
kind of whale) and carefully shared among the
inhabitants of the island (Tveraa) in quantities
according to social standing. Like porpoise, the meat
was dark red and was “blanketed” in gritty-looking
white blubber about three inches thick. .The blubber
again was covered with a thick, black hide. I did
not care for the flavor of the meat —it was too fish-
oily—but the natives liked it as much as South
Sea Islanders like braized shark’s liver.

RABBITING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Knights of the rabbit-lioe, wheat-bag, and lantern,
Putting the cunning of Bunny to shame,
City folk think it's a loafing profession,
Wouldn’t you like to have them at the game?
Out in the night when the frosty ground crackles,
Fingers and toes numb and chill with the cold,
Making your rounds with a curse and a shiver,
Wishing that you in your blankets were rolled ;
Out in the night in the scrub and the forest,
Weird in the lamplight and sombre and drear,
Nothing to see but a jumble of gum-trees,
Nought but the plaint of the mopoke to hear;
Down on your knees setting traps in the midnight.
Cursing the cold as it bites to the bone !
Still there are chances and luck for the lucky,
And never a boss when you work on your own.
So it’s mate your rounds again, be it frost or snow
or rain,
They all are bad for trapping, bringing wrath and
pain and grief;
But the city must be fed, it must have its meat and
bread,
And rabbit-meat comes handy at the present price of
beef.
Tramping for miles every day for your living,
Looking for “sets” that are likely to score,
Hoping for ever, with hopes often blighted,
For catches to net you a guinea or more;
Cheered by the wonderful v.jrns in the papers,
Dealing with places some distance away,
Telling of trappers who get for their labors
Tenners a week as their minimum pay ;
True they may be, and good luck to the trapper,
Piling up wealth at so splendid a rate,
Yet somehow or other there seem to be places
Where toil is abundant and catches not great;
Places in plenty where billions of bunnies
Proud local liars say cumber the earth,
Sadly you find when you've gone there exulting
Just what such wonderful stories are worth.
So it’s round the traps again, working hard for what
you gain,
And to-day your heart is joyful, and to-morrow all
is grief;
But the city must be fed, it must have its meat and
bread,
And you sell it country rabbit so that you may live
on beef.
N.s.w.
QUIDAM.

It Can Be Readily Darkened to Its
Youthful Color by a Simple Home
Recipe.

when age or a diseased scalp streaks the
hair with silver; nor is there need to use
expensive dyes or sticky, smeary prepara-
tions. You can make up an excellent mix-
ture at home, which soon makes grey or
faded hair dark, soft and young-looking if
you will try this simple recipe:—
Bay Rum • } oz -
Oriex Compound 1 small box.
Water • • i'Piut.
Any chemist can supply the ingredients at
a small cost, and you will have a prepara-
tion that will be excellent for scalp dis-
orders, dandruff, falling hair, etc., as well as
a reliable darkener of grey hair. Apply
once a day until the hair is of the required
color, then use once every two weeks. Try
it. —Advt. [7
“Wal”: The, trepang-hunting Malays who
visited Northern Australia in the days when Binghi
was alone in his glory also included the southern
coast of Papua in their itinerary when they went
before the north-west monsoon from Macassar. All
along the seaboard from Hale Sound to Samarai the
population shows undoubted signs of the visitation,
more especially the natives of Hula, on llood Penin-
sula, and Keapara, on the lagoon that also bears •
Hcod’s name. The light brown skin and straight
hair accompanying the almond eye of the typical
Malay is abundant at Hula, while the fishing craft is
to-day the principal means the people have of getting
tucker. For seamanship the Hula man stands in a
class by himself, while his love for bathing and
habits of cleanliness stamp him as far apart from
the pure-blooded Papuan. In intelligence, too, the
gentleman from Hula is a long way in front of his
cousins in the interior, while in trading pursuits he
cah give points to an Armenian Jew when it comes
to watering a bag of copra or artistically sanding a
sack of beche-de-mer. Like most Papuans the Hula
man is not able to give a very reliable story of his
origin, but so far as he knows he is the descendant
of people who came from the West and were great
canoe sailors and fishermen. Both he and the
Keapara people use without any alteration hundreds
of words of pure Malay. Another custom that shows
Malayan ancestry is the building of the villages on
piles in the shallow waters off the beaches. Only the
Papuans dwelling between Galley Reach and Mar-
shall’s Lagoon, about 130 miles in length of coast-
line, adopt this type of architecture. Other tribes
that build marine villages are absolutely unlike the
Hula, their physique being purely Papuan, but they
have possibly imitated the reef dwellings as a means
of protection from the attacks of the warlike moun-
taineers, who, until Law and Order came to Papua,
raised old Henry on their saltwater cousins, killing
and eating them whenever they felt inclined for a
little change in the menu.

“The Hum”: The Devil’s puff-box men-
tioned by “E.E.S.” has virtues that belie its name.
The cocky who knows applies it to Dobbin’s sore
shoulder and thereby fits him for the collar again
in quick time. It is the best healer I know for a
wound or sore.

“Eddyson”: Old Thornton has been telling
me about his dog Boxer. “He’s a well-bred dorg, a
little bit of everythin’, but what there is is good.
Mostly retriever, I should say he was, ’n’ there ain’t
nothin’ whatever wrong with him ’ceptin’ too much
intelligence. I trained him t’ run messages fer me.
He’d take a basket, ’n’ trot off fer anythin’ I wanted
from the grocer or the butcher. I jest writ out the
order, put the paper in the basket, ’n’ sez I
‘Butcher!’ or ‘Grocer!’ ez the case might be, ,’n’
away he goes ’n’ is back in ten minutes with the
goods. But one month ole Hockin’ the butcher has
at me with a bill what ’u’d roon a bank. S’elp me, I
couldn’t have ate the truck he had up agin me if
I'd bin a horfan asylum. But Hockin’ was dead
square; ’twas me dorg went back on me. What does
he do when the butcher has served him with my
meal but he collars the written order ’n’ drops it in
the basket, too! Some other time when I’m off at
work he hunts up that paper, drops it into the
basket, ’n’ hoofs it off t’ ole Hockin’s, ’n’ gets
another helpin’. I found ten uv me written orders
where he’d hid ’em fer his own use. No, it wasn’t

“Yarriambiak”: The yarn about the bush
publican who used to rush out and ring a bell to
inform the neighbors that a shearer had wafted in
with a cheque reminds me of a Carlton (Vic.) bung
whose rule it was not to serve more than two drinks
to one person at one sitting. He still sticks to it.
“Now, boys,” he says, after the second round, “it’s
time for you to get home,” and whether they go
home or not they get no more coffin varnish in
pub. Apparently the old chap must make the system
pay, as he has been carrying it out for a good many
years, and seems to be prosperous.

“Sarto”: How many uses has a bottle in
the bush? Let’s see now —(1) it is just a bottle
and holds beer; (2) rolling-pin; (3) domestic
weapon; (4) foot-warmer; (5) garden and path
border; (6) glass-house for young plants; (7) lan-
tern; (8) bush-fire starter; (9) gate-hinge; (10)
bird-scarer in fruit-trees. Then it is just a bottle
again and holds more beer. Some other Abo.-liar
carry on.
* * * *
“Split-link”: What's the matter with the
aboriginal method of finding the cardinal points?
Hop off the roost early and turn the face to the
rising sun (east), stretch out the arms semaphore
fashion, and the left points north, and the right,
of course, south. The back of the head is west.
It’s a sure and accurate system unless the sun
makes a mistake.
“Papua” : “Oko” in a recent issue makes
the rash remark that “a great chief has never
existed in Papua.” I will correct this ignorance.
There have been many great chiefs, the most power-
ful being Kauri, of .the Purari River tribes, who once
fought a pitched battle with Dr. Macgregor’s ex-
ploring party and was only defeated after many of
his nobility and commoners had been blown to the
Happy Hunting Grounds. Kauri had great influence
in his day, and kept the respect of his people as
well as 75 wives. When a man angered him the old
chap would order his killing and subsequent disposal
at a banquet. But the effects of unlimited power, or
the results of his strenuous matrimony, undermined
his constitution, and he went west in 1909. Kaipu,
his successor, with only about two dozen wives, is a
wicked old wretch, who wields the lime-spoon with
effect even in these more enlightened days of Law
and Order. Another prince in his own right was the
late lamented Enamakala, who ruled over the
Trobriand Islands. This chieftain had his palace at
Kavataria village, and was noted for the number of
yam-houses, pigs and princesses he held in fee simple.
In the Trobriands there are two classes of natives—
chiefs and common people. Enamakala was the son
of a chief, and before the coming of the white men
exercised enormous influence throughout the Group,
waging war on the other villages until he had them
all under his control. He forced the inhabitants to
render him tribute of pigs, yams and women. While
he lived it was suicide for a commoner to stand
before him. Every ordinary native had to crawl on
his stomach when passing the great man. All pro-
perty was his. The gardens and cocoanuts planted
by the commoners he regarded as his own, and ex-
pected his share of even the petty chief’s industry
when he asked for it. With a backing of several
hundred fighting men, who looked on him as their
god, he always got what lie asked for—generally the
lot, if there was any resistance on the part of the
princelets, who usually died during the excitement.
To name other discolored noblemen, Ivoapena, of
ICeakoro Bay, and Reboka-Namo, of the Gosoro tribe,
on the Kemp Welch River, were both great chiefs
in their day. They led their warriors in many a
sanguinary raid on each other’s women and pigs,
and their combined efforts did more to thin out the
population of the Kemp Welch Valley than clothes
and dysentery have done since. Bushimaiwa was
once an important man who lorded it over the Mam-
bare River villages and used his influence to effect
the murders of Clarke, leader of the first band of
prospectors to go gold seeking around there, and
Magistrate Green and his police. Captured and im-
prisoned at Port Moresby he broke gaol and made
his way, with several other prisoners, across the
ranges to his home, living, so the survivors said, on
the flesh of the weaker members of the party. Want
of evidence against him saved his life, and he now
lives, a person of no importance, upon the memories
of a murderous life.
“Hay” : I have never seen any tanned
skins more pliable and durable than those cmed by
Binghi of the rangy country of Central A us.. I
had a rug, made of rock-wallaby skins, which was
used constantly in the roughest way for over five
years and was still none the worse for wear. The
pelts were treated as follows: Binghi pinned them
out to dry, woolly side down, and spread plenty of
wood ashes over them. When dry they were trimmed
into squares. Then about half an inch of the edge
was folded down, woolly side in, and rubbed with a
piece of flint having a rather sharp edge. This took
off all the waste stuff and raised a kind of rib on
the back of the skin. Another half-inch was then
folded and rubbed, and so on right across the
square. Then crossfolds were rubbed in the same
manner. When finished a nice half-inch checkered
pattern was formed on the reverse of the skin. I
have tried often but could never manage to raise the
soft, velvety rib that black brother produced with
apparent ease. The skins were sewn together with
strong threads, from the sinews of the emu’s leg
for preference, though sometimes kangaroo tail
sinews were used. The sewing implement was a
bone sharpened to a needle point and used in the
manner of a shoemaker’s awl. My rug cost me a
quarter of a pound of black stick tobacco, and
nothing warmer or more comfortable for camping
out was ever fashioned than that old “meetroo
cumbi,” or sleeping skin.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: Re the homing instinct of
animals. A Melbourne man sent a setter to friends
in Sale (Vic.) to be worked. The dog was kept on
the chain for two days to accustom him to his new
quarters, but on the third day he was allowed off for
a run. Shortly afterwards he did the disappearing
act. A search was made, but without result that
day. Next morning a wire was sent to his owner
127 miles away that Bruno had vanished. A reply
was almost immediately received that the truant
had just arrived. He had found his way home
within 24 hours.
* * * *
“E.N.W ” : “Yancy” (B. 18/7/’l6) is right
when he says lucerne pressing is hard yakker; but if
six tons is the best he knows of for a day s work 1
can beat him. At Bacchus Marsh 18 months ago I
was in charge of a Smith press, which was
driven by a Foden engine, and in 16 hours we
put through 45 tons. With the same machine I have
put through on an average 22 tons of straw a day
for a fortnight. We put the press across the end of
the elevators of the thrasher, and thrash and press
in the one operation, thereby saving a lot of labor
in building straw stacks. I would like to know if
anyone who follows chaffcutting has a better tally
than 960 bags in 9* hours, the bags averaging 20 to
the ton.
* * * *
“Wilberru” : A few months ago a couple of
splitters got 250 posts from one red-gum tree on
the Broadlands estate, Bacchus Marsh (Victoria). I
don’t know if this is a record for Australia, but if
anybody wants to say it isn’t, let him mention
something that can be verified. Remarks like, A
few years ago me and my mate dug 27861 posts
from a tree on a hill in Westralia” have no influence
with me.
THE LUCK OF HIS WIFE.
JACKY (to visitor who has been ill-treating
his horse): “By cripes, boss, you
married?”
VISITOR: “No damn fear, Jacky. Why?”
J ACKY: “I been tinkit plurry good thing for
your missus.”
Mclntosh Brown, of Dover (Tas.), used
to try to save souls, but now he’s out to sal\e
bodies with a new blue-gum ointment, said to con-
centrate the virtue of the young blue-gum leaves
and to be a cure for cuts and sores and such odds
and ends. If it came from Greenland it would have
had a better chance two or three years ago; but
perhaps things are different now. Mclntosh also
turns out a blue-gum fluid warranted unbeatable for
bringing the rust off boilers and cleaning locomotives.
LIVING BEYOND HIS MEANS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They brought the bitten man in haste
Along the dusty track,
And up and down the hills they chased
Our most unhappy Mac,
While Grimm, to raise the needed din,
Beat fiercely on a digger’s tin.
Disposed was Mac to sit and sleep.
They would not have it so,
But tirelessly essayed to keep
Their patient on the go,
And when they came to Hegan’s bar
Their clamor woke the hills afar.
It wakened Hegan. Up he got,
And Billy Seaman spake:
“Quick, bring your liquor, all the lot—■
Mac’s bitten by a snake!”
But Hegan softly scraped his phiz.
“You know,” says he, “how whisky’s riz?
“See here,” he says, “ye’d better take
And fill him at the creek.
McGinnis bitten by a snake!
’Tis loike his blashted cheek.
To live at that rate, Oi declare,
He’d nade to be a millionaire!”
VICTORIA.

This little co. has had a remarkably success-
ful career since the Lithgow coal interests
were brought into harmony. During the
past three or four years, besides the regular
10 p.c. divs., the shareholders have had one
bonus of 6s. Sd. per share and two others
of half-a-crown each. The whole lot could
have been taken in cash; but the lirst lot
was naturally taken in shares when that
alternative was offered. Still, the past isn t
always a guide to the future. For one thing
the State is opening up a coal-mme at Litn-
gow, and after supplying the railways with
their requirements, the public’s custom is to
be sought. This isn’t going to make things
any better for this and other Lithgow
owners —is likely, indeed, to make them much
worse. Also the co. has put a lot of money
lately into brickworks ; and it is very ques-
tionable whether that branch of the business
is going to flourish until we get over the
effects of the recent construction boom.
Sydney is overstocked with brickworks, any-
way. In the long run, however, any owner
of coal can hardly avoid making a lot ot
money. Money in the “long run” isn t what
most people are after, though. Still, even in
the immediate future some profits should be
made; and the co., besides having tremen-
dous coal reserves, is in a nice, easy financial
position, and well able to pay out most or
what profit it does make. This shows that
and one or two other things: —

This co. was formed in 1914 to take over
the businesses carried on in N. S. Wales
and Queensland by lleiron and Smith and
Aleock’s, who were manufacturers of billiard
tables and billiard-room appointments.
Shortly afterwards, to spread the standing
charges, it was decided to go into general
furnishings, and the purchase of the business
of Sherwood, Ltd., carried the co. into wall-
papers and house-decoration. Some day an
interesting story will be told of a young
insurance clerk who, losing his job in Adel-
aide through an amalgamation, dropped off
a boat at Flinders-street, Melbourne, turned
into the first decent shop he met and asked
if it wanted an agent in Adelaide. The
shop happened to be Whitelaw’s. The young
man, who had been trained in a literary
society, persuaded Whitelaw’s, and an agency
was opened in Adelaide. Two days or so
after the young man who didn’t know any of
the technicalities of the business had put
up the sign, Hugh It. Denison called and
wanted his big house decorated by the new
firm. The young man who didn’t know how
superintended the decoration of that house,
and when a satisfied client had enthusiasti-
cally got him further orders, he thought it
time to put himself right. Whereupon Deni-
son said, in effect, “Young man, I’m not
.agoing to lose sight of you.” The young man
was A. C. Sherwood ; and presumably because
Sherwood had become Sherwood, Ltd., and
agent for Alcock’s in N. S. Wales, H. It.
Denison is chairman of directors of this co.,
of which A. C. Sherwood is managing direc-
tor ; and a good deal of the £113,664 of
capital in the business is understood to be
Denison and Dixson money.
The co. is pretty heavily loaded with
goodwill, no less than £60,000 in shares hav-
ing been paid to the various firms that were
bought out. This gives only £53,664 of
capital for stock and premises and to
finance the business; and that isn’t enough
for a business which sells a lot of stock on
terms. Hence as at March 31 the co. owed
£22,368; and its assets (apart from good-
will) were stock, £20,882; book debts,
£45,525; premises. £13,996; and plant and
' sundries £5537. The premises include free-
holds at Alexandria (Sydney) and Bris-
bane ; the former a factory, the latter ware-
house and billiard-tournament hall. These
additions have been in progress during the
year, and have represented several thou-
sands of unproductive capital. Further,
when comparing the profits for the two
years, it has to be remembered that, apart
from war and other circumstances of that
kind, the first year did not carry any bur-
den of income tax. As an energetic Aus-
tralian concern, busy in producing high-
grade articles which have been very largely
imported hitherto, and keen on gradually
increasing the range of its manufactures,
Heiron and Smith, Ltd., earns this paper’s
blessing.
* * * *
The asbestos deposits at Macquarie Har-
bor (Tas.) are being worked a little, and
as asbestos is scarce these days there ought
to be a chance of developing them and the
other deposits near Beaconsfield. The fibre
of the Macquarie Harbor asbestos is short,
but otherwise is said to be good.
The Inky Way.
“The Moth” : Members of the Australian
Journalists’ Association have lately been
agitating themselves over the question of
instituting a diploma of journalism. The
publication in the Australian Journalist
of details of a Maoriland University diploma
started a debate which has been ploughing
its way through the Association’s paper.
The authors of the M.L. certificate appar-
ently held the view that a fairly
sound knowledge of history, economics,
sociology, literature, languages, human
nature, psychology, mathematics, the world,
the flesh and the Devil is required
before a man can chronicle the doings of the
Marine Board with that accuracy and ele-
gance for which our moderately great dailies
are famous. But the qualifications men-
tioned would appear to be only a few of those
required in a really good reporter. One
proprietor, writing to the Journalist, men-
tions that “the journalist ought to be a
reader and a lover of good books, familiar
with the panorama of history (even the
history of to-day), thought and politics,” and
another gent, demands “tact and good nature,
push and energy, imagination and quick sym-
pathy, and a strong sense of humor.” Add
to these a knowledge of shorthand, a nose
for news, a digestion that can put up with
irregular hours (or quarter-hours) for meals,
a thirst which the owner is able to regulate
despite continual invitations to comeanave-
one, a genius for remembering the faces and
initials of persons of no importance, and 44
other qualifications, and the possessor would
probably be regarded as a capable senior
reporter—one of the breed that a number
of bosses consider grossly overpaid at £6 10s.
a week.
“Tasman”: The Australasian Journal-
ist has been filled with arguments for the last
three months about the wisdom of establish-
ing an Australian diploma in journalism, as
was recently done in Maoriland. Proprie-
tors, professors, pressmen, a Chief Justice,
educationists and sundry others have been
dragged into the controversy, but not much
light has been shed so far. Opinions differ
widely as to the subjects that ought to be
included in the qualifying exam. The M.L.
paper, set by M. C. Keane, M.A., associate-
editor of the Press, required the candidate
to reach the B.A. standard in Modern His-
tory, Economics, Logic and umpteen other
subjects of the Arts course, and in addition
demanded a knowledge of statistical method,
the working of a newspaper and the prin-
ciples of newsgetting. The opposition says
that “wliat’s good enough for the old man s
good enough for me,” and alleges that jour-
nalism can be learnt only by sleeping on a
paper and drinking printers’ ink. ' This is
partly true, of course; but the contention
misses the point. Press-work can be learnt
only on the press, but the 1 starter who has
had a useful Uni. education is going-to find
the track a whole heap easier than the
other fellow. Other things being equal, he’s
going to turn out far better “specials,” his
promotion will be far more rapid, and he
will collect a bigger salary and raise the
status of the profession;—and not before it’s
needed.
“C. 8.”: I laugh scornfully at the claim
by “D.” (B. 3/8/T6) that a Brisbane press-
man who has been on Government rounds
continuously for 16 years holds an Aus-
tralian record. P. J. Bennett, of Melbourne
Herald, can beat him by nine years.
“Paddy” has looked after the State depart-
ments continuously since 1891, and was
identified closely with them for several years
before that. His reminiscences would fill
seventeen (17) large volumes, including a
couple with asbestos covers. He can supply
inside information of how Tommy Bent
came to — : well, perhaps it would be as well
not to mention that. “Paddy” has been
chairman of the State Parliament gallery
committee for* seven years, and was lately
elected chairman of the Victorian district of
the A..T.A.
Amiable Andrew Gerrand has left the
Argus to sub-edit the Northern Times,
Newcastle’s evening infant. One of the
props of the A.J.A. in Melbourne, Gerrand
was lately chairman of the Press Club house-
committee, and could always be depended on
to give an extra shove in the pressman’s
interest any time it seemed needed.
The first printing machine in M.L. turned
up in Christ’s College, Christchurch, the
other day. It is a small, ricketty hand-
press, and was brought to the Bay of
Islands in 1835 by William Colenso, who
came out as printer to the missionaries and
afterwards set up in the regular Gospel busi-
ness himself. Colenso had a lively time of
it with his first “devils,” wild Maori boys
who used to tattoo each other with ink from
the roller.
* * * *
They could soon settle the war in Broken
Hill. Barrier Truth has a cheap, quick
and, except for the capitalist, easy way of
ending it: —
If there is a 100 per cent, mortality amongst the
capitalists, we shall hear no more of conscription or
of the war.
Certainly it is kind enough to add that this
is not meant as an intimation that the
season for shooting mineowners and land-
lords is now open.
“D.F.” : It isn’t all pie being the official
organ of Labor in the Speck. The Hobart
Daily Post has that honor, and it was
observed at the recent political Labor Con-
ference that, though large amounts had been
written off, the various Labor leagues still
owed about £250 for advts. and seemed
quite likely to go on owing.
Dead at Melbourne, aged 44, Robert
Mc-Coll, at one time proprietor of the
Drouin Express. On leaving Gippsland he
went to the Speck and started the North-
Eastern Advertiser at Scottsdale, a sheet
that was hung out to dry twice weekly. His
health became very bad some five years ago,
so he sold out to A. E. Osborne, the present
proprietor, and took himself back to Vic.
He passed out in hospital after a long-
acquaintance with the Enemy.
* * * *
“Feng-shin”: It is not generally known
—or perhaps it is forgotten—that Louis
Raemaekers, the Dutch cartoonist, was as
fiercely pro-Boer and anti-British during
the Boer war as he is now pro-Ally and
anti-Hun. His earlier cartoons, which for
obvious reasons were not republished across
the Channel, were a terrible indictment of
Britain. His art was less mature then, but
his passion of scorn and sarcasm just as
biting. His attitude during the Boer war
and his attitude to-day are, of course, per-
fectly consistent. Not love of England but
detestation of wrong inspires him.
“Sardonyx”: I worked once with a sub
who, unlike “Full Point’s” man (B.
27/7/’l6), was a ruthless slayer of journal-
ese. With him, “an inebriated individual
pursuing a serpentine course in the direction
of his domicile” became “a drunk staggering
homewards.” From him the half-educated
reporters learnt that people received injuries
—not “sustained” them; that they didn’t
“witness” an accident, they saw it; that,
though you may give a welcome, you cannot
“extend” one ; that, instead of “partaking” of
a dinner, you eat it; that to say a thing is
not necessarily to “state” it; that a written
order is a “verbal order,” though it isn’t an
oral one ; that an effect is owing to a cause,
not “due” to it, and that to expunge is
not, as “F.F.” himself puts it to “expur-
gate”—it’s the remaining stuff that has been
expurgated.
* * * *
“Cuisine”: One of our helpful papers
recently published another of those
“economy” articles which keep the Bank-
ruptcy Court idle. It was headed :
THE POSSIBILITIES OF LEFT-OVER FOOD.
SOME POINTS IN ECONOMY FOR THE COOK.
Here are some of the “possibilities” :—•
Put uncooked meat through the chopper twice-
Add salted peanuts, canned tomatoes, an egg, celery,
onions, horseradish, butter and herbs.
Left-over strawberries, cherries, pineapples, etc.
Serve in marmalade in puff pastry.
Left-over game. Press next day for luncheon.
These others, I understand, are to appear
later:—
Poultry.—Don’t throw away the livers. Take a
dozen, grind them fine, mix with pickled walnuts
and olives ; bake in a brisk oven. Serve with cream
sauce.
Don't throw away champagne. Any good wine
(Sauterne, Chablis, etc.) may be used for tipsy cakes
or trifles. Left-over wedding cake may be used up
this way.
Many people are wasteful with dessert —almonds
and raisins, for instance> Any good chef can find a
way to use up raisins, and almonds may at least be
devilled.
It is these little economies which will bind
our Empire closer and strafe the hated Hun,
who has nothing but mere food to eat.
“Spell-oh”: That deplorable publication,
The Australasian Journalist, which has
just reached members of the A.J.A. on the
Eastern coast, has a note (“A Federal
Appointment”) regarding the holiday trip
to U.S.A. for a journalist with the fake
Industrial Commission. No hint is given
as to who is to have the job, so I suppose
I’ll have to tell you myself—or perhaps
you’d better ask B. S. B. Cook, of Mel-
bourne Herald. He knew weeks ago.

“Copy Boy”: The Argus dipped its pen in
superior blue-black ink and remarked
severely
The efficiency movement finds little favor with
the Trades Hall. Only by the narrow majority of
seven votes in a meeting of 74 members was it
decided to give approval to the decision of the Federal
Government to send a Commission composed of em-
ployers and employees to America to inquire, as to
the methods of manufacture there, with a view to
ascertaining how Australian production may be in-
creased.
And when the Federal Government asked the
Australian Journalists’ Association to for-
ward the names of three of its members from
whom the Government might select a journal-
ist to accompany the Commission, the Collins-
street daily patriot said, in effect: “On
account of the war we cannot spare any mem-
ber of our staff.” That is how much prac-
tical favor the “efficiency movement” found
with the Argus.
THE IMPRISONED POET.
(AFTER SEIDL.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And if the tyrant blind mine eye
Can he blot out the glorious sun?
And if in dungeon dark 1 die
Is Freedom’s battle therefore done?
And if in fight I fainting fall
With shattered shield and shivered spear,
Yet at the cry, the clarion call,
New heroes rise, new helms appear.
And though my voice be lost to-day
And fail upon the martyr’s pyre,
Think ye less loud will swell the lay
When Freedom leads her glorious choir?
Say ye, “Right lies for ever crushed” ?
Hope ye no more will souls aspire
Because the soaring song ye’ve hushed
And silver sound of one sweet lyre?
Think not sweet spring with balmy breath
And budding life and love will fail
Because ye raised the hand of death
And slew —one gentle nightingale 1
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
UNINSPIRED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It has now been decided that alcoholic drinks
hall not be served in the refreshment-rooms of
J.S.W. Parliament House after 6 p.m.
It used to be a pleasant thing
To sit aloft and hear
The epigrams they loved to fling:
“Say that outside 1 You curl You cow!’’
The member for the Dingo Flats
When full of hops would sit and scream,
“Yous blokes have all got blanky rats!”
Such things will be a vanished dream.
To what dull depths our House will sink
When quiet is the atmosphere, _ w
And no one yells, “Gaw-strike-me-pink!
To get thrown out upon his ear.
K.S.W.
