POLITICAL
POINTS
A “national committee” met in Melbourne
recently and drew up an anti-conscription
manifesto; and the worst it could say about
its bogey was that, while in principle con-
scription was an instrument of national
defence, in practice it was an instrument
of working-class subjugation. Whenever the
asses who lead the lions of the Labor party
are short of an argument they use big
words; and “working-class subjugation” is
a sample. If in principle something is good
but in practice it is bad, plainly those who
are responsible for putting it into practice
are to blame. What the “national com-
mittee” says in effect is: “Conscription is
all right. It is an instrument of national
defence. But the Labor party which is in
power is a traitor and a fraud, and it would
use conscription to tie the hands of the
very people it should protect and hand them
over to the enemy.” That is what the
argument of the “national committee”
amounts to!. It isn’t an attack upon con-
scription. It is an attack upon the Labor
Government.
NO COMPULSION.
ANTI-CONSCRIPTION UNION PRESIDENT:
“You can either join the union or
starve. D’you think we're going to
fight for an extra bob a 'week and let
you get the benefit of it, if you don’t
join in and lend a hand?”
In a country where voluntary enlistment
is the law any old thing which is liable to
embarrass a party Government —any adverse
criticism of any act of administration—may
be suppressed on the ground that it is
“prejudicial to recruiting.” With universal
service a Government has no excuse for
applying this handy gag to its critics. Hence
there has been far more liberty of speech m
France than in Australia. The anti-con-
scriptionists profess to be champions of
liberty. Yet their policy produces more
repression and suppression than conscription
docs.
There has been a lot of mystery about
the movements of Hugh Mahon, Minister for
External Affairs. First he was and then
ho wasn’t going to England as Australian
representative to the Irish National Con-
ference. The Bulletin doesn’t know
where Mahon is going, but it knows where
an Irish Nationalist can’t stay and retain
his self-respect, and that is in the Hughes-
Pearce Government.
The anti-eonscriptionist of Australia must
feel pretty cheap and mean when he reflects
that he is loafing on the conscripts of France
and Russia and Britain. There are not
many men with nerve enough to condemn
lifeboats at the very moment when a life-
boat crew is fishing them half-drowned out
of the water. But the anti-eonscriptionist
manages to do it.
DOING IT ON PURPOSE.
BILLJIM : “ 'Elio. male, hare ych lost a leg?”
THE ANZAC: “Now — I'm malingering.”
Thus Vida *Goldstein, president of the
Women’s Political Ass. of Victoria-
Compulsory methods are morally indefensible,
because they legalise vice and encourage immorality
in men by professing to offer them protection against
disease.
Presumably Vida Goldstein, like the wowser,
would encourage morality in men by offering
them no protection against disease- And if
they are, then, not moral —well, damn the
children, that’s all! Pretty, gentle, womanly
gospel, isn’t it?
The S. M. Herald thinks there should be
no need for legislation to prevent the killing
of female cattle, in view of the obvious ad-
vantages of keeping them when the demand
is likely to be so good. But the cautious,
canny, well-to-do cattle-owners who . look
ahead are not the people such legislation is
wanted for. Every day good cows, often in
calf, are being sold to the butcher by
country people who cannot resist the temp-
tation of the prices offered, and half of these
vendors know their action is indefensible.
Practically everyone recognises that it is a
bad thing to drink too much, so no legisla-
tion should be necessary to stop people from
being foolish and incidentally working
national evil by over-drinking. Yet the
Herald supported six o’clock closing, didn’t
it?
In spite of the tremendous increase in
factory machinery and in the price
of raw materials and finished pro-
ducts, actually a less value of the latter is
now turned out for each £lOO of wages paid
than was the case in 1904. In that year in
Victoria it cost £2O 2s. IGkl. to turn out
£lOO worth of finished 'goods; now. though
£lOO. worth of finished goods represent a
much smaller bulk, the labor cost is £2l 4s.
It isn’t that wages are too high. They are
still much under the United Stati-s standard.
But the output is too small, and is evidently
getting smaller.
This is how the N. S. Wales Public Service
Board does its little bit: —
Teachers of Small Country Schools or of Assistants
in Country Schools or of Junior Assistants wanted.
Applicants must be unmarried and between the ages
of 16 and 35 years.
If it is untrained men the Department is
after, surely it might find scores in the
Returned Soldiers’ Assoc. Perhaps they
might not all be as good scholars as some
of the stay-at-homes between 16 and 35; but
geography and grammar are not all 'that are
needed in a teacher.
A Maoriland paper quotes as “an inter-
esting item showing the march of British
progress in Samoa” a paragraph in the
Samoan Times that that journal, which for
the last 16 years had been carried on by
Mr. Emil Tuebke, would “henceforth be run
by Mr. .las. Ah Sue.” British progress is
expected to march to some queer words of
command.
The Victorian Ministry proposes to do
Little Jim a good turn. At present when a
child is born out of wedlock and the
parents afterwards marry, only the father
can make application to have the youngster
proclaimed legitimate. To meet the cases
in which the fathers are soldiers on active
service or have perhaps been killed, the bill
provides that the mothers shall also have
the right to make the application about the
]it tie thing Bill left behind him. This will
have its practical uses as well as its senti-
mental value. For instance, legitimation
will give Little-Jim a chance for a pension.
There is more worry for John 11. Cann,
N.S.W. Works Minister. Under the Act
municipalities may vote “reasonable' ex-
penses” to oue delegate to the Local Govern-
ment Conference. Cast year the Broken
Hill Council sent two, and the ratepayers
got the bill for £9O. Lately the Depart-
mental Examiner declared the expenditure
“unreasonable” and charged it back to. the
aldermen who voted it. Now Cann arrives.
The aldermen have the right of appeal to
the Minister, who happens to represent
Broken Hill in the Assembly. If an appeal
is turned down it’s certain the circumstance
won’t make some of the Labor aldermen
love Cann anv more than they do now ; and
if it is upheld the ratepayers will say louder
than ever that Cann is prepared to crawl
through any mud in order to get back to
Parliament.
Old Bill Lyne, who represented a coun-
try district all his days, once had an idea
of offering a prize for a design for a fran-
chise that would give the hungry empty
spaces of Australia a chance. Bill knew
that where the votes were gathered together
there would the politician’s heart be; and
he reckoned that was no good-to Australia.
The Northern Territory is one striking in-
stance of the neglect of a vastly important
area because it is bare of votes. Now
Adelaide is developing another. A few
years ago S. Aus. built at great expense an
outer harbor; and the harbor, though it
attracts the big shipping, hasn’t attracted
population. But Port Adelaide, which lies
up at the end of a creek and has the inter-
state trade, has the population; so the
vested interests of Port Adelaide reckon
that th(> Outer Harbor is a failure and that
tin' Port Adelaide creek ought to be scooped
out a few feet deeper. Wluit the voter thinks
the politician of course thinks; so it won’t
be long before S. Aus. borrows a bit more
in order to knock the bottom out of a pre-
vious loan work.
Maoriland is tackling venereal disease —-
at last. It ought to make a good job of it.
The country is singularly well situated for
purposes of cleanliness, and prostitution is
comparatively limited. But is is not going
to succeed by loud talk. If Parliament, as
seems likely, baulks at notification and
segregation, then it will be only playing with
the thing. Meanwhile the syphilitic condi-
tions at. the Cook Islands are so gross as to
suggest that Maoriland’s present anxiety
about the front garden could with advantage
be extended to the backyard.
HER GAITERS.
LONDON. — The English Church Union is organising
opposition to women preachers. The Bishop of
Oxford and the Dean of Canterbury also oppose the
idea. The latter states that an innovation of the
kind would raise a most vehement convulsion in the
Church. — Cable.
Well, what's wrong with giving a few lady
bishops a trial —just to see how they
shape?
A lot -of economy is. being practised
somewhere. A number of Commonwealth
public servants have gone to the war, and
their positions are being kept open for them ;
yet there were 567 more permanent Com-
monwealth employees on June 30. 1016,
than on the same date of 1914. Concurrently
the wages bill increased by £234,534. Both
ends of the candle are flaring all right.
CAIRO, KIN AND RAD LUCK.
S TIGGINS: "I am told that Cairo is a most
sinful city."
RETURNED SOLDIER: " My good sir, it is so
awful it made me tarn pale. And, mind
yon . I had had luck—I wasn't there long
enough to see all there was to see.”
An indignant patriot named Koid was
charged in Melbourne the other day with
throwing things at the son of his sister who
had married a German named liolil. The
angry uncle complained that his nephew had
removed his original German moniker from
his shop front in favor of the name of Keid
which his good Scotch-Australian mother was
horn to. Why this furious objectionV Any
man who is as much British as German is
surely entitled to drop the name that asso-
ciated him with enemy nationality in this
country, provided he has no intention of
picking up the abandoned inheritance after
the war. It is more than his right: it is
the Government’s duty to provide him with
facilities for taking this first step towards
a complete severance of the old Ilun connec-
tion.
The lately-formed Farmers’ Union of
Victoria threatens vaguely to do a sensible
thing. It claims that too high a commission
has been allowed to agents by those control-
ling the wheat pool, and hints that its own
numerous branches will next season apply
for agencies, “thus saving thousands of
pounds to the wheat-growers.” But there
isn’t any Government standing in the way
of the farmers co-operatively handling a
dozen other things; and what are they
doing about themf Bags for next season’s
crop, for instance ?
The Commonwealth Government is dis-
covering that its brilliant idea of sending a
Commission to the U.S.A. to find out all
about everything has a back kick in it, after
all. Pearce asked the Launceston Chamber
of Commerce to nominate somebody to
handle the paper-making industry, and now
Hobart yells that of course it should' have
the nomination, because it is older and has
more people who sell three-penn’orth of
stationery. The whole business is a time-
and money-wasting -farce; another good
proof of that is given by the fact that, desir-
ing to get points is the desiccation of vege-
tables, the Iluon apple-driers are sending to
California , a man who knows exactly what
it is that the local people want to know.
More Sosh, and in the very place where
the raging tiger was understood to have
frightened the life out of every citizen. The
State’s “lucerne pool” has been such a suc-
cess —the grass having realised the equiva-
lent of £3 15s. per ton in Melbourne —that
these Rodney farmers lately persuaded
Water Commission Chairman Cattanach up
there to make arrangements for repeating
the dose next season.
Allan Cameron, member for Dalhousie, has
been complaining in the Vic. Assembly that
the State Savings Bank won’t join in boost-
ing up the price of farming land :—-
In Victoria -we have a State Savings Bank which
never lends money to anybody who can't get it
anywhere else. The Credit Foncier branch has experi-
enced valuers who value properties at half their
worth and then lend 50 or GO per cent, of that sum.
In other words agricultural land has been
boomed from SO to 100 per cent, above what
experienced valuers regard as its true value;
and Allan Cameron complains that the
Credit Foncier doesn’t use the people’s
money to help sustain these artificial values,
which are a deadly burden on people who
want to get land to use.
SUMMER DAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“it is good lighting weather.” — Cable.
Bright shines the sun and all the skies are gay ;
Behind the lines the fields are harvest white;
Small birds amid the merry branches play ;
The happy streamlet gurgles with delight.
Life is a song. It's burden seems a feather....
Kush for the trenches! It is fighting weather!
A little wind creeps up from the warm south ;
The new-mown hay is sweet in all the field.
Summer, with happy kisses on her mouth,
The laughing garden-squares with bloom has sealed.
Bees drone about their scented honey-making. . . .
Plunge into Hell! These villages want taking!
Boar upon roar- the thunder of the guns !
The stench and fume of battle loads the air.
From hill to hill each rifle-volley runs.
The earth, instead of flowers, has blood to wear.
See! Overhead the planes of death are winging....
Hark! How the speckle-throated thrush is singing!
The laundress spreads her linen on the line.
Sleek cattle drowse beneath the beechen green.
Far off, the quiet convent’s windows shine.
The village roofs through haze of gold are seen. . . .
Ask the grim, crouching, rattled Teuton whether
He thinks these August days fine fighting weather!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

PROBATE JURISDICTION.
In the Estate of THOMAS CONDON late of 264,
Castlereagh Street, Sydney in the State of New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, of
Independent Means, deceased intestate.
PURSUANT to the Wills Probate and Administration
Act 1898 NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that all
creditors, next-of-kin, and other persons having any
debt, claim or demand against upon or affecting - the
Estate of the abovenamed deceased (who died at
Gladesville, near Sydney in the State of New South
Wales, Australia, on or about the twenty-first day of
July One thousand nine hundred and thirteen intes-
tate) and Letters of Administration of whose Estate
were granted by the Supreme Court of New South Wales
in its Probate Jurisdiction on the sixteenth day of
October One thousand nine hundred and thirteen to
Mary McQuillen of Narrandera in the said State (a
sister of the said deceased) are hereby required to
send in particulars of their claims to the said Mary
McQuillen in care of the undersigned, Kershaw,
Matthews and Lane, the Proctors for the said
Administratrix, at their address hereunder mentioned,
or to the said Proctors at their said address on or
before the twenty-eighth day of February next, at
the expiration of which time the said Mary
•McQuillen will proceed to distribute the assets of
the said deceased amongst the persons entitled
thereto, having regard to the debts and claims only
of which she shall then have had notice. AND
NOTICE IS HEREBY FURTHER GIVEN that the
said Mary McQuillen will not be liable for the
assets so distributed or any part thereof to any
person of whose debt, claim, or demand she shall
not have had notice at the time of such distribution.
The said Thomas Condon was a son of John Condon,
late of Gurtlimore near Thurles in the County of
Tipperary, Ireland, and of Catherine Condon (for-
merly Catherine McNally) his wife, both of whom
died prior to the year One thousand eight hundred
and seventy, leaving certain children (other than
the said Thomas Condon and Mary McQuillen) then
surviving.
Dated this eighth day of August, 1916.
KERSHAW, MATTHEWS & LANE,
Proctors for the said Administratrix,
Ocean House, Moore Street,
Sydney, New South Wales.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
There was great racing at Caulfield and a
great crowd on Australian Steeplechase day. In the
first event, over the little sticks, the placed horses
were separated by heads only, Becassine’s head being
in front. In the next race a rank outsider, getting
away with the lead, lasted just long enough to beat
John Wren’s good thing by half a head. Later on,
Wedge won the Malakoff Stakes by a head. In this
case, Caracre, the favorite, was expected to justify
the rash punters who had taken a short price about
him for Caulfield Cup. The long-talked-of animal
blighted all hopes by breaking down. The last item
on the bill was a seven-furlong flutter which Sol
Green’s horse Vibratory won easily. The odd thing
was that he started at 10 to 1 with little Dempsey
up. Only a few weeks ago Dempsey had earned a
reputation for getting home in the last race of a
meeting. And now the punters allowed him 'to go
out at 10 to 1 when he was riding for astute Solo-
mon Green !
Once in a way the public and the prophets get a
sharp reminder that no form in steeplechasing is so
trustworthy as the most recent form, and no old
stager can be said to be properly prepared for a
four-mile steeple unless he has lately been put over
numerous fences at racing pace. The spectacular
event was practically a long struggle between Ungala
(9st. 71b.), Bonnie George (9st. 101 b.) and West-
away (10st.). The top-weight, Booligal, headed
Ungala for about 2i miles, but he was clearly a poor
imitation of his old self, and he gradually tired to
nothing. Bonnie George had the most pace of the
three light-weights, but his final dash could not be
sustained. Ungala wore him down and won by a
neck.
The winner’s staying ftowers had been under-rated,
for no sufficient reasons. Thus it came to pass that
he was a 25 to 1 chance, like Bonnie George, whereas
Westaway was backed at 8 to 1. The public gave
no heed to latest form. A week previously Ungala
had beaten Bonnie George in a two-mile steeple at the
same difference in weights. Three weeks previously
Westaway had beaten Busra on the same terms
as now, and Busra finished fourth, behind Westaway.
All four horses had done plentv of jumping within
the past month, and were fully practised for this
journey. On the other hand, Booligal and Highland
Clan had shaped disappointingly, and their backers
should have lost faith. Also Rendrock had come to
grief at an easy jump since running second in the
Grand National,' so it was not surprising that he fell
again. Glenquinn, Battleship and Admiral Grand
were other fancied ones that fell early in the race.
It was Battleship’s third attempt to win this most
trying of steeplechases without having been wisely
prepared for it. Twice he made a fatal blunder. The
other time he won, because the pace was not too
fast and there was no occasion to bustle the little
chap.
# * * *
Melbourne racing clubs are combining not
to grant Ikey a fielder’s license when he is eligible to
enlist. Laudable, perhaps, but of doubtful legality
and questionable equity in a country where volun-
taryism is the basis of public policy. To be con-
sistent the clubs should apply the same kind of rule
to the club, member and the man who pays for
admission at the gate. But not they!
* * * *
,T. H. Davis, appointed handicapper to the
V.A.T.C., was one of the V.R.C. stipes. He loses
£lOO a year, but relishes handicapping more than
stiping. There were 31 applicants for the job.
Davis has been associated with racing from boyhood
and trained and owned several good winners, includ-
ing Fortunatus.
*■ * * *
“Sheila”: Xavier College, Kew (Vic.), got
a worldly testimonial on Saturday when Ungala won
the Australian Steeple. Justin Curr, who prepared
the animal, was a student there before he became a
bank clerk and took to hunting. So, too, was
Charley Leonard, owner of Westawa.v, which ran
third. John O’Keefe, who brought Traquair to Aus-
tralia, and his brothers who composed the trinity of
“J. E. Stanley,” also learned to do sums there.
* * * *
Mentioned at the annual meeting of the
V.R.C. that £72,340 was given in stakes during
the year. The Canterbury (M.L.) Jockey Club gave
£37,840. The V.R.C. knows MacShortodds; the
C.J.C. knows the tote. Population: Melbourne,
600,000 ; Christchurch, 60,000. With the tote it
pays the owner very well even if he runs straight.
Bar Von reduces the prize money and thus encour-
ages the owner to go crooked.
* * * »
Punters were on the winning marble again
at Canterbury Park (Sydney) on Saturdav, but Ikey
had the odds cramped and took little risk. Of the
six winners one paid evens, one 2’s, two If to 1,
one 21 to 1, and the other 4’s. There were 51
starters. This writer does not know what the odds
are against a lunatic picking all the winners at a
meeting, but on Saturday anyone who did it with a
pound would have acquired £lB 10s., which is pro-
bably about a twentieth of the odds against him
doing the same thing next time. Which is just
where MacShortodds finds salvation. When excep-
tional luck comes the way of the punter, what he
wins is not a circumstance alongside what he loses
when the fickle commodity goes in another direction.
*-*■**
Maoriland owner H. O. Taylor brought
three nags across to Sydney some months ago, Zuland,
Bee and Spangle. They have all paid for their oats.
Spangle was the last of the bunch to be let loose,
and on Saturday she won Canterbury Park Flying
Handicap easilv. But she carried only 7st. 31b., and
was running in a very ordinary field even for a
suburban course. Hats have to be raised to the
people behind the gun for the manner in which the
three animals have been placed. Every time money
talked the mokes arrived. Zuland has captured a
brace of events and Bee and Spangle one apiece. But it
is not likelv that Spangle will ever capture anything
big, as she is light and on the small side. ■
* * * *
Something like a record was put up at
the Ascot (Sj«ney) course one day last week, when
in the interests of horse-breeding and national effi-
ciency 17 scrambles occurred. It demanded an
almost continuous all-day procession. Moses com-
menced to bellow at 11.30 and was going strong at
sundown. One punter at least had a thrill. In
the opening event he took a long bet about Miss
Ariki. The lady duly arrived, and the backer had
just decided how to invest the coin when down
came the nag’s number on account of her weight
being wrong. Another lady (Idle Girl) was
declared the winner. But Moses didn’t object. Both
beasts had been quoted at 100 to 3 or thereabouts.
“Wart”: Wliat is the record age for a
horse? The real, certificated age, not the age which
the Oldest Inhabitant thinks a prad he used to own
in his boyhood lived to. Secretary W. T. Doolan, of
the Northern Tasmanian Trotting Club, had -a mare
Lena, which died last week, aged 28 years at least.
Lena, ridden by J. McClutehey, won the first pony
trot at Garrick 25 years ago.
* * * *
“Squib”: When “Sassafras” (B. 29/6/’l6)
says the modern horse is inferior to the horse of
bygone times he ought to have something more than
the performance of two prads in the long- ago to
support his condemnation of the Australian horse of
to-day. Four years last November I rode into
Normanton (Q.), a distance of 180 miles, in
two days, driving a pack-horse in front of me
with a good pack up. One was a saddle
pony, 14 hands, four years old; and my pack-
horse a 7-year-old bug’gy mare, 14.1. Both had been
spelling for four months, and were not shod. Also
the grass was pretty scarce. Although this does not
beat “Sassafras’s” ancient performance it is one as
good. And it was not anything out of the common
among modern performances. There are any number
of Queensland horses that have done better.
* * * *
“Sardonyx” : The death of the famous old
show-performer Desmond suggests a word about
jumping-horses in general. The steeplechase was
originally a cross-country event for bona fide
hunters. It was a better test of horse and rider
than the modern laid-out course, because the man
had to pick his line, and the horse had to jump
whatever turned up. The present-day ’chaser flies
everything with almost no steadying, and is into his
pace again the moment he lands. He is, in fact,
not a jumper that races, but a racer that jumps.
As regards mere jumping, irrespective of pace, some
of the best performers have been quite innocent of
pedigree; yet one of the best cross-country horses I
ever saw was a son of Hermit, out of an equally
blue-blooded mare. I remember his being heavily
backed to gallop 21 miles and jump 21 “fair hunt-
ing-fences” in an hour. He did it in 57J minutes.
E. MOY ( Yank, lightweight) after stopping
a few.
Owing to a dispute about weight, Jack
Cole (lOst. 71b.) was substituted for Llew Edwards
against the ’Murean boxer Moy (lOst.) at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday. As the meeting failed to
arrive at a decision Arthur Scott was called upon
to deliver judgment. He pronounced for Cole. It
looked like a judgment which might be upset on
appeal to the High Court. But Moy didn’t deserve
to win, anyhow, mainly because he boxed without
brains and delivered his hits with the emphasis of a
canary. Cole was not much more intelligent, but
he brought a hefty wallop into the argument and
kept the other fellow respectfully jumping away
from it most of the time. Cole may be heard from
again. But unless he improves he is most likely to
be heard of as a loser. Moy's mouth seemed likely
to be a sore point with him for a day or two.
* * * " *
Much chastened in spirit, Arlos Fanning
was given another chance against Bert Spargo on
Saturday night at Melbourne Stadium. The Yank’s
schedule ran this way: Round I—6, overcome nerves
and warm up ; 7—lo, test Spargo’s wallop on jaw ;
11- —14, distribute bother; 15 —20, produce coma. It
was all carried out bar the coma, which Spargo and
the timekeeper beat in turns. Through the first half
Spargo tore in hurriedly, landing three wallops to
Fanning’s one in the place where it wasn’t expected.
When America’s contributions began to roll in Spargo
quietened down considerably, but was always pre-
pared with unpleasantness and a hop out of danger.
Why the referee anointed Fanning neither Fanning
nor anyone else seemed to know. Even in the 19th
interval the voice of Israel had been heard cry-
ing “Take 10 to von about Fanning.” Fanning and
Edwards will draw well now. #
The first professional pug in Australia
able to make money at the game has enlisted Les
Darcy. This young man has been doing very wel<
since the war burst out and can go to the Front with
the easy consciousness that when the scrap ends uj,
he will not have to look for a job or bother niuct
whether he never gets one. The claim that tht
world’s middleweight championship belongs to Darcj
is of course, untenable, but he most likely is tin
champ., all the same. Before taking ship for k ranee
or somewhere he will probably have time and libeitj
to argue with Mr. George Chip, of the U.S.A., whi
arrived in Sydney yesterday, and with Mr. Jamei
Clabby, who wants another go. #
Georges Charpentier, the French heavy-
weight who is serving with the aero corps at Verdun,
has, so it is said on what looks like reliable evi-
dence, lately declined an offer of £20,000 to box
one 20-round bout and two exhibition contests m
Buenos Aires. To the Argentine “sport” who
attempted to “promote” the matches, Georges re-
marked: “You could not tempt me with a million.
While my friends and countrymen are falling around
me, I would under no consideration ever think of
entering the ring again. I fight for France, not
for money.” It’s probably true. Thank God for
France!
.* * * *
Killed in the Big Fight: C. A. Boone,
captain and coach, Mosman (Sydney) Rowing Club;
Sampson, motor-cyclist and sculler; T. Lyons, a
top-notch cvclist. Dr. C. J. Tozer, who wielded a
scoring bat for Sydney ’Varsity, has been wounded.
When it was reported last year that Tozer had been
killed first-grade clubs sported black.
Billy Read, who died at Sydney last week,
was one of the best-known sailing skippers in Port
Jackson. Back in the ’Bo’s he went before the wind
in pursuit of prize-money, but the victory which
cheered him most was his success with Esmeralda
among the inter-State 22-footers in ’97. In later
years he skipped the 18ft. Arawa, and usually induced
that craft to get the last push a breeze was capable
of giving. *
Death came as. a kind release to Phil
Newland, at 41. He was a son of old Simpson New-
land, and was probably the most brilliant all-round
athlete S. Aus. has produced—a very fast runner,
inter-State footballer, and even more brilliant at
lacrosse,’ a sound bat, and wicketkeeper for S.A. for
years. He went to England with the 1902 XI. It
was a fatal trip. At deck-cricket on the voyage he
received a smashing blow in the face with the ball,
at close quarters. He never was quite the same man
again, and the paralysis which set in gradually, a
few years later, was presumably due to that old
cause. While his health lasted he was law-partner
of Fred Young, lately Attorney-General of S.A., and
now its Agent-General in London.
Passed out while doing his bit in France,
Capt. Ivor Margetts, one of the best-known of Tas-
manian footballers. He was for several years a
leading player for Launceston, and _ afterwards
represented the southern end of the island in rep. com-
bats. Margetts, who had made several ineffectual
attempts to score a seat in the island Assembly, got
into khaki at the start of the scrap and went right
through the campaign at Gallipoli. On one occa-
sion, with uniform nearly blown off, he was the
only officer of his (Company to come out of the pro-
ceedings on his feet. On the field and off he was a
dinkum Australian, and there was 6ft. 3in. of him.
* * * *
Harold Marshall, who, after being inva-
lided from Egypt and England, died at Caulfield
(Vic.) the other day, was an athlete with a three-
star record. In ’lO he dead-heated with S. G. Hollow
for the Vic. mile championship (4min. 38sec.), and
a few months later he went to Tasmania and won the
Speck’s mile flat and 440yds. hurdles. Previous to
that he won the Malvern Harriers’ all-round cham-
pionship by lobbing' home first in the three-mile
walk, 440yds. and 120yds. hurdles, and 440yds flat;
second in the three-mile run and high jump; and
third in the 220yds., weight-put and half-mile. Mar-
shall was other things besides speedy. In ’OB-9 lie
was Vic. Y.M.C.A. champion gymnast. Also, when-
ever he picked up a cue he was a likely winner;
and motor-cycling and motor-boating claimed him
’tween-whiles. Tubercular trouble finished him at 27.
* * * *
Balmain, playing at Sydney on Saturday,
Australian rules, booted up 109 points while South
Sydney painfully amassed seven.
The destination of this year’s Vie. Foot-
bool League pennant was advanced a stage on Satur-
day, when ’Wood met ’Roy in the first semi-final on
the M.C.C. Ground, and passed out by 6 points. The
Maroons had been walloped so often that they wearied
of it and boggpd in so vigorously in the third
quarter that they got a 13-point lead. The Magpies
rose to the occasion and drew level. ’Roy retaliated ;
’Wood came again, and ’Roy did another dash, which
yielded two goals. The Magpies made a last tremen-
dous effort, snapped one goal and were on the road
for another when the bell rang. ’Roy, 63 points;
’Wood, 57. It was a great finish. “Gate” yielded
£lB3, most of which, including the M.C.C.’s Ground
rent, will go to Patriotic funds.
* * * *
Wally Messenger has the family kick. He
piled up 112 points with it in N. S. Wales League
competition for the City Cup.
Brisbane’s defeat of Maryborough by 29
to nine in the inter-city scramble was one of the
worst hidings handed out in the run of these matches.
Annandale Club won 12 out of 13 matches
in N.S.W. Soccer premiership and achieved 57 goals
against eight. Pyrmont, with 9 wins, panted in
second.
* * * *
Those hair-raising spins by Victorian
motor-cyclist Jack Booth didn’t get into the record-
book, after all. As Booth only applied to the
“authorities” to tackle the 10-mile journey, red-tape
ruled out all but the figures for that distance. Booth
was credited with 96 miles an hour in the mile and
88 miles an hour for five miles. These hustles may
take some working up again.
The greatest motor-drive yet accomplished
went on the slate at Noo Yark recently when R.
F. Mulford pulled up after pursuing S. F. Edge’s 24
hours’ track record of 1581 miles made at
Brooklands in 1907. Mulford in a 6-cylindered
Hudson whirled round the line two-mile wooden
track at Sheepshead Bay until at the end of 24
hours he had reeled off 1819 miles. The next thing
to wait for is the maniac who starts out to drive
2400 miles in a day, but swings into a two-miles-a-
minute gait and pulls up for breakfast 440 miles
past where he expected to smell the bacon. Then
he will sprint back for lunch and over-run it by
100 miles —and all the Yankee editors will be
rushing out special editions about the record-break-
ing Jonathan E. Roternote who is frantically strug-
gling to reach a free-lunch counter and can’t do it.
Jack Donaldson can still hurry over the
cinders. It is cabled that at Stamford Bridge (Eng.)
he last week defeated Applegarth over 100yds. and
220vds. in 9 4-ssec. and 21 4-ssec. All the same
The Bulletin wouldn’t mind swearing that John
didn’t let the watch beat him for anything.
A systematic attempt is being made to
stock South Australian waters with good ,fish.
Hitherto this has been left to private ventures by-
sportsmen, such as the Melroses and Stirlings, but
now the State Fisheries Department is taking a
hand. Ballarat is the headquarters of Australian
mainland, fish-hatching, and fry by the thousand
have been bought there and are being released in
the Murray, Hind-marsh, Onkaparinga and other S.A.
rivers, as " well as in some of the big reservoirs.
They are mostly trout, which can stand a taste of
salt water, and" are relied upon not to die when the
Murray gets low and the ocean leaks in through its
mouth.
* * * *
“D.F.” : I don’t know how fur a kangaroo
can jump, but James Kelly swears in the log of
his 1815 boat voyage around Tasmania that he saw
a black fellow cover 11 yards in one hop, which
must be easily the world’s record for a standing
jump. It was" on Hunter’s Island, and someone let
off a pistol and made the black brothers who had
gathered round the camp go for the tall tim-
ber. One big fellow was sitting near the fire and
in three springs he was well away in the scrub.
Kelly says they measured his first jump and found
it 11 yards. In the published version it was re-
duced to 11 feet but the MS. says “yards” plainly
enough.
“Eagle”: “Preparedness” is the newest
U.S. hobby, and the people who are going to get the
most fun out of it are the millionaire owners of
fast little pocket cruisers that do fever so many knots
an hour on petrol. Under modern war conditions
there is a use for nearly every sort of craft that
floats, so it isn’t necessary to own and run a cruiser
in order to be a person of some naval importance.
Provided there isn't a real war, Ulysses V. Shell-
maker and his gum-chewing girls will have a real
fine time this next summer or two cruising off their
summer resort in the family warship.
» * * *
“Daybreak”: Among the many old
pubs handed out by Bulletin contributors lately
are two that I have not seen mentioned, viz., The
Pig and Whistle and The Traveller’s Rest, which
had licenses in the far north-west of Tassy in the
bad old .days. Both were situated practically in
dense bush "and some hell-fire customers used to fore
gather thereat. Hughes, a one-time proprietor of
the P. and W., was a Welshman, a champion
wrestler and pretty swift with his “dukes.” To
him came news that a gentleman by the name of
Logan who was knocking about was alsp simi-
larly gifted. Quickly a match for £2O a-side was
arranged, the contest to be conducted with the raw
’uns ar.d to a finish. The clash took place in a
paddock adjoining the pub, and after an hour s
slogging Logan said he’d had enough. But Hughes
hadn’t. “No,” he said, “you have had a lot to
say, and I will not let you give me best. I have
not half hided you yet.” So Logan was forced to
face the music again, and after another hour s
pounding it was Hughes who began to look sick.
Logan, noticing this, managed to sum up sufficient
energy to send in a final fusillade, and the man
from ' Wales sagged at the knees. Which goes _to
prove that a good thing in the hand is a fleeting
luxury and not to be trifled with by a wise man.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the wel!=known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints _on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face.is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the fade. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
Photo: Wrather & Buys.
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any-
thing inferior to wash the hair with. Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
weir and it will look bright and
glossy.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little —what; do you call
it? —stimulant, and for this 1
would advise you to "get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick. Give your
hair a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.
Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and. enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result. ■
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to. get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.

this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable ha if

method of reducing obesity certainly is far

vious methods. It consists merely in eating

pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from

The Bulletin
If any illustration appearing in The
Bulletin takes your fancy, write to the
Manager, who will quote you price for it.
“ Jwosp”: The playmaking championship
oil the world is being written for by Vachell
(Britain) and Willard Mack (America).
Vachell opened in 1907 with “Her Son,” and
waited live years before he got into his
stride. Then followed “Jelf’s,” “Sunny
Bushes,’.’ “Searchlights,” “Quinneys,” and
“The Case of Lady Camber” all in three
years. This year he has already “Fen” and
“Fishpingle.” Of these five have pleased
the box oiiii es and the fifth lias to have its
trial. Mack in 1913 was stock-acting in
America. He kicked off in 1914 with “Kick
in,” at first a vaudeville sketch and then
padded out to a full-size melodrama to annoy
“Harrison Owen.” He landed over the bar
and went on within a few months with “So
Much for So Much.” This year the Mack
factor has turned out “Jane O’Day from
Broadway,” “King, Queen, Jack,” “Gentle-
nlen, Think It Over,” “Alias Santa Claus”
and “Common Kittle Things.” Somerset
Maugham put on seven plays between 1907
and 1909, and holds the blue ribbon witli
four plays running profitably in London
simultaneously.
“Melb” : The picture show given here of
Sarah Bernhardt in the drama of “Jeanne
Dore” is very surprising. I went falter-
ingly as one might to a hospital; but won-
derful old Sarah might well be without any
affliction at all. As lead in a long five-
reeler she is before the camera nearly all
the time, yet if you are not an old hand
with a recollection of her natural gait you
would have no suspicion of the artificial
leg. She is cleverly supported, in more
senses than one, by her indispensable com-
panion, little Miss Seylor, who performed
with her in Sydney and Melbourne. Divine
Sarah has put on a lot of weight, but her
face, I declare, is more pleasing than ever.
“Harrison O.”: “You get no Clara Butt
or Cisneros or any sort of true contralto in
Italy,” declares “0.K.” (B. 20/7/TO)_, and
then proceeds to blame the Italian climate.
But Italy can produce and has produced
true contraltos, some of the world’s best
artists, such as Marietta Brambilla, of
whom musical history records that she was
one of the rottenest actresses and finest
singers that grand opera has known; Gras-
sini, whose rich contralto De Quincey found
“delightful beyond all I have heard” ; Alvoni
and others. Also, if the spaghetti climate
is unfavorable to the production of con-
traltos, one would naturally suppose that it
would likewise affect the supply of bari-
tones and bassos, and of course it doesn’t.
May not the absence of decent contraltos
from visiting Italian cos. be accounted for
by the fact that, comparatively speaking,
the contralto cuts little ice in the florid
Italian operas? It was for the tenor and
soprano that Verdi and Donizetti mostly
wrote ; practically all their purple passages
for females are for the soprano. In any
case, it is not only from Italian opera com-
panies that contraltos of the calibre of Butt
and CrosSley have been missing, for we did
not get them in the English Quinlan co.
The. singers named are known to Australia
not as operatic, but as concert, performers.
“First Old Man” : One of the hard-worked
male choristers was petitioning the stage-
director to excuse him from the morrow’s
rehearsal. “Never heard of such a thing!”
said the stag-mag. “Why, in the name of
Heaven, do you want to be let off?” “Well,
to tell you the gospel truth,” returned the
up-stage singer, “I’ve got a particular ap-
pointment. It’s with a man who has owed
me 30s. for two years, and lie’s promised to
pay to-morrow.” “Go at your own risk,”
roared the director. “But I’ll tell you this
—if you absent yourself you’ll be fined.”
“llow much?” queried the chorus man.
“Ten shillings.” rapped out the S.M. “Um —
that settles it,” said the chorus man. “I’ll
be here.”
“Joybelles”: Manly, where the Sydney
summer girl wriggles pink toes in the bottle-
green breakers, is very fine as it is; but it
would be finer still if it had a troupe of the
open-air girl dancers who now wave pink
chiffon-wreathed limbs on the warmer Ameri-
can beaches. Artistic and artful photographs
show groups of these maidens prancing with
linked hands where sand and ocean meet
and the winds give the proper flicks to float-
ing draperies. A beauty sitting in a mer-
maidenly pose on a rock encircled ..by swirl-
ing water, with the ocean wind combing out
her floating hair, has more of the joy of life
in it than anything else I have seen on
calico or canvas.
Percy Grainger has just totted up 18
recalls at a concert in Toronto. The
Canadian papers describe golden-hair as “a
Siegfried of the piano,” though we’re not
playing in the same yard with many Sieg-
frieds just now.
THE COMEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The show is tedious, and dull
The actor people needs must he —-
The angel and the usual trull;
The hero, elegant to see;
That scion of iniquity,
The villain, with his raffish air ;
A father, and a comic pair.
I turn me from the stage direct
The circle and the stalls to see,
Where are assembled the elect;
And here the tragic comedy
Betrays a vital piquancy.
Not on the stage one sees, I wis,
Humanity laid bare like this.
Is lolling there a golden dame;
Her smiling husband sits below
With one who’s labelled “Lost to Shame.”
Her husband in the upper row
With youthful ardor seems to glow
For madam of the golden locks.
The world looks on, and grins and mocks.
Which sex will for divorce declare?
This problem plagues us where we sit.
But wife and husband of each pair,
With eoimnonsense and native wit,
At home have gravely tossed for it.
None heeds the actors. Plainly they
Should stop the show and watch the play !

BOOTS have come back again and no mistake.
But such (beams of hoots, up-to-date,
dainty, delightful, fit accompaniment to the
flounced taffeta frocks of the day, which
combine smart modernity with old-world charm.
Paris and London both are now tripping to work
and plav in short-fronted, high-heeled, suede-
topped boots like the Bostock style here illus-
trated. . . . ,
It is a smart little boot, there is no. doubt
about that, and every bit as comfortable as it is
smart. The beautifully fitting- last on which it is
made sees to that, and lots of women, who have
never imagined they could wear a short-fronted
style, will be amazed to find how comfortable it
is' how jollv it makes their feet look.
Then for those who want something different,
who prefer shoes perhaps, there is a fine range of
styles to select from, all as up-to-date and charm-
ing as this model,, and . all safe to wear well and
feel as peculiarly one’s own as if they had been
made to measure.

11. Campbell: Don’t care whether the
system is English or not, a co. which fixes its capi-
tal in plant, property and stock, and then under-
takes to repay the shareholder his capital almost
on demand, is built on a rotten foundation; and a
respectable law wouldn't allow it. The best English
co-operative cos. know the system is rotten. Hence
they have built large reserves, which are not with-
drawable. But in Australia and Maoriland the fact
that the capital is withdrawable has been almost in-
variably regarded as a reason for paying away all
the profits and building up no ' reserves. ... Bill
Adams: Keep going; the battle of Waterloo was not
so big as it’s been painted... .Überin: No.... The
Squib: Thanks, but we already had it.... W.5.N.:
Much obliged..... Cocky: lie was pulling your leg.
....Nemo: What gardens? Vuna: A curse upon
you for jambing your lines so tight together that
even the thinnest sub. can’t squeeze in... .Haka:
Well, Billjim isn’t slang, and it was an affectionate
moniker bestowed upon the typical Outback Aus-
tralian years before the war was dreamed 0f... .
A.8..J.: After the old land-grant system was abol-
ished in N.S.W., unprincipled persons who wanted
land went out and occupied it. A report dated 1836
says: “The Crown lands are occupied by persons
denominated squatters, mostly convicts holding
tickets of leave or becoming free try servitude. They
are cattle thieves of the worst order.” To suppress
them the Crown Lands Occupation Act was passed
in 1830 appointing commissioners to mark out suit-
able areas, and allowing occupation by proper persons
on payment of £lO license fee. These were called
licensed squatters. Heavy penalties were imposed
on the unlicensed squatters, who 'gradually dis-
appeared ; and the licensed person then became
known as the squatter. The original Act and
Regulations were continually amended as faults
were disclosed. Thus the squatter became entitled
to lease a portion of his holding on which he put
liis permanent improvements with a right of pur-
chase—“pre-emptive right”—at the upset price at
the end of the term. . . .8.M.: “Whilst War is
Waging” is not a poem. It is bad verse. .. .N. 8..
Nearly.. ..D.C.Q. :■ Feeble and sloppy... .Idaloo: No
g00d.... Manana: “For Judgment” does not strike
us... . Gunner Mac: Some quality, but hardly enough.

As wooden as if it had come from a timber yard.
This is something, like a record in mono-
tony. G. H. Taylor, of Dubbo (N.S.W.),
lias been for 43 years president of the local
hospital and for the same number of years
president of the Mechanics’ Institute. He
is a Sydney native ; his father was an early
teacher of St. Philip’s School.
Some people did not think much of the eclipse at
all,
Said it wasn’t dark enough, owing to the moon being
too small.
Others said they much preferred eclipses in the
summer,
That the moon, as a sunshade, was out of place in
winter.
Others, who took much greater interest in
astronomy,
Said it was a very great success scientifically,
That the weather, for taking observations, was with-
out defect,
When the moon became a bullseye on the sun, it
was perfect.
Wise men, years ago, timed this eclipse to the
minute,
And said, the following Sth Aus towns would be
right in it.
Tarcoola, Iron Knob, P6r£ Broughton, Snowtocvn,
Hoyleton, Riverton s
Kapunda, Angaston, Mannum, Peake, Wolseley, and
Serviceton.

T H , E : ‘Beale” Player-Piano is an instrument of
infinite capacity for intelligent interpretation. It
is in no sense mechanical. At all times the operator
is able to infuse his own personality into the rendition.
As for the tone, we can say no more than that the Piano itself
is just the usual good “Beale” quality—an Australian production,
which, in competition with the world's best makes, gained the
Grand Prize and three gold medals at the Franco-British
Exhibition in London.

' 1
IN
BANANA LAND
When Townsville parted with P. F.
Hainan a few days ago the sultry town lost
a bit of as genuine good stuff as ever left
the Ould Sod. “P.F.,” as he was commonly
called in the North, came to Victoria in
the early mining days and in ’66 migrated
to Townsville, where he became known as
a good citizen, a solid Conservative and the
kindliest soul north of Capricorn. Towns-
ville made Hainan Mayor some seven times,
elected him alderman almost unto _7O
times seven, and sent him. moreover, into
Parliament, where the Labor party fought
him and loved him at the same time. Eighty-
five years —and then the cursed climate!
Gone into retirement, J. G. Drake, a
capable man who was for a time a leader
in Queensland politics. Drake budded as a
journalist and afterwards served a term on
Hansard, where he found time to qualify
for the Bar. Originally a Radical, he
entered Parliamentary life and became Post-
master-General and Minister for Public
Instruction in the first Pliilp administration.
Then he went into the first Senate, and was
8.M.G.. Defence Minister and Attorney-
General. Later on Premier Ividston
appointed him Crown Prosecutor, a job for
which, despite his 66 years, he was still well
fitted.
R. de Moleyns, who passed out lately in
the North, came to Australia 20-<;dd years
ago for “colonial experience” at Hambledon,
near Cairns. In partnership with his
brother and a Chinaman named George Ah
Ilee, de Moleyns cleared the Sawmill Pocket
scrub country, in the same district, for sugar
growing; and the brothers also started the
fiist coffee plantation at Babinda. Their
dad (Yentry) was a member of the British
House of Lords; and the wife vf Hopetoun,
first Gov.-General of the Commonwealth,
was their sister.
Passed out lately, .T. Y. Walker, one of
the patriarchs of Bundaberg. For over 50
years he pioneered on the Burnett River,
and in his overtime worked a more or less
facile pen to record the history and possi-
bilities of;the district. •
“E.G.H.” explains the practical way
Bananalaud railwaymen are preparing for
the homeward retreat of damaged Billjim: •
Four months ago the railwaymen started to prepare
farms at Birkdale for their returned wounded com-
rades; 10 acres are now under cultivation and
another 10 acres are being cleared, Birkdale is on
Pie Cleveland line, between Manly, and Wellington
Point, and is ideal for fruit and vegetable growing.
Five acres were a. gift from Railway Commissioner
Evans, who had the area cleared, fenced and
ploughed. Five acres were also purchased from
him by the fund; and another 10 acres are being
cleared by voluntary workers at week-ends. Two
returned men liave been Installed, and they are
working under the direction of a professional man
who looks in twice a week. There are 30,000 pine-
apples already growing, and also beet, beans, peas,
cabbages, tomatoes and other vegetables. In another
part of the farm are 50 citrus fruit trees, including
oranges, lemons and limes. Oats are also growing,
and round the residence are roses and nowering
shrubs. Poultry houses of malthoid have been
erected on the most up-to-date lines, and the farm
is well equipped with everything necessary to its
successful working.
“F.J.McM.” : The people who are plucky
enough to live in the out-of-the-way places
of the North are surely worth some con-
sideration for doing what most of us want
them to do and won’t do ourselves. Yet
one can travel up the Queensland coast and
along the Gulf and see the dry-rot in towns
that were once flourishing. People hang on
and desperately hope for a new prosperity,
knowing the richness and resourcefulness of
the great hinterlands at the back of their
dead towns. Now and then a vote-seeking
politician drops in to remind them of their
vast natural resources and what he intends
to do to develop them, and as suddenly
returns to his old Parliamentary job of
subsidising centralisation. The s.s. Mus-
grave, the ancient tub that makes a tri-
weekly trip to Cooktown, Normanton and
Burketown, is typical of the conditions in
the North. This old punt- carries 36 pas-
sengers when there’s not decent accommoda-
tion for half that number, the steerage pas-
sengers having to find an unpleasant corner
among the cattle, fodder and other inci-
dentals of the trip. There would be a riot
if a city suburb was asked to put up with
such things.
“Curtins,” from Cloricurry :
The letter from Gerard Williams (B. 27/7/'l6)
is a reminder of what the staff of Mt. Oxide has
done to redeem the unsavory name of that mine.
The first manager, Freeman, fell in the opening
stages of the fighting in Gallipoli ; M illiams, the
next, is in khaki ; Jim Henry, who was mine-manager
with William?, is a major in the Miners’ Corps;
Tandy, surveyor and manager after Williams, a
lieutenant; Leech, the accountant during those three
regimes, a quartermaster in the same corps. Since
Tandy went the mine has had no independent man-
agement, being now a short-handled dependency of
the Mt. Elliott Co., which is just as well; for the
atmosphere of the place is such that no manager of
fighting age could breathe it comfortably.
“Dyer”; Black brother knew our natural
dye stuffs long before the first importer
come. When I was a youngster the niggers
around Avliere I lived could paint themselves
blue and red from the juice of the fruit of
tiie prickly pear, and the color didn’t fade
either. They were “done” for white dye,
but they used a kaolin clay with good effect.
I came across an abo. on the Johnstone
River a few years back who had happened
on the vividest scarlet I ever saw. He told
me it came from a tree and he used it for
marking banana cases. The prettiest blue
I ha\ 7 e glimpsed, from pale sky to royal, is
got by the N.Q. abos. from some plant
found "all over the place pp there. It goes
on to wood, nigger skin and cotton rags,
and sticks to all three like scandal to a
politician. Now will someone investigate or
must we confess ourselves less progressive
than the blacks?
COMPLIMENTS.
PESSIMISTIC TRADESMAN: “So you're taken this shop, 'ave yer? Well, it’s an unlucky
shop, an’ I guarantee ye’ll fall inside six months.”
COHEN: “My dear friend —you flatter me.”
Senior-Sergeant Kenny, of Cairns, made
out an admirable case for compulsory ser-
vfice the other day : —•
I counted upwards of 20 lazy louts of the class to
which defendant belongs lounging under hotel veran-
dahs. To see these robust, healthy young- men, all
known to me as spielers, coming- to the races,to seek
what prey they may devour, enjoying freedom while
the pride of our manhood are shedding their blood
for us in France, is one of the most practical demon-
strations we can have of the necessity for conscrip-
tion, which would make these loafers of some use to
the country.
AVe are told, of course, that these fellows
Avould be no good in the trenches. Well,
they could attend to the latrines.
“Bumpkin”: The ratepayers of a dairy-
ing district near Brisbane finally moved
their shire councillors to unanimously
decide on impounding all straying horses
and cows. The pound-keeper was accord-
ing! v instructed, and went straight to tvork.
First day’s bag: Two animals—the shire
clerk’s horse and the chairman’s coav.
N.B. —There Avas no second day.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 12, 1916.
My Dear Thakgomindah, —
The G.-G. and his Avit'e are here, and we
are showing them our pet charities anc
patriotic turnouts. On Friday Lady Helen
went through the Red Cross rooms m
Adelaide-street. She Avas particularly inter-
ested in some bandages of which Aye Banana-
landers seem to have the monopoly. A look
in at the Red Cross Kitchen and the
Y.W.G.A. rooms and afternoon tea at
Bishopsbourne filled in the corners of the
day.
Our own Ex. found your climate too
severe and has been so busy sneezing that
he has not found time to come home; but
he mav be back on Monday. He is due to
unveil the Roll of Honor at the Grammar
School in the evening. There are already
591 names on it.
Home Sec. Huxliam, a keen barracker
for Young Australia, opened a fete in aid
of the AA r est End Creche and; Kindergarten
to-day, and to-night is presiding at a con-
cert in aid of the Queen Alexandra Home
for children.
The first day of the August meeting at
Eagle Farm brought an invasion of squatters
with their hands full of money, and squat-
teresses in the newest thing in gowns.
Local aristocracy is too worn out with good
works to do much frivolling these days. Or,
perhaps, it was the influence of the presence
of the G.-G., who has sworn off the gee-gees
until after the war.
Henry Hart, hon. organiser of the enter-
tainment committee of the Red Cross, goes
into camp on Monday. The soldiers in
hospital will miss him badly; so will the
society.
This evening the Commercial Travellers’
Association celebrated Association Day by
a concert in the club rooms. Lena
Hammond, Maggie Lloyd, George Kitson
and Billy Crisp were the singers, and W.
It. Wishart, Gladys Frost and Marjorie
Steward helped piano and ’cello to make
glad noises.
A new singer has arrived, and quite a
local product. She’s Ivy Norman, a con-
tralto with lower notes like the tones of
an organ. She made her appearance at a
recital in Beale’s salon last Wednesday.
Lina Hammond, who has been coaxing tlu
voice into blossom, attended to accompani-
ments.
Alleyne Conran, second daughter ot the
Harry Conrans, of Westgate-on-Sea, and a
granddaughter of Mrs. George Rail, ot this
citv. is engaged to Mountjoy Upton, son ot
tin* Viscount Templetown who wants Billy
Hughes to save England. In pre-war days
the two Conran girls were oil visiting terms
with Willie the Kaiser’s son and lus missus,
and Nancy, the elder, taught b illie. ski-ing.
Pa made money out of Australian mines and
share-dealing.
Mrs. John McCallum spread the alluring
cream cake at New Earni last \A ednesday
in honor of the Orange Dandies, who
opened their season here to-night.
The Exhibition Hall was crowded on
Tuesday for the Musical Union concert.
Andrew Black, Mrs. S. B. Harris, AY. AY.
Crisp and W. Templeton were the soloists,
and “Faust” the music.
Market Square looks like a village of
fantods. Various shapes, some like animals,
others like clumps of bush, have been
hurriedly erected. They are the stalls for
the sale of goods in Do Without Week. You
do without something you want in order to
save money to buy something you don’t
want.
On Friday there was another street raid,
this time in aid of the hospitals. Over
£lOOO was raised. Meantime Brisbane Hos-
pital is so desperately hard up that tin 1
Government is seriously considering the
question of taking over the complete main-
tenance of all the infirmaries.
Brisbane is the only capital city in the
Commonwealth where no effort is made to
regulate pedestrian traffic. At night crowds
wander aimlessly up and down, whichever
side of the footpath they please, mobs loiter
round picture shows and street corners, and
it requires infinite time and patience to
arrive at a given spot anywhere within the
city boundaries. You don’t know how often
we miss our engagements just through this.
And, dear, we can’t afford to take any
chances now.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Says Reynolds’S Newspaper (London):
It is the calm and firm resolve of the -Australian
people to push the war to a successful completion.
The successful prosecution of the war is a subject
of paramount importance to everybody there. To
this everything else is subordinated. There has been
a marked absence of Labor disputes !
This splendid joke was signed by T. J. Ryan.
Premier of Queensland.
“Orpheus”: I had occasion to hire an
'orchestra lately for a public meeting, and the
cost of the thing was £4 10s. I didn’t mind
that —it was the approved union rate —but
along came the State Insurance 'Commis-
sioner' and made me insure the lot for that
one evening at a cost of 10s, The infuriat-
ing effect of Brisbane instrumental music on
the average audience has at last obtained
State recognition.

From the letter of Billy Khaki at the
Front: —-
We have been giving the Bodies a little of their
own back. They tried to gas us yesterday morning.
This morning we replied with a cloud that laid out
over .50.
This no doubt is what is called a gas retort.
“Bad Australian” : Who’s making all the
noise about cigarette-smoking and its per-
nicious effects? Almost every human being
in South America consumes the comforting
“fag” by the score, and in many years’
wandering over this country I have never
struck anything among white, Indian, Mes-
tizo or Zambo to confirm my juvenile super-
stition that cigarette indulgence was the
surest and quickest way to qualify for the
looney-house. Although almost everybody in
tliis country uses tobacco, yet. in most places,
a pipe is looked on as a curiosity, and pro-
bably S 5 per cent, or DO per cent, of the
leaf is consumed in the form of “cigarillos.”
In no country is the average consumption of
tobacco greater than in S. America, yet the
cigarette “rat” is no more in evidence than
public drunkenness-—a rare thing, though
practically every member of the community
gets away with at least a pint of wine per
day.
“Trooper Blnegum” : Don't believe all the
tales you hear about the heat of the
Egyptian desert. Not once lately has it
been up to 200° in the shade. It is only in
the sun that the thermometers burst. It is
not true either that the Gyppies feed their
fowls on crushed ice to prevent them laying
hard-boiled eggs. But they cook the eggs
by covering them with scorching sand. An
Anzac claimed to be able to light his
cigarette by getting outside his tent at
midday and sucking hard at the fag. That
lad is going for a war-correspondent's job
as soon as the Censor will let the truth have
a chance. And the scorching sand does not
set fire to your socks through the heavy
military boots if you keep moving. All the
same, the Immigration Restriction Act will
have to be amended to meet the case of the
Light Horse when they return.
“Archdeacon Gush” : The recently-pub-
lished biography of the late Robert Benson
gives a clue to the motives of those of my
brethren who believe with me that spiritual
boundary-riding in Australia can best be
done by Englishmen. Benson was the son
of an Archbishop of Canterbury, and his
sympathetic biographer records his “tem-
peramental detachment from the impressive,
courtlike existence of Lambeth, and even
of Addington, where the atmosphere was
heavy with ecclesiastical politics, and indeed
with a certain sense of sovereignty.” Where
is your impressive, courtlike existence in
Australia? Where is your “certain sense
of sovereignty”—except at the Trades Hall?
You’ll never be able, I tell you, to brew this
fruity old atmosphere. [What is the good
of it to the cure of souls at Boggabri?]
Pardon me, I am not here to argue the
matter.
When the Stawell train charged into a
goods train at Trawalla (Vie.) the other
day, the driver of the goods, Charles Davis,
escaped injury, although it was a head-on
collision, and he was in the cab. But when,
an hour later, he was helping to shunt the
disarranged rolling stock, his head was
caught between two buffers and he was
killed. Fate had smiled and fired his second
barrel.
“Nine Feet” : The world’s savage reverence
for tall fighting men will never die out. The
Sydney newspapers have been printing the
photograph of the “Tipperary Giant,” who
has enlisted and will shortly be sent to the
creeping, crawling war to run the risks of
two ordinary men, and the public has been
invited to stand off and admire. Then
Cullen, Lieut.-Gov.. of N. S. Wales, pinned'
the D.C.M. on the chest of a nuggetty little
hero of whom a morning paper remarked,
“he is so small it is a wonder how he ever
came to pass.” Anyone would think the
modern warrior had to leap into the air to
reach the enemy instead of going at him
like a. snake.
Recently the National Council of Women
in Melbourne protested against the State
training of schoolgirls in the domestic arts.
But that the attitude of the Victorian
parent isn’t hostile was plainly indicated
the other day, when the third school of the
kind was opened by the Education Depart-
ment. It is at Bendigo and provides facili-
ties for teaching 108 children how to peel a
rabbit and hash a potato. By 0 a.m. on
the opening day tin; full number of pupils
was enrolled, and throughout the rest of
the morning a horde of disappointed
mammas struggled vainly to get Jane and
Emma on the register. As a reflector of
national feeling the National Council of
Women is no better than 21b. of dough.
“8.D.”: It’s all rot to talk about the
possibility of doing without German goods,
after all'. This week I went into a big
Flinders-street (Melbourne) shop to get a
sixpenny tape-measure. They _ took several
minutes to find one, and then it had “Made
in Germany” stamped on it in large letters.
Of course,' we can’t make tape-measures.
But when our boys come back from the war
we are going to spend millions in “repatri-
ating” them. “Repatriating” is a large word
which means finding new jobs for our own
boys while we keep the old jobs for the
foreigner—the old jobs of making tape-
measures and the like.
So far, technical education in the Speck
has been mostly a rather poor joke. Half a
dozen local committees in different towns have
run a school apiece, but except at Zeehan
and Queenstown they haven’t done much
more than give people a chance to follow
harmless hobbies. But the new Minister for
Education, Lee, who as a waggon-builder is
a bit of a technical expert himself, prepares
to shake things up. The State is going, ll it
can find the cash, to set up a complete sys-
tern of technical and trade schools, catching
the young when they leave the piimaii
schools and teaching the boys to plumb and
carpenter and the girls to cook. As a start.
Lee is trying to get N.S.W. technologist.
Nangle to tell him how to go about all these
things.

The Most Modern and
Palatial Hotel in Australia!
THE Hotel Carlton, completed at a cost exceeding £lOO,OOO,
and furnished sumptuously throughout by David -Tones, Ltd.,
has, since its opening on March 30, 1916, been thronged
with crowds of delighted visitors • ~ •
While its exquisite appointments—its perfect cuisine and courteous
service more than commend themselves to travellers used *to every
comfort —the Hotel Carlton can hardly be described as being
merely the leading hotel.
It is more than that, •
It is from every point of view the most desirable place in Sydney
to stay.
Every detail in the perfect attention to visitors’ wishes has been
thoroughly studied, and reflects the careful thoughtfulness of the
management, who have had a wide experience in catering for the
best class of patronage
One of the attractive features of the Hotel Carlton is that every
bedroom is naturally ventilated, unusually spacious, has hot and
cold water installed, and is artistically furnished.
It is absolutely fireproof, having marble staircases and concrete
floors throughout.
The general appointments include amongst others: Magnificent
Dining Hall (with separate accommodation for children), elegant
and comfortable lounges, reception, smoking, writing and reading
rooms, also private rooms for weddings, dinner-parties, afternoon-
teas, etc.

The Story of the Lamp
62
-mnh
With Rome in its Glory, 64 A.D.
The luxury which Rome lavished on itself
at the time of Nero was reflected in the
lamps of that period. Magnificent stands
holding six or eight lamps adorned the
homes of the wealthy. Olive Oil was com-
monly used in these lamps. The better
classes, however, generally mixed perfume
with their oil to counteract the strong smell.

7he soft white light

Laurel gives a brilliant, clear, white light,
without flickering. The last thimbleful
burns as well as the first. For lighting,
heating and cooking, Laurel cannot be
excelled. Laurel is our new trade mark
name for the highest quality American
Kerosene ever sold in Australasia and re-
places our old brand, White Rose.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The Emden made the Launceston (Tas.)
Marine Board a present of over £30,000
when it sank the dredge Poonrabbel some-
where in the Indian Ocean. The Board put
the insurance money into a new dredge for
which the contract price on the Clyde was
£32,717, equal to £40,907 delivered at
Launceston. Now the Russian Government
has offered £70,000 for the turnout. The
Board has decided to help a gallant Ally by
taking £70,000 net with delivery on the
Clyde. As for itself, it thinks now it can
worry along without a new muck-shifter for
a while.
“R.S.A.”: For those who happen to
possess a horse, a dray, a kelly, a box of
matches and a timber-getter’s license, a
whisper. The job is known in the profes-
sion as “coal-burning,” and those who follow
it with any degree of energy can easily keep
the wolf out of range. A rough knowledge
of different timbers is required. Get into
your nut that the best are bloodwood, white
gum, stringybark, box and turpentine (with
apologies to Mr. Maiden), their burning
propensities being in the order named ; but
avoid, shun and disregard apple and »blue-
gum. A pit is dug in proximity to your
timber, and is generally about 9ft. x 3ft x
The heaviest and greenest logs go
down' first and should project to allow of
being poked in as they burn away. Finally,
the lightest and driest are dumped on, and a
thin layer of earth is heaped over the top.
With occasional rakings the arrangement is
left till next day, when the charcoal will be
found ready for bagging. Ordinarily, one
man can get through 40 to 50 bags a day
and each bag is worth, on an average, Is.
3d.; but of course the cost of transportation
and jute reduces the profit a bit. The
demand is created by the use of charcoal in
cold storage.
* * * *
“Iris”: Now that German coal-tar by-
products have become scarce, Australia is
doing something to make good the deficiency.
In one direction at least German ingenuity
has been bettered. An Australian, having
invented a paint with a coal-tar base, has
put it on the market and persuaded a num-
ber of public bodies to use it. It comes in
all colors, though not in white (cream is
the nearest), and is allegedly the only thing
suitable for painting ships below the water-
line —it is already used for that purpose by
several firms. Also it does away with the
need for white lead, which is a consideration
now that the price of that item has gone up
nearly 100%. The only thing needed to
secure this apparently excellent article for
good is an absolute prohibition against the
importation of paints of a similar nature.
Then, if Germany or the U.S.A. should steal
the secret after the war, there will be no
interference with the sale of the article, in
Australia at least.
Victoria is pursuing with great energy
the conservation and development of the
great natural resources. Down in the
Beech Forest, not 100 miles from Melbourne,
magnificent cabinet-making timber is being
split into fencing-posts, or, in many cases,
burnt. Settlers are breaking their hearts
and their backs trying to get rid of timber
which would, if it could be got to market,
be worth far more than the land will be
when they get it cleared. Meanwhile the
State Ministry is spending money in plant-
ing trees which may in half a century or
so be nearly a quarter as valuable as the
trees now going up in smoke.
Ginseng-growing appears to be a get-rich-
quick industry that Australians might try.
According to a booklet written by H. V.
Edwards (Gordon and Gotch, publishers)
one acre of five-year-old roots of the vege-
table when' dried is worth £5OOO, and he
makes the statement after experience at
Bega (N.S.W.) to support the assurance
of an American grower of 15 years’ stand-
ing. The Australian declares that a gin-
seng garden of one-eighth of an acre may
be started for £25, and should return £BOO
net profit in five years. Little hired labor
is necessary, and two girls in Ohio
(U.S.A.), cultivating less than an acre, are
reported to be making £lOOO to £2OOO a
year. That is the life ! Ginseng, as most
people do not know, is John Chinaman’s
medicinal plant No. 1. He uses it in nearly
all his prescriptions, and as there are
400,000.000 of him ready to pay up to £3
per pound retail, the time seems ripe, or
words to that effect —particularly for sol-
diers whose health has been impaired.
“Scales”: It has been quite interesting to
listen to the jam-trade drummers of Vic-
toria. The Pure Foods regulations have
been roused to say that a lib. tin of
jam shall contain IGoz. of boiled vegetable;
and one after the other the manufacturers
have been raising their voice in a song of
thanksgiving that at last the unfair compe-
tition from which they have so long suffered
has been stopped.
THE TOWN IS FULL OF FOOLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The town is full of solemn fools,
Of sneaking fools and sly,
Who plan and work with various tools
“To put a little by” ;
The dullest fool will sometimes win
When wiser fools fall down,
So, full of hope, each fool goes.in
To fool about in town.
The town is full of various fools
Disguised as honest men;
They mess about with “rings” and “pools”
And toil with wits and pen;
They spread no sail, they guide no plough,
But plot, and dodge and shirk,
And yet the world to these must bow
Who neither fight nor work!
The poorest sailor on the seas,
The meanest harvest-hand,
Is worth a creeping street of these.
And you must understand
That I’m no farmer, brave and stout,
Or sailor ’neath Gocl’s sky;
The sort of fools 1 see about,
That sort of fool am I.
N.S.W.
