The Sea Witch
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHE dwells forever in her dim sea-caves,
Her gleaming self half-shadowed in
’ their gloom ;
And, round her strewn, a kingdom’s treasures
glow
Through the green, baleful twilight, and the
low
Croon of the waves comes to her—the long
waves
That hear the gallant ships to her and doom.
Heavy about her falls her loosened hair,
Yellow as is the blood-bought Spanish gold
Cast at her feet. The jewels of a queen
Burn in a-myriad colors through the green -
The wavering, gleaming green that shines
her fair,
Rich beauty; yet her eyes are clear and cold.
Remote she broods above the many dead-
Grotesque lie they, each with 'ins quest
unsought—
Unheeding them; yet if, a moment’s space,
Her glance dwells on them over her calm
face.
There flies a quick, still smile that leaves
her red
Soft lips more cruel for the passing thought.
The first Sea Goers heard her siren call,
And, hearing only, longed to kiss her lips;
Onward and onward still her blue eyes drew
Them till oblivion in her kiss they knew.
Eternally she waits her lovers all
Till the last day of men and seas and ships.
N.s.w.
A. SMITH.
The War That Went On.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fl HE war went on, and went on going
on. In fact, its confirmed habit of
going on going on was the only sta-
tionary thing about the war. Wars are
easily enough started: the slightest push
will do it. But as the thing gathers speed
its inertia becomes so enormous that a seis-
mic disturbance or three revolutions are
about the only things that will stop it.
But the war didn’t remain the same war.
Comparative strangers and even relations-
in-law that met it in its later stages hardly
recognised it for the same war that they had
known in their young days. It was a nice,
clean little baby of a war at first; but as it
grew up and came into contact with life it
went on becoming more dirty and disrepu-
table and violent and uncontrollable with
each succeeding year. Even its German
father grew rather ashamed of his child,
and went about loudly disowning his off-
spring to anybody who would listen to him.
But everybody was too busy making explo-
sives and explosive noises to hear him.
In the tiny islet of Atlantis, somewhere
in the Pacific, the little community of white
settlers became gradually accustomed to the
permanency of the war. In course of time
they recognised that war was the usual
order of things, and peace a mere discredited
legend. The children that had been born
after the war started grew into manhood
with a contemptuous disdain of the curious
tradition of their elders that there had once
been a thing called peace. But this changed
world hardly affected that lonely islet of
Atlantis, somewhere in the Pacific. At first,
when the war was exciting, ships used to
come and tell the interested inhabitants all
the war-news; then the visits of the ships
grew infrequent, and at last no more ships
came. The reason, as the inhabitants of
Atlantis rightly guessed, was that it was no
longer safe for ships to use the surface of
the ocean. There were too many super-
submarines about. However, being self-
supporting upon their semi-tropical islet, the
Atlantans minded their own business;
though John Psmith (the first),*.one of the
brightest young fellows in those days, often
wondered what exactly was happening
beyond those 1000 leagues of ocean that
separated Atlantis from the world —the
world of war.
John Psmith would have been surprised if
he had known; though if he had merely
argued it out he would have succeeded in
hazarding _ a few logical conclusions. But
John Psmith was not that sort of bloke.
As the war went on, the submarine and
the Zeppelin went on. The submarine’ grew
larger and larger, and the Zeppelin put on
flesh rapidly, and the aeroplane suffered
more and more from embonpoint. And
naturally the submarine went deeper and
deeper, since there were soon so many sub-
marines about that the traffic within 50
fathoms of the surface became too congested
for safety, and joy-riders in that vicinity
were criminally careless, and the submarine
police never could see their numbers. And
the they submerged the better they
liked it. So the Zeppelin and the aeroplane
went higher and higher; the clouds made
them skid.
But it was not their concern for their
own comfort that ultimately drove the sub-
marine into the ocean depths and the air-
machines into strangely rarefied atmospheres,
the compelling reason was that the surface
°L the globe, land or water, wasn’t safe for
either. The submarine became so deadly
.hat it could sink a super-dreadnought with
celerity and ease. And it did —all the super-
ui cad noughts that it could find. Long before
this the hostile submarines had sunk each
other s and all neutrals’ mercantile marine,
borne of the surviving merchant-lines shel-
tered in harbors for a few years; but the
super-sausages found them out and plunked
I??®. I ', ° m bs on to them. Whenever the
nntisli or German battlefieets ventured out
the super-suomarines sank them all. The
submarines didn’t need to come to the sur-
face to settle the battleships; they had no
!^r'Au betra ™ P ei ’ isc °Pe- They speeded
aon fathoms deep, and fired their gigantic
! rom ~t heir gigantic howitzer
rmm t i audltf ! r y range-finders enabled
them t<> locate the battleships by the
sound of their screws. The last Ger-
man super-dreadnought for a long time
cunningly puzzled the lurking British
submarines by stopping her tell-tale screws
and drifting: but incautiously one day the
German mess-room toasted the centenary of
“The Day” with ringing “Hochs” ; and the
British officer in charge of the range-finder
promptly finished that day for them.
Before this the navies of the world had
begun to take to the depths. It was a bril-
liant effort on the part of the British naval
experts when they sank their newest super-
dreadnought under water. The transforma-
tion of the latest naval monster into a sub-
marine was so successful that she
never came up. After that no more surface
battleships were built; and the gigantic sub-
marines that swarmed the under-seas had
nothing to do but to fight each other. They
had effectually swept the waves of every-
thing that tried to ride them. So the oceans
of the globe returned to their prehistoric
state, except that they were undisturbed by
storms. The amount of oil that floated upon
their surface kept them in a permanent
calm. Peace had come to the waters, though
beneath them pulsed the busy traffic of the
submarines.
But the inhabitants of Atlantis could not
see beneath the gentle, oily swell. And
though John Psmith (the second) fitted out
a schooner to explore the outside world he
never came back. Some lurking submarine
got him, from force of habit.
As it wasn’t safe , on the surface of the
sea, so it wasn’t safe on the surface of the
land. The super-Zepps and the super-
planes, looking in the air like cathedrals and
the prize-winning design for the Federal
capital, soon settled that. At the Front the
habit of trench-life proceeded to its logical
conclusion. The further down you dug your
trenches the safer you were. The war had
settled down. For all wars are restless in
their youth, and begin with vague, splendid
ideas of glory, and waste a lot of their
strength charging the foe and making
strategic advances and strategic retreats;
but this irresponsibility of youth changes to
the placidity of okl age. And so the com-
batants found it safer and easier to engage
each other through tunnels, and made their
brilliant charges with navvies’ picks and
mine-boring drills. On top there was nothing
but annihilation, since the development of
guns and shells and aerial torpedoes and
bombs made it unhealthy for anybody who
hadn’t 50 feet of solid earth as an umbrella.
These soldiers excavated enormous cities in
the innards of the earth ; again in the his-
tory of mankind they became troglodytes.
And as generation after generation of
soldiers came to inhabit these underground
metropoles, they took to the underground life
as the natural order of things. Their enor-
mous eyes, so useful in their semi-darkness,
could not contract sufficiently to shut out
the cruel brilliance of the sun. Only on
dark, moonless nights did a few of the more
adventurous spirits creep up to gaze in
vacant wonder at the incomprehensible stars.
But it was cold up on the bare, swept sur-
face of the globe, cold and dreadful and
awe-inspiring; and at the threatening of
moonrise or dawn they gladly scurried back
to the damp warmth and comforting dark-
ness of their native cities in the basement of
the earth.
What went on at the Front went on all
over the earth. The appallingly destructive
power of the super-Zepps and the super-
planes threatened every city. When British
planes obliterated Berlin and German Zepps
utterly destroyed the Albert memorial in
London it was recognised that something
must be done. At first the engineers set to
work to roof the cities over with an in-
destructible dome of ferro-concrete; but this
was soon recognised as merely a makeshift.
The dome was too conspicuous a mark for
the enemy air-machines, and the hollow,
reverberating sound of gigantic bombs thud-
ding on the dome seriously interfered with
the debates in the British House of Com-
mons on the Home Rule Bill. The members
could not hear themselves speak.
The only thing to be done was to put the
whole city underground. So St. Paul’s
Cathedral was carefully lowered into an
immense excavation below the surface, and
roofed over with the excavated spoil, both
bomb- and sound-proof. There were, of
course, some unfortunate accidents. In Syd-
ney the whole of George-street was safely
lowered, but remained at an angle of 49
degrees to the perpendicular, which made
business in the buildings on each side of that
street rather perilous. The poets bringing
their contributions to The Bulletin office
had to haul themselves up from the lower
side of the street by ropes; but when
ejected they slid gracefully into the lopsided
bar of the hotel almost opposite. The staff
had to be roped to their desks, and the edi-
torials became skew-wiff. But, on the whole,
the work of lowering all the cities, towns and
villages a hundred feet into the earth was
safely and creditably performed. These
underground cities were, of course, rather
dark; but 'Pie Londoners felt the depriva-
tion. of light very little. The lowering of
the Thames proved impossible; the only pre-
caution taken was to roof it over and plant
innocuous cabbages on the roof. There was
a tremendous development of tube-railways
all over the earth.
The subsided cities required an under-
ground agriculture. The air-machines, find-
ing no cities to bomb, began, rather meanly,
to destroy the agricultural crops methodi-
cally. Whole asparagus beds disappeared in-
stantly ; and for some time the inhabitants
of the capitals of the world had to subsist
on mushrooms. But the spirit of the race
tackled the new problem with courage. Pad-
dock after paddock was excavated, lowered
underground and roofed; and within a few
years all crops were grown 50 fathoms
deep.
As for the super-Zepps and the super-
planes. they became so efficient that they
could stay in the.air apparently for ever.
And their crews got so accustomed to living
at tremendous heights that they felt no in-
clination to come down. It wasn’t safe to
hover near the earth, for the cities of the
trenches and the seats of government had
anti-aircraft guns of an incredible accuracy
and range.
The cave-dwellers had developed an un-
charitable, almost suburban, attitude toward
the air-dwellers. They thought that the air-

brother was one of life’s obvious failures, and
didn’t care avlio knew it; but Gertrude had
the sacrificial habit so badly that, despite the
protests of all who knew the circumstances,
she continued to send half her small income
to the man who had started to drift lazily
downstream ever so many years before. Per-
haps the sentimental Aveakiiess of a younger
Gertrude had originally encouraged a lazy
man to give up trying. Now in advanced

her Avomen friends and relatives. There is a

carried to excess. ' And doubtless the thing
Avould go on until loyal old Gertrude, Avorn
out by her sacrifices and endless duty visits,

primitive savage half of me Avas left, and my
mental review of her past record and future
sacrifices made me more determined to finish

That night as the Avife and I sat on the

To my secret delight she said they left her

engaged to him years ago and we were all
young and foolish. It’s lucky she didn’t

Understand why he didn’t drop us twenty
years ago—as any other man of decent
instincts would have done. But of course
lie is too dense to realise that he doesn’t
belong to this period at all and far too dull
to make 2'ny new friends. His type always
hang on r*
“They all hang on !” I corrected sadly.
“Yes, and I wish to goodness we could
get rid of them and only have the people
who entertain us! I don’t want to spend
my life making tea for bores and listening
to them till I am tired and irritable. It
isn’t a fair thing!”
“Of course it isn’t! Now, while'l think
of it, when are. you going to take that
holiday? How about starting away on
Saturday afternoon?”
As usual she demurred for a little while,
but finally gave in and agreed to go away
to a farm in the hills for a month. On the
day appointed I saw her off and then went
straight home, where an assortment of bright
new gardening implements, fresh from the
ironmonger’s, awaited me.
Curiously enough the garden showed no
sign of the loving toil I performed during
the subsequent fortnight; but the picks and
shovels always had fresh earth on them and
the large basement somehow became full of
fresh-broken clay. At the end of the first
fortnight I was feeling so toned up by my
steady exercise that I decided to hold an
invitation garden f§te in aid of the church
on the front lawn, and sent out 43 gilt-edged
invitations to the 43 bores. They came,
as I knew they would, and when I saw them
all together and safely involved with the
good things provided, I stole out into the
the back garden and pressed with one
trembling finger the button of an electric
battery. Instantly there was a blinding
flash and a shattering roar, and a shower of
dark objects flew skywards, followed by a
great and expanding mushroom of thick
yellow smoke. The fete was over!
The explosives e: rts and the police sub-
sequently agreed that it was caused by an
accumulation of gas from a leak in the main
' —a tribute, I think, to my policy of steal-
ing sparingly from many sewage contrac-
tors’ magazines instead of crudely clearing
out one or two. I saw to it that the soil
put into the mine crater was of good
quality, and the garden is now so beautiful
and luxuriant that we hardly ever remember
it was once the scene of a great tragedy.
In conclusion, I wish to compliment the
authors of several admirably-written text-
books for sappers and miners, without which
I should never have been able to manage.
